THE ACTS OF THE SESSION
(FIRST SESSION: THIRD PERIOD)
Air Navigation (Charges) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 123 of 1981)An Act to amend the Air Navigation (Charges) Act 1952, and for related purposes.
Apple and Pear Export Charge Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 147 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Apple and Pear Export Ch~rge Act 1976.
Apple and Pear Levy Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 1460f 1981)- An Act to amend the Apple and Pear Levy Act 1976.
Appropriation Act (No.1) 1981-82 (Act No. 1640f 1981)An Act to appropriate certain sums out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for the service of the year ending on 30 June 1982.
Appropriation Act (No.2) 1981-82 (Act No. 165 of 1981)An Act to appropriate a sum out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for certain expenditure in respect of the year endingon 30June 1981.
Australian Apple and Pear Corporation Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 145 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Apple and Pear Corporation Act 1973.
Australian Capital Territory Stamp Duty Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 124 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Stamp Duty Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Cheques) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 126 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Tax (Cheques) Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 125 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 130 of 1981)- An Act to amend section 6 of the Australian Capital Territory Tax (lnsurance Bus;ness) Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Life Insurance Business) Act 1981 (Act No. 128 of 1981)An Act relating to the imposition of tax in respect of certain life insurance business having a connection with the Australian Capital Territory.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Vehicle Registration) Act 1981 (Act No. 129 of 1981)An Act relating to the imposition of tax in respect of the registration of certain vehicles in the Australian Capital Territory.
Australian CapitalTerritory Taxation (Administration) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 127 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Taxation (Administration) Act 1969.
Australian Meat and Live-stock Corporation Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 150 of 1981)- An Act to amend section 50 of the Austrillian Meat and Live-stock Corporation Act 1977.
Australian National University Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 1660f 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Natiollal University Act 1946.
Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Act 1981 (Act No. 168 of 1981)An Act to amend the Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Act 1964, and for related purposes.
Census and Statistics Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 177 of 1981)An Act to amend the Census and Statistics Act 1905, and for related purposes.
Commerce (Trade Descriptions) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 151 of 1981)An Act to amend the Commerce (Trade Descriptions) Act 1905 in relation to the exportation of food.
Commonwealth Grants Commission Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 173 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Commonwealth Grants Commission Act 1973.
Companies and Securities Legislation (Miscellaneous Amendments) Act 1981 (Act No. 153 of 1981)- An Act to amend laws relating to companies and securities and for related purposes.
Continental Shelf (Living Natural Resources) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 182 of 1981)An Act to amend the Continental Shelf(Living Natural Resources) Act 1968 to facilitate the collection of levy imposed by the Foreign Fishing Boats Levy Act 1981 and of amounts payable under certain agreements, and for certain other purposes.
Crimes (Currency) Aat 1981 (Act No. 1220f 1981)An Act to make provision with respect to offences connected with the counterfeiting of money or certain kinds of securities and other activities injurious to Australian currency, and for related purposes.
Customs (Unlawful Exportation of Food) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 152 of 1981)- An Act ~to amend the Cllstoms Act 1901 in relation to the exportation of food.

Wednesday, 19 August 1981
Mr SPEAKER (Rt Hon. Sir Billy Snedden) took the chair at 2.15 p.m., and read prayers.
PETITIONS
The Clerk-Petitions have been lodged for presentation as follows and copies will be referred to the appropriate Ministers:
Diabetics: Insulin
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled, the petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That in view of the recent standardisation of insulin and insulin syringes, having the effect of increasing the cost of both items to the Diabetic in general, and as every insulindependent Diabetic must use the same, daily or twice daily according to medication.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that syringes be made available on the N.H.S. and that insulin prescriptions revert to 10 bottles as previously supplied.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Charles, Mrs Child, Mr Cunningham, Mr Duffy, Mr Hawke, Mr Holding, Mr Innes, Dr Jenkins, Mr Barry Jones, Mr Kent, Mr Mildren, Mr Milton, Dr Theophanous and Mr Willis.
Petitions received.
Trans Australia Airlines
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
Many Australians depend almost exclusively upon Trans Australia Airlines for the provision of essential air services.
The Federal Liberal Party policy objective of selling Trans Australia Airlines within 4 years is contrary to the national interest.
Sale of Trans Australia Airlines would inevitably result in:
(i) the creation of a private monopoly of major airline services in Australia;
(ii) highe~ domestic airfares.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the House of Representatives will reject outright any proposal to sell Trans Australia Airlines.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mrs Child, Mr Dawkins, Mr Free, Mr Milton, Mr Morris and Mr Wallis.
Petitions received.
Health Care Services
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That many Australians will be financially disadvantaged by the health changes proposed by the Federal Government, especially the withdrawal of Commonwealth medical benefits

for the uninsured, and the introduction of a means test for free public hospital care;
That the health insurance changes promote inequity, inefficiency and lack of universal coverage;
That the public hospital system and community health programmes will be financially disadvantaged by the abdication of Commonwealth responsibility and funding.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the House of Representatives will take all steps necessary to reintroduce a system of health care based on ability to pay, and access to health care based on medical need.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Beazley, Mr Free, Mr Howe, Mr Les Johnson and Mr Charles Jones.
Petitions received.
Family Allowances
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament Assembled. The humble Petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
I. Because of an anomaly in tax rates a single income family pays nearly $10 a week more tax than a dual income family earning the same total amount.
2. Family allowances established in 1976 have not been increased since then, yet over the same period living costs have increased by nearly 60 per cen t.
Your petitioners most humbly pray thal:
The Parliament should act-
I. To restore the lost value of family allowances and to index them for future price rises.
2. To increase the spouse rebate from $830 to $1,342 per year.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Donald Cameron, Mr Ewen Cameron, Mr Cross, Mr Falconer and Mr Jult.
Petitions received.
Health Care Services
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in the Parliament assembled. The Petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully requests that the Federal Government ensures that:
(a) It honours its 1975 election promise to maintain Medibank-a free, universal health insurance scheme, and institute methods of improving hospital and health services for the Australian people.
(b) The Community Health Program remains a Federal Government responsibility, with increased funding and expansion of services.
(c) It abandons means-testing and the re-introduction of charges at public hospitals which will severely disadvantage low and middle income groups.
(d) Every effort be made by the Federal Government to meet the health care needs and demands of the Australian community.
Your petitioners most humbly pray that the Honourable Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in the Parliament assembled will give earnest consideration to this most vital matter.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.

Tertiary Education
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in the Parliament Assembled. This Petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth that:
I. the proposal to introduce fees for higher and second degrees will mean that access to post-graduate and recurrent education will be limited to the rich; Australian research and ability to cope with technological change will suffer; this proposal must be seen as a step in the direction of the introduction of fees for all tertiary students.
2. the proposed introduction of a loans scheme for students will mean graduating with a heavy debt or being denied a tertiary education if loans are refused because a student is seen as a 'poor risk' (particularly affecting women and those doing non-vocational courses); only the rich will be able to get a tertiary education without course restrictions and the burden of indebtedness.
3. in regard to the proposal to restrict eligibility for TEAS and the foreshadowing of reviewing TEAS levels only in the light of the availability of loans, it should be noted that any further downgrading of TEAS, which is already inadequate can only further limit access to education.
4. the proposed restriction of Adult Secondary Assistance to those needing qualifications for employment will severely restrict access to a full high school education and necessary bridging education to tertiary education for those who left school early.
5. the proposal not to fund 30 Colleges of Advanced Education if they are not rationalised is an attack on teacher education.
This attack will create teacher shortages in the mid 1980s, restrict access to education now and endanger the future quality of teaching available to Australian children.
Your petitioners therefore pray that:
(i) post-secondary tuition fees not be introduced for any courses or for any category of students;

(ii) that the TEAS scheme be extended so as to more fully

meet the real financial needs of students.

(iii) that a comprehensive loans scheme not be introduced.

(iv) that there be no restriction of Adult Secondary

Assistance.

<

(v) that the proposal not to fund 30 CAEs unless they are

rationalized be rescinded.

And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.

by Mrs Child, Mr Dawkins, Mr Innes and Mr Milton.

2. That Moreton Island is included on the Register of the Australian Heritage Commission, and the Federal Government is urged and indeed it must, under the Australian Heritage Commission Act 1975 take into consideration the protection of the island.
3. That the Queensland Government has given approval to the sandmining of Moreton Island despite the advice of conservationists and despite the opposition of the majority of electors in the Moreton Region.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the House of Representatives will request the Government to refuse export permits covering mineral sands mined on Moreton Island.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.

19 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 383

A substantial contribution to Australia's economy by the payment of taxation and to the quality of .life of fellow Australians, is made by parents in the work-force who have preschool children.

Toxic Shock Syndrome
To the Honourable the Speaker and members of the House of Representatives assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned showeth that your petitioners are gravely concerned by the threats to women's health and lives posed by Toxic Shock Syndrome, and urge the following steps to be taken as a matter of urgency:

384 REPRESENTATIVES 19 August 1981

Petitions

(I) Tampons, sanitary napkins, vaginal deodorants and similar sanitary products to be re-c1assified under Therapeutic Agents (not under "Cosmetics" as at present), in order to bring them under the scrutiny of the Australian Drug Evaluation Committee. We believe manufacture must be under the strictest hygienic conditions.
(2) After re-classification, we urge an immediate government-funded inquiry into all aspects of manufacture and content of these products. This must lead to: (i) contents of product and instructions for correct usage to be shown on packaging; (ii) removal of synthetic fibres, chemicals or ingredients that are potentially or actually harmful to users.
(3) Allocation of extra funding for ongoing research by the National Health and Medical Research Council into these products, results of this research to be made public to ensure all health workers and potential users are fully informed. This campaign of public education would necessitate multilingual distribution of the information where appropriate.
Your petitioners, therefore, humbly request that Parliament take immediate and positive action to avert possible threats posed to women's health by implementation of the above measures. And your petitioners as in duty bound will epray, etc.

Privacy and Personal Information
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth that:
Paragraphs 107 and 108 of the Privacy and Personal In Formation discussion paper number 14, brought down by the Law ReForm Commission in June 1980, undermine the Family unit and the authority of parents, which is the Christian basis of our society, by proposing that 'between 12 and 16 years, a child should be entitled to object to disclosure of information toa parent.'
It is your petitioners sincere belief that:

386 REPRESENTATIVES 19 August 1981

Petitions

(a) an adolescent between the ages of 12 and 16 is not at a mature enough stage of life to decide whether or not the disclosure of information to a parent will be in the best interests of the adolescent.
(b) as a record keeper has no responsibility for the child, and as his moral standards may be different to those of a parent, he should not be permitted to come between child and parent.
(c) a parent has a unique position which may enable him/her to perceive a situation that the record keeper does not. The parent must therefore be aware of the problem if remedial action is to be taken, and has the right to guide the child in the direction he/she feels fit.
Your petitioners therefore pray:
That the Members of the House of Representatives will reject the recommendation of the Law Reform Commission and find some other means to protect children from the excessive abuse of some parents.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by MrDobie.

International Relations
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the Ho~se of Representatives in Parliament assembled. It is the petition of the undersigned electors of Australia that:
Australia increase diplomatic contacts with countries with whom we have differences, not reduce negotiations.
Australia's leaders declare in advance that they will strenuously work for, and be bound by decisions of, a respected mediator agreed on by both sides in any dispute with another nation.
At least as much time, thought, and money be available to find such mediators as Australia now spends to plan military defence and forge military alliances, and to appoint conciliators and arbitrators whenever our international negotiations falter or break down.
Experts of departments, faculties and institutes for pea,ce studies have equal facilities and status with military experts in international negotiations of our country, especially for disarmanent talks or military pacts, and such studies have more government support.
The United Nations organisation develop into a body or bodies more democratically chosen by more enlightened

peoples of all nations, with powers given to it by member nations to improve international law between consenting members, to provide adequate extension of the personnel, functions and facilities of the International Court of Justice to satisfy those countries which agree to abide by such courts' decisions on matters in dispute between those countries, with the powers and right to arr~st, try, sentence and enforce penalties upon individuals in those'countries who breach such laws, and the right to raise revenues from licensing and controlling of international activities of types agreed between the nations concerned.
The mass media of communication of our country exchange comparable amounts of coverage of news and views with comparable media in countries.antagonistic to Australia, and try to put in depth all viewpoints before acceding towartime style censorship, distortion or exhortation.
And your petitioners pray that you will act upon our plea and make public your response.
by Dr Everingham. Petition recei~ed.
Excise on Wine
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
1. The wine industry is accepted throughout the nation as occupying an important place in tl)e social and economic activities of Australia.
2. The wine industry of Australia has a need to sustain an appropriate level of production and sales to ensure profitability and to sustain and improve the level of employment with the wine producing industry.
3. The wine industry of Australia has a need to sustain an appropriate level of production and sales to sustain and improve the economic viability of persons, partnerships or companies engaged in the occupation of wine grape production and the leyel of employment and standard of living of persons employed in the grape growing industry.
4. An excise tax of 50 cents per gallon applied to wine in 1970 severely retarded sales of wine and converted a 10.7 per cent increase in sales in the previous year to a 0.3 per cent decrease.
5. Any tax applied to wine would seriously retard the industry at a time when the economic viability of all sections of the industry is essential if the necessary reconstruction of vineyards and expansion of plantings of premium quality grape varieties is to continue and further enhance the quality image for which Australia is becoming increasingly recognised.
6. The grape growing and, brandy producing sections of the wine industry are presently suffering the effects of substantial increases in excise on brandy which effectively reduced sales of that commodity by more than 1,600,000 litres (approximately 180,000 dozen bottles) or 20 per cent.
7. It has been assessed by both the Australian wine industry and representatives of the Bureau of Agricyltural Economics that the application of a 15 per cent sales tax on wine would retard sales of wine by 20 per cent, or the equivalent of approximately 50 million Iitres of wine annually which in turn represents approximately 80,000 tonnes of grapes of a market value of approximately $13, I00,000.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that Honourable Members of the House of Representatives:
1. Recognise the importance and the place the Australian wine industry has in the social and economic life of the

Petitions

19 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 387

communities which constitute the wine producing industry in Australia and in the economic life of those persons employed in goods and service industries associated with the wine industry. 2. Recognise the importance of the wine industry as an attraction for tourists and the ec.onomic advantages gained from this source of income at district, State and Commonwealth level. 3. Accept that the economic viability of the wine and grape growing industries is essential to the maintenance of employment opportunities in the period of reconstruction so necessary to the further development of the industry.
And further, that Honourable Members of the House of Representatives resist and reject any move to apply a sales tax or an excise on wine.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.

Plant Variety Rights
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. We, the undersigned citizens of the Commonwealth, do humbly pray that the Commonwealth Government:
I. Note that legislation establishing plant breeders' rights in other countries has had serious adverse effects, namely:
(a) Virtual monopoly control of seed production has passed into the hands of a few large international corporations seeking to profit from the exclusive rights over plant genetic materials created by such legislation.
(b) The varieties of seeds available have been restricted mainly to hybrids which will not reproduce truly and will not grow without the aid of artificial fertilisers and pesticides, thus maximising corporate profits without regard for the interests of growers and consumers.
(c) The genetic diversity of crops has been eroded, rendering them vulnerable to disease and other environmental threats.
2. Recognise that maintenance of the genetic diversity of plant varieties is crucial to the continued well-being of the Australian nation, and take all necessary steps to preserve and promote such genetic diversity as a public resource and to prevent exclusive control over plant genetic materials from falling into private hands.
3. Defend the vital interests of Australian farmers and gardeners, independent Australian seed companies and their employees, and consumers of Australian farm and garden produce, by rejecting any proposal to legislate for the establishment of plant breeders' rights in Australia.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Les Johnson.

Petition received.
Excise on Beer
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled: The humble Petition of undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
I. The CLU B industry in Australia is long-established and a valuable part of the community of this nation, providing places where the people may partake of three basic needs of life: a place to eat, a place to drink, a place to sleep.
2. The CLUB industry is accepted thoughout the nation as occupying an important place in the social and economic activities of Australia.
3. The CLUB industry is entirely within the private sector and makes a contribution to the government in taxes of various kinds.

4. The CLUB industry has a need to sustain an appropriate level of turnover and growth, to make profit, and to sustain and improve the level of employment.
5. The CLUB industry of Australia is gravely concerned at published reports that the Government intends to increase excise charges on beer in the forthcoming Budget.
6. Sales of beer constitute the major source of income of most of the industry.
7. The very steep rises in beer excise in 1975 and 1978 severely retarded the growth of the Australian CLUB industry.
8. Any further increases in excise on beer in the 1981 Budget would further retard the industry at a time when growth is essential to maintaining economic viability and the ability to serve the constantly growing needs of the community.
9. Such is its significance as a community requirement, beer occupies a place on the Consumer Price Index, and any alteration to its cost to the community would have an inflationary effect.
10. Any increase in excise would result in and force the CLUB industry to pass on such increases to the consumer by way of higher prices for beer.
II. Higher excise would be only the starting point for an upward spiral in beer prices because of the presence of other factors, such as State Licence Fee taxes, which are directly linked to excise rates.
12. The CLUB industry of Australia already is gravely
a handicapped by heavy excise and other tax imposts, its growth
is at standstill; and, inevitably must decline if beer excise is increased.
Your Petitioners therefore humbly pray that Honourable Members of the House of Representatives:
(I) Recognise the importance and place of the Australian CLUB industry in the social and economic life of the nation.
(2) Accept that the viability of the CLUB industry is essential to the maintenance of employment opportunities and stability of the industry is essential to enable it to continue to provide for the CLUB needs of the nation in this period of growing population and increasing tourism.
And further, that Honourable Members of the House of Representatives resist and reject any move to increase excise on beer.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
byMrKerin.

Staging of American B52 Bombers
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The petition of the undersigned in South Australia respectfully showeth:
That the proposed use of Darwin airport as a refuelling and rest and recreation base for American B52 bombers:
Jeopardises the welfare of the citizens of Darwin and of all Australians, in that it establishes a stronger potential of Darwin being a nuclear target in any conflict between the USA and another power.

Impedes the sovereignty of the Australian nation in that Australian citizens would have neither control nor knowledge of whether B52's:
(a) carried nuclear weapons (b) were engaged in bombing missions against another
State. Violates the Australian people's independence and links this nation irreconcilably with United States foreign policies thereby destroying the potential for independent Australian defence policy. Violates democratic procedures in committing the Australian nation to participation in future wars without due consultation and informed assessment by Australian citizens and further increases the militarisation of the Indian Ocean, thereby increasing the likelihood of super power conflict in this region. Jeopardises the welfare of the Citizens of Darwin, in that noise pollution and the threat of accidental radioactive fallout will increase substantially to the residents. Negates the potential of the Australian nation to establish itself as a nation of peace in the Pacific region and violates Australia's committment to the Nuclear NonProliferation Treaty.
Your Petitioners therefore pray that the Members of the House of Representatives will ensure that the Prime Minister withdraw the offer of Darwin, or any other location in Australia, as a base or depot for American B52 bombers. And that furthermore will ensure that any request of such usage by the US Government be rejected.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Dr Blewett.

Petition received.
Plant Variety Rights
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. I, Dr Paul Robert Neuman, Leader and Lord of Knights of the Order of the White Cross International, a humanitarian knighthood for the defence and protection of life, jointly with officers and members of the Order and with citizens of the Commonwealth of Australia by this our humble petition respectfully showeth:
That I, jointly with and on behalf of the undersigned petitioners voice our concern and objection about the proposed legislation of the so-called Plant Variety Rights Bill 1981 and that [ claim, according to the nature and principles ofmy organisation, to have a mandate to defend the silent Millions of Life-forms which are unable to defend themselves and are not

represented other'l"ise to protect their fate and future and also the majority of pople, who are not or very little informed about the consequences of this Bill and whose freedom and well-being is at stake, should the Bill become Law in Australia.
Since time immemorial, life has dominated Earth Hnd has formed in the course of evolution a Web of Life, composed of animals and plants which are depending on each other and are adjusted to each other within their Eco-system. Man, as part of nature and being bound to the laws of nature, must suffer the consequences if this nature is brought out of balance. The new life-forms, this Bill will allow to enter Australia, are tailor-made creations of genetic-engineers, aliens and monsters to our eco-system, but fit to open an entirely new marketing opportunity for food and chemicals. Scientific achievement or not, the Gene-manipulators playa wicked Leggo-game for international finance and the chemical industry. I hold, that the natural species in Australia or elsewhere in the world have not even the slightest chance to compete with the invaders from man's laboratories, since the new creations are invested with the power of money, using man's abilities, man's Laws and man's chemicals to clear the way for genemanipulated commercial Mono-cultures, designed and controlled by those who hold the Patent Rights over these plants.
It requires no imagination to realize that all power for dominance over people is contained by those who control the food. Indeed, control over all beings bound to eal. Over farmers and horticulturists as to what to grow, how to grow and with which kind of chemical to treat. Il also means control over licenced breeders of the new varieties, who may be directed to refuse supply to certain growers as the patent owners feel fit. Beneficiary of the entire exercise is the chemical industry which is eagerly buying up already such patents, thus the toxic pollution level in the environment must rapidly rise as a result of such policy. [n the final analysis, [ am convinced, that people in fear of famine due to possible repercussions, will to a great extent surrender their freedom and dignity to the will of those, who are in control of food. Il has been voiced in various circles of Science and Philosophy that the potential of genetic engineering and cloning can be more devastating than the Nuclear-Bomb and [ believe, a unification of gene-manipulation, cloning, computer-technology and international finance together can form a power able to result in a sort of Domesday for the world as we know it. It may be the beginning of a "Brave New World" similar to that, described in Huxley's novel, but whether mankind can survive it's own creations or vanish together with nature, remains a question mark.
When the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives deal with this Bill, they may also consider the religious aspects of the matter. Australians, being so close to nature, have naturally a strong attachment to the Creator. It is not unlikely that spiritual divisions may be enhanced amongst people over this ethical atrocity, indeed, the scientific take-over of creation is in a sense a dethronement of God as a control over all beings. I am awaiting in anticipation the protest of the World-Leaders of Religion to come to the defence of the Divine Creation, since they do so very often on the subjects of procreation and abortion and I pray, the very same Leaders will not 'side with the powerful' as they usually do. After all the foundation of Judeo-Christian Faith is at stake.
I am sure, the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House do share the pride of all Australians over the unique beauty of this continent and the natural composition of it's diverse life forms. Surely no-one of sound mind would wish to see this marvel of the Pacific Region being harmed and finely transformed into gene-manipulated mono-cultures, and yet this very Plant Variety Rights Bill 1981 is the door-opener for such a future. We believe, the driving forces behind this Bill did get away with far too much already in Australia. I refer here to the chemical pollution and the concerted efforts of

392 REPRESENTATIVES 19 August 1981

Petitions

that industry to increase the sale of toxic chemicals to the agricultural community. Many housewives nowadays fear the method of high pressure salesmen: "Foot in the door and no retreat." Australia is well advised to keep her doors tightly Closed to the salesmen of new life-forms.
We cannot undo the scientific 'success' of cloning and geneengineering, but the people of Australia can refuse to legalize their own dependence upon the mercy of Multi-National Corporations. Such corporations generally do not break the Law openly, but rather lobby for the introduction of new laws, tailored to fit their future intentions. The present Bill, we believe, is a typical example of this kind and will give full cover of the Law to any foreign body or person as outlined in Division 2 Section 12 of the Bill and Division I Section 10 states unmistakably that virtually every plant of commercial interest falls under the Bill.
The promotes of gene-engineering may praise Interferon as a Wonderdrug, capable of eliminating cancer. We hold that from the very same corner of the industry once DDT was praised to be the Wonderchemical, capable of eliminating the insect-pest, but yet the insects have not suffered real losses in the long run, while the environment of the planet is cursed with a dreadful chemical which causes constant harm to nature now and in times to come and we believe, since the increase of cancer incidents appear in correlation with the increase of chemicals, it may be wise to reduce these chemicals rather than adding more to it, including substances derived from gene-manipulated matter.
I conclude,jointly with the undersigned petitioners that:
(I) The Plant Variety Rights Bill 1981 bears destructive consequences for the environment of Australia.
(2) Due to protective chemicals, required for this kind of plants, the toxic level in the environment must increase.
(3) The independence of people and the free trade is endangered.
(4) The Peace of Faith is likely to be disrupted. (5) The beneficiaries of the Bill will primarily be Foreign
Corporation.
Should this Bill become law.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that:
The Representatives of the Commonwealth of Australia reject the Plant Variety Rights Bill 1981 entirely and in all parts.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr MacKellar.

QUESTIONS WITHOUT NOTICE
TAXATION
Mr HAYDEN-I ask a question of the Prime Minister. Has the Government decided on a -huge increase in sales tax of more than $800m-an increase in fact of 39 per cent? Has the Government also decided to increase personal income tax collections by 22 per cent, which is the equivalent of $11 a week extra per taxpayer? Does .the Prime Minister recall that the Government pledged in this House on 12 March that greater indirect taxation 'should be accompanied by "- noticeable reduction in personal income tax '? Would he regard this as evidence of another solemn undertaking to the electorate being broken by the Fraser Government?
Mr HOWARD-I am very conscious of what the Government said to the House on 12 March of this year when it explained in a statement the reasons why it was not going to move to a broadly

Questions Without Notice

19 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 393

based indirect tax. The decisions that were announced last night represent no more than a modest broadening of the taxation base in a very limited area. Any suggestion that one can liken what was announced last night to the type of change that was in contemplation earlier this year, which included amongst other things the introduction of a retail turnover tax and also, as an alternative, the introduction of a broadly-based indirect tax including not only the major expansion of the wholesale tax base but also the introduction of a tax on services, is without foundation. I think the true way to measure the increase in indirect taxes in this Budget is to compare the rate of increase in indirect tax in the prospective financial year 1981-82 with what occurred in 1980-81. If the Leader of the Opposition does that, he will find that the differences in increase between the two years are minor indeed.
FRENCH PACIFIC TERRITORIES
Mr FALCONER-My question is directed to the Minister for Foreign Affairs and relates to his attendance last week at the South Pacific Forum meeting in Vanuatu together with the attendance of the Deputy Prime Minister on behalf of Australia. Can the Foreign Minister explain to the House the nature of the course of action which the Forum decided on to assist in bringing about the independence in particular of French territories in the Pacific area? Can he give the House an indication of the nature of the support that Australia will be giving to such efforts, particularly bearing in mind the very positive statements which were made by the Deputy Prime Minister in Vila?
Mr STREET-I thank the honourable member for his question. As the House will know, the Australian Government's policy is that all people should be able, by free act of self determination, to decide for themselves the form of government they want. That policy applies to the French territories in the Pacific to which the honourable gentleman referred. The issue was raised at the recent meeting of the South Pacific Forum and discussed at length. Interestingly, after this discussion the Forum decided by consensus, without any opposition, that a delegation led by the Prime Minister of Fiji should go to France and convey to President Mitterrand the concern of the Forum members that progress should be made towards the independence of the French territories in the Pacific. I think this is a sound, common-sense approach to what is a very difficult and sensitive issue, because it has to be remembered-that in the case of New Caledonia, anyway, the Melanesian population is not in the

majority. Therefore, the handling of the independence issue has to be approached with great care. The timing of the visit of the delegation to President Mitterrand has been left at the discretion of its leader, the Prime Minister of Fiji. The Australian Government, through my colleague the Deputy Prime Minister, gave strong support to this initiative. I hope that it will lead to movement on the part of the new French Government towards what we all regard as a desirable objective-that is, independence for the French territories in the Pacific.
SINAI PEACEKEEPING FORCE
Mr COHEN-I direct my question to the Minister for Foreign Affairs. Is it a fact that the Camp David Accords have brought the first real peace between the two major military powers in the Middle East after a period of 30 years and four wars? To enable a step by step process involved in solving the problems of the Sinai, the West Bank, Gaza and the Palestinian people to proceed, did the Camp David Accords and the IsraeliEgyptian peace treaty propose a United Nations peacekeeping force without super-power involvement being stationed in the Sinai Peninsula to facilitate a final Israeli withdrawal from that region? Has that proposal the support of both the Australian Government and the Opposition? What barriers exist to the raising of a United Nations peacekeeping force? What action is this Government taking to remove those barriers?
Mr STREET-It is true that the Camp David Accords and the Egyptian-Israeli peace treaty have had a significant effect in reducing tensions in the Middle East. I understand that those Accords and that peace treaty had the unanimous support of all honourable members of this House, as the honourable gentleman suggested. It is a fact that peace now exists between Egypt and Israel, the two major antagonists in past Middle East conflicts, not that that has resolved all Middle East problems. The failure to solve the Palestinian issue and the present difficulties in Lebanon illustrate that only too clearly. It is true that, in an annex to the peace treaty, reference was made to the composition of a United Nations force, excluding the involvement of any permanent member of the Security Council. It was against the possibility that the United Nations may not be able to fulfil its role that the United States of America undertook, at the time of the signing of the peace treaty, to ensure the establishment and maintenance of an acceptable multinational force. Of course, it is this force that the Egyptian, Israeli and United States governments are presently endeavouring to form.

394 REPRESENTATIVES 19 August 1981

Questions Without Notice

In my statement to the Parliament in May I expressed regret that for various reasons, including the prospect of a veto by the Union of Soviet Socialist Republics, the President of the Security Council was unable to agree to United Nations involvement in the implementation of the peacekeeping arrangements in the Sinai. Accordingly, for the immediate future anyway, United Nations involvement is not an option.
Honourable members should be aware that, at a meeting with the Secretary-General of the United Nations in New York in July, I raised again the question of a United Nations force to be responsible for peacekeeping arrangements in the Sinai. Dr Waldheim confirmed the earlier advice that I had had from the President of the Security Council and he stated categorically that United Nations involvement was not an option unless and until the Soviet Union indicated that it would not use its veto against United Nations participation. In reply, I expressed to the Secretary-General the hope that the option of United Nations involvement at some future date could be kept open. I understand that the parties to the multinational force agreement also want to keep that option open for the future.
BRISBANE AIRPORT: AIR TRAFFIC CONTROLLERS
Mr JULL-I ask the Minister for Transport whether reports are correct that Brisbane Airport is to be closed for three hours a day, beginning in September, because of a shortage of air traffic controllers. Will this also mean that air space over much of Queensland will be uncontrolled during this time? If so, what moves are being made to increase the numbers of air traffic controllers in Queensland and elsewhere to bring their numbers up to strength? Will the Department of Transport resort to importing air traffic controllers from overseas if numbers cannot be found in Australia?
Mr HUNT-The reports that the Brisbane Airport will be closed for short periods as from 10 September are correct. The actual periods of closure are yet to be determined. We are discussing with the airlines the period that is least likely to cause disruption to ~he travelling public. The partial closure of the Brisbane Airport is due to a very severe shortage of air traffic controllers in Australia. We are short of 110 air traffic controllers. We are training air traffic controllers to our maximum capacity. We have in hand plans to transfer some air traffic controllers to Brisbane in the near future, but that will not have any real effect on the situation until some time next year. We are also recruiting air traffic controllers overseas. We have had some success in our recruiting

campaign in the United Kingdom and also in Hong Kong. We are, of course, looking to other areas to see whether it is possible to recruit a few more.
Quite clearly, there is a shortage of air traffic controllers and there has been a shortage for some time. There is a very high wastage rate among them because of the intensity of the work that they perform. It is not easy to recruit people who have the qualifications or the stamina necessary to carry out the task. We are most concerned about the problem and we are taking every measure possible to ensure minimum disruption to services to Queensland. We are taking every measure possible to recruit and to train additional air traffic controllers to overcome this immediate problem. I might add that I have also asked my Department to make sure in whatever arrangements it makes that there will be no closure of the facilities at Brisbane over the Christmas holiday period.
FAI HEALTHCARE INSURANCE
Mr ROBERT BROWN-I refer the Minister for Health to an advertisement which has appeared over recent days concerning FAI Healthcare Insurance and featuring the Honourable Harry Jago who is identified as a former State Minister for Health. The advertisement says in part:
And remember, you may be entitled to additional tax deductions at 32 cents in the $1 on your total medical and hospital costs.
I ask the Minister whether that statement concerning possible tax benefits is correct. I also ask whether the statement attributed to Mr Harry Jago that 'FAI Healthcare Insurance gives you the best possible health cover available' is correct. Does FAI Healthcare Insurance come within the ambit of the National Health Act? If it does not, will the success of this insurance scheme in creaming off the low health-risk people and taking them away from the insurance schemes offered by the registered health funds increase the health costs of those people who are insured with registered health funds?
i\1r MacKELLAR-Firstly, my attention was drawn to the advertisement in a very broad sense. I have not read the fine detail contained in it. I have not seen the words quoted. The rebate of 32c in the dollar for basic health insurance is available only to those people who take out health insurance with a registered health insurance organisation. The FAI is not a registered health insurance organisation. So people taking out insurance with companies such as F AI will not be eligible for the tax rebate, as suggested in the honourable gentleman's question.

Questions Without Notice

19 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 395

I have no comment to make about Mr Jago's participation. Nor do I have any comment to make about his alleged statement that FAI 'offers the best cover. I do not agree with that, but it demonstrates that the Government has in place a health scheme which offers to individuals within the community a range of choices which enables them to pick the form of health insurance which they believe is most suitable for their circumstances.
As to the last part of the honourable gentleman's question, I think that people who take out insurance with commercial organisations should look very carefully at the fine print in relation to the cover offered. All in all, I am not aware of any evidence that commercial insurers are taking a high proportion of individuals away from the registered health insurance organisations. Therefore, the community rating principle is very largely in place, as the Government intends. Finally, I advocate that all members of the general public look very carefully at the alternatives that are offered in health insurance. I advise them to think very carefully before they decide not to insure with a registered health insurance organisation.

SYDNEY (KINGSFORD-SMITH) AIRPORT
Mr MacKENZIE-I direct my question to the Minister for Tnmsport. What progress has been made towards the expansion of runway facilities at Mascot Airport after some four years or perhaps five years of inquiry? Has the New South Wales Premier yet made any decision on this matter? Will restrictions on commuter aircraft and perhaps on intrastate aircraft services have to be

brought in soon, unless a decision is made? Is there any truth in the suggestion I have heard only recently that Brisbane may soon take over from Sydney as Australia's international gateway?
Mr HUNT-Yes, we have received a reply from the Premier of New South Wales. Mr Wran has replied to a letter from the Prime Minister, making it quite clear that the New South Wales Government is not prepared to co-operate with the Commonwealth in the development of a close-spaced parallel runway at Sydney (Kingsford-Smith) Airport. In fact what he has done is to turn his back on the people of Sydney and the people of New South Wales. As far as the major airport needs of Sydney--
Mr Charles Jones-So did Willis and so did Lewis.
Mr HUNT-Well, you said that. Does that makehis--
Mr SPEAKER-The Minister will resume his seat. The honourable member for Newcastle will cease interjecting. The Minister will cease taking notice of interjections and will continue with his answer.
Mr HUNT-The truth of the matter is, of course, that Mr Willis agreed to it. Mr Wran agreed five years ago to co-operate with the Commonwealth in setting up a major airport needs study committee involving both Commonwealth and State officials. But in December 1979 the New South Wales Government decided to withdraw its officials, leaving the Commonwealth offichils to make a report. The Commonwealth has agreed to the recommendation for a closespaced parallel runway to meet the immediate needs of those people wishing to use Sydney (Kingsford-Smith) Airport. PRIVATE CAPITAL INFLOW
Mr WILLIS-I address my question to the Treasurer. Is it the case that the Budget strategy presupposes a level of private capital inflow this year of $7 billion or more, which is an even greater amount than the record and extraordinary $6.5 billion inflow of last year? In view of the fact that the surplus of this private capital inflow over the deficit on the balance of payments current account adds to the money supply and this year, as last year, is expected to account for some 45 per cent of money supply growth, did the Government, in drawing up its Budget strategy, give any consideration to reducing the rate of growth of the money supply by controlling private capital inflow rather than increasing taxes on the Australian people? If that course was considered, why was it not adopted?
Mr HOWARD-It is true that the Budget is based upon an assumption that the rate of private capital inflow will be between $6,000m and $7,000m and will make a contribution to private sector foreign exchange transactions of about the same level as occurred last year. The honourable gentleman's question, like other questions from the Opposition on the subject of private capital inflow, assumes that one can turn the tap of foreign investment in this country on and off without damage to the long term level and pattern of that investment. It is necessary, if the development of this country is to proceed, for us to have foreign capital. There are simply inadequate reserves of domestic savings to support the sort of investment that is going on in Australia at present. When the honourable member for Gellibrand and the Leader of the Opposition talk about controls and blockages on private capital inflow, they are advocating a slower rate of growth, because there is an insufficient volume of domestic savings in this country to support the current levels of investment. If the Opposition argues for less investment in Australia at present, it is arguing for lower living standards, higher unemployment and less growth.

EXCHANGE RATE
Mr HAYDEN-I ask the Treasurer a question supplementary to the question which has just been asked of him by the honourable member for Gellibrand. The Treasurer will recall in his answer confirming that the Government is anticipating an even larger foreign capital inflow this year than last year; it will be in excess of $6,000m and possibly as high as $7,000m. Does he recognise that the implication is that the Government must also anticipate an appreciation in the Australian dollar. during this fiscal year at least as large as the 10 per cent general appreciation that took place last year and in particular cases rising much higher, as high as 38 per cent against West Germany? Does he recognise that the effect of appreciations, especially such significant appreciations, is to disadvantage the traditional farm and manufacturing export industries and manufacturing import replacement industries? Finally, does he recognise that an appreciation of this level, on top of the appreciations which have already taken place in the preceding 12 months, would effectively wipe out protection for the majority of Australian manufacturing industry?

398 REPRESENTATIVES 19 August 1981

Questions Without Notice

Mr HOWARD-I will pick up the last part of the honourable gentleman's question and correct him immediately. The highest levels of protection for a large part of Australian manufacturing industry are in respect of those industries that enjoy quotas. Exchange rate appreciation, far from reducing protection for those industries, in fact increases it to a massive degree. The really high levels of protection in Australia are in respect of the industries that are subject to quotas. I refer, of course, to the textile, clothing and footwear industries, the motor vehicle industry and a number of other industries. Any suggestion in those circumstances that exchange rate appreciation lessens the protection to those industries is diametrically opposite to what precisely happens. It is true that the private capital inflow is likely to be of about the same order as last year; but, of course, the current account deficit is projected to be somewhat larger and, as a consequence, the net monetary effect of that private capital inflow is likely to be about the same. I will not make forward speculations about how the Australian dollar will move. Its value compared with other currencies, including the American dollar and the Deutsche mark, is affected not only by what happens to our currencies and to the Australian economy but also obviously by what happens in domestic conditions in both of those economies. I can only repeat what I have said to the honourable gentleman on many occasions: Anti-inflationary objectives will remain dominant in the determination of our exchange rate policies.

COMPUTER EQUIPMENT
Mr DONALD CAMERON-I rephrase in everyman's language the question directed by the honourable. member for Lalor to the Prime Minister. Is he aware that, in the 12 months ending 30 June 1981, the value of computer equipment imported by Australia rocketed to $587m? Is he aware that for 11 months in 1980-81, compared with a similar II months in 1979-80, the value of computer equipment imported by Telecom Australia rose from $380m to $502m? Do figures

such as these have an effect on our balance of payments situation? Does the Prime Minister regard the warnings that there will be greater import reliance in this fieldas such as to motivate the Government to pay special attention to increasing Australia's ability to produce computer equipment?
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-One of the reasons for the growth in Telecom orders would no doubt be the growth in demand for telecommunications services. The capital program for Telecom has been very large over recent years. It will be very large. Even so, in some areas there are still delays in providing services. TRANSPORT WORKERS UNION: INDUSTRIAL DISPUTE
Mr GOODLUCK-Is the Prime Minister aware of an article which appeared in the Hobart Mercury dated I August 1981 and which in essence refers to the role that the Premier of Tasmania played in the recent Transport Workers Union of Australia strike? My question is a direct one: What role did the Premier play in solving the TWUstrike?
Mr Scholes-Mr Speaker, I raise a point of order. It is not within the ambit of a Minister of this Government to determine what role the Premier of a State plays,nor is it within the responsibilities of the Prime Minister's portfolio. The question asks for an expression of opinion, which is contrary to the Standing Orders.

Mr SPEAKER-The point of order is not sustained. The Prime Minister was engaged in discussions with people about settling a dispute. As I understand the question, it involved the Premier of Tasmania and discussions with the Prime Minister. Therefore the question is in order.
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-Let me relate to the House some of the events that occurred over that weekend and during that period. There had been discussions before the weekend involving the Deputy Prime Minister, myself, the Minister for Industrial Relations and, I think, one or two others and the Australian Council of Trade Unions because of a serious situation which was developing in .relation to not only the Transport Workers Union but also certain matters which looked .as though they could involve a total national stoppage on the Australian waterfront. For that reason-I have paid due regard to thisthe President of the Australian Council of Trade Unions approached me and said: 'Will the Government participate in talks in an effort to see what can be done to resolve some of these difficulties?' As a result of that, a statement was issued by the Government and the ACTU. Sir John Moore also was present at those discussions on the Friday, I think it was. It was basically a sensible statement but one which also recognised that the rigidity of the indexation guidelines was making some trade unions feel that they were not able to have their cases heard and argued before the Conciliation and Arbitration Commission. A form of words which had the support of the ACTU and the Government and which might have relieved some of that pressure was placed in that statement.
In the event, there was an attempt based on that document to get the Transport Workers Union to go back to arbitration. The ACTU was significantly involved in that. Other Premiers were also involved. States of emergency, we need to remember, had been declared in more than one State, and Premiers were in touch with me concerning the severity of the dispute-whether it was likely to become a full national stoppage or what might be done to relieve the matter. The Minister for Communications also was involved because he had been having discussions oVt!r the same weekend with the Minister for Industrial Relations on disputes in relation to Telecom Australia. Therefore he was involved with me and the Minister for Industrial Relations in the discussions that took place over that weekend and on the Monday. Part of the problem was to get the Transport Workers Union to accept arbitration. There was a belief that it ought to. There was a

400 REPRESENTATIVES 19 August 1981

Questions Without Notice

prospect at the time that its problem might be resolved through an anomalies conference, even though I think Deputy President Isaac had indicated that the Commission could not make a judgment of the kind that the union had wanted.
In the process of persuading the Transport Workers Union to accept arbitration, Mr Wran was in Melbourne because of a certain Australian Labor Party meeting of which some honourable gentlemen here would be aware. I think he undertook through the New South Wales Trades and Labour Council to see what influence could be exerted on the TWU. Either the Minister for Communications or the Minister for Industrial Relations rang around other States again to try to establish the circumstance where some influence could be brought to bear on the Transport Workers Union to accept arbitration. In the process of that, through my colleague the Minister for Administrative Services, an approach was initially made-to Mr Lowe. After that I discussed the matter with Mr Lowe. He indicated a wish to help because Tasmania was being--
Mr Young-Is this a sunset answer?,
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-I think it probably will be. I thank the honourable gentleman for reminding me of the time. Tasmania had been very severely hurt by the Transport Workers Union dispute, in many cases more severely hurt than other States. I have to say that, up to the point at which my colleague the Minister for Administrative Services had approached the Premier, it appeared that the State was'not doing a great deal to assist itself. I should also say that in my conversation I pointed out to Mr Lowe that other Premiers were doing a good deal to protect the interests of their States. He said in that conversation that obviously he wanted to do what he could to protect Tasmania. The action seemed to be in Melbourne and I offered to makp. transport available to him to come to Melbourne, again with the objective of getting the Transport Workers Union before arbitration. I think that is a quiet and accurate description of events.
I would like to make one other point only, and that is that nobody who was involved in these matters and nobody in any of the discussions that the Government has had with the Australian Council of Trade Unions over recent months has sought to make any political capital out of the attempts that people from different spheres have been making to solve difficult problems and problems which concern many Australians. I think senior officers of the ACTU, and the Government certainly, recognise that the level of industrial disputation is not really in anyone's interests. It is

certainly in the interests of all 'Australia to overcome that effort as much as humanly possible and to establish a better industrial environment. I think it is a great pity that that particular article ever appeared in the Hobart Mercury, because it was not in accord with the spirit of what had been taking place over the weekend. Since the Opposition does not want any further questions I ask that further questions be placed on the Notice Paper.

HOUSING FINANCE
Dis,cussion of Matter of Public Importance
Mr SPEAKER-I have received a letter from the honourable member for Reid (Mr Uren) proposing that a definite matter of public importance be submitted to the House for discussion, namely:
The Government's imposition of increasing hardship on first home buyers, existing home buyers and people renting homes, resulting From rising interest rates, declining availability of housing finance and cutbacks in public housing expenditure.
I call upon those members who approve of the proposed discussion to rise in their places.
More than the number a/members required by the Standing Orders having risen in their places-
Mr UREN (Reid) (3.17)-Next to unemployment, housing is the most serious social problem in this country. This Government has deliberately sacrificed both private and public housing for foreign control of unplanned and unco-ordinated resource development in this country. I want to deal with some of the social problems arising out of this Government's economic policy. Last weekend the Sydney Morning Herald reported the plight of a young couple living in Sydney's outer western suburb of Penrith. The family was forced to put its home up for sale because it could no longer afford its mortgage repayments. According to a real estate agent in the area, about lOO homes are for sale in the Penrith area, but rising interest rates and restrictions on the availability of housing finance meant that few people could afford to buy those homes. As a result, while the total repayment debt for existing home buyers went up by around $20,000 in the past 18 months for a home loan of $30,000, the selling prices had moved down on houses and land in this area.
Not very long ago a young couple could buy a modest home on the periphery of the Sydney metropolitan area, delay having children, both go to work, save and scrape and forgo other needs in

order to get their foot on the bottom of the home ownership ladder. They would borrow to the limit of their means and trust that they could get themselves to a position where they could improve their living standards with the security of having their own roof over their heads. Alternatively, they would consider selling their first home and use the capital gain to buy another home better suited to their needs. But this young couple in Penrith had that avenue closed to them. They faced the prospect of little, if any, capital gain and possibly a loss. They faced insecurity and the prospect of increasing hardship resulting from a continual erosion of their household disposable income, So they decided to put the home up for sale. They intended to buy a caravan. For them, buying their first home was a move that put them on the path of downward mobility. Yet these people were lucky enough in the first place to get access to home ownership.
There are many who are even worse off. The Minister for Housing and Construction (Mr McVeigh) admits that he has no idea of the number of homeless people in Australia. He can neither confirm not deny the estimates of the welfare groups that there are about 150,000 homeless people throughout this country-young, unemployed, elderly, deserted people-people who have been dispossessed as a result of the callous policies of this Government. The Minister can neither confirm nor deny that there are about 250,000 people across Australia excluding Aboriginal people who suffer an outrageous neglect of their housing needs, who aJ;e living in mobile, temporary or makeshift accommodation. The Minister cannot tell this Parliament how many people spend their nights in the back seats of cars, in parks or in alleys. He cannot tell tis the extent of overcrowding in sub-standard, overpriced rental accommodation. The Minister does not know these details. The Government does not really care and it cannot possibly develop appropriate housing policies when it cannot understand the housing problems. It is prepared to sacrifice the housing sector to make room for corporate investment and foreign speculation.
In July this year, before the last round of interest rate rises, the Sunday Telegraph reported a survey conducted by the social workers in the western suburbs of Sydney. This report linked the stress of loan repayments with child abuse, drugtaking, mental illness and abortion. The survey revealed a trail of broken marriages and shattered dreams for hundreds of married couples. The men were working full time with as much overtime as they could get. In many cases they even had a second job. The women were going out to work,

402 REPRESENTATIVES 19 August 1981

Housing Finance

often at night. The survey found that despite both partners working they were incapable of keeping up with rising costs-whether in the form of higher loan repayments for those paying off their homes or the widening deposit gap for those seeking to buy their first home. The survey also found that this pattern of scraping for funds put pressure on the marriage and on the family, particularly the children. I havequoted from two newspaper reports and I commend the sensitivity of the journalists involved. Why do we ,not have government publications detailing such f~cts so that government can make a decision arising from these findings? The Government seems unwilling seriously to examine the impact of its policies on people.
The rate of interest is only one aspect of the effect of rising housing costs on householddisposable income. But that effect is made more acute in a context of employment insecurity; partial wage indexation, casualisation of work, cutbacks ,in social services, rising petrol prices, increased health care costs, rises in charges for public services and increasing indirect taxes. The combined

impact of these Government polic!e~ hits people, particularly little people, hard. ThIS IS part of the policies of this callous Government.
Since February 1980 the home loan interest rate from savings banks has increased from 9.5 per cent to 12.5 per cent. For the average mortgage of $30,000 this has meant an increase in repayments over the last 18 months of $65 a month. Over the same period, average earnings of wage and salary earners have increased by only $30.50 a month. Real household disposable income has been eroded by this Government's redistributive measures. Around 80 per cent of income recipients in Australia get less than average weekly earnings. House and land prices have been increasing in most capital cities, especially in Sydney and Melbourne, beyond the reach of the average income household. I seek leave to incorporate in Hansard a table setting out the quarterly average of monthly median prices for houses and home units in Sydney and Melbourne for the period June 1965 to December 1980.

Mr UREN-I thank the House. In the December quarter 1975 the median house price in Sydney was $33,500. Average earnings were $167 a week. At that time the value of a home was 200
weeks of average earnings. In the December quarter of 1980 the median price of a house was $78,700 and average earnings were $278. The value of the house was then 283 weeks of average earnings. Since that period the median price of a house has moved to around $84,000 and interest rates have gone up still further. In my view

interest rates on housing have not peaked yet and they will rise still further. Several effects of these changes need to be understood. I seek leave to incorporate in Hansard several (abIes showing the changes in monthly repayments of different loan sizes at varying interest rates and the deposit gaps for home buyers on average weekly earnings for different house prices and home loan interest rates.

Mr UREN-The tables show that more people are not able to obtain finance to get access to home ownership. Almost four out of five income earners in this country are excluded from access to a $30,000 loan from a savings bank at an interest rate of 12t per cent. A $30,000 loan is not enough for most first home buyers faced with rising house prices, whatever State they live in. I stress that it does not matter where they live. A loan of $30,000 pins down a person. Over 80 per cent of people-four out of five-cannot afford to meet a $30,000 loan. A modest home in Penrith, which is at the bottom end of the market in Sydney, costs around $55,000. People on average single incomes have little chance of buying a home at that price with prevailing interest rates. Dual income families have to save for years to raise the necessary deposit gap. They have to cut back on other necessities. They have to forgo having children. They have to rely on the charity of relatives or they have to take out a crippling second mortgage at an interest rate of 15 per cent to 20 per cent.
These options threaten the stability of families. They impose immense long term hardships. The policies of this Government are not helping these people in need. They are forcing more and more people into the already overstretched private rental market. Private rents are likely to increase substantially as a result of the rental accommodation increase as the landlords put the prices up to meet their own interest ,repayment increases. At the same time, the Government has cut back dramatically on public sector housing. In 1974-75, 3.9 per cent of government expenditure was on public housing. This year it is one per cent of government expenditure. Public housing stock represents only 4.4 per cent of all housing stock in Australia. This Government has also made decisions which will further aggravate housing costs

and undermine the building industry. Inflation in the housing market will follow from the imposition of a 2-lper cent sales tax on building materials and furnishings. A 2-lper cent increase in sales tax could add up to $1,000 to the price of establishing a home. This is in addition to the impact of increasing interest rates.
Deeper economic problems are also associated with the housing policies of this Government. We have seen a dramatic rise in the amount of domestic funds taken up by foreign corporations in this country. Australian borrowing by foreign corporations rose from 1.4 per cent of total domestic savings in 1977-78 to 18.8 per cent in 1978-79. We have a huge increase in the raisings of funds by public authorities to provide infrastructure for resource development. We have seen a large and rapid increase in foreign capital inflow to speculate in land and housing, to speculate on interest rates and to speculate against the exchange rate. This Government is squeezing the rest of the domestic economy to make room for the structural shift of investment in resource development and the takeover of Australian business by foreign corporations. The ordinary people of Australia are paying the real price-immediately through reduced social services and increased interest rates and in the longer term by increased dependence, widening inequality and entrenched unemployment. We are moving to a society oflandlords and tenants. The rate of home ownership under this Government has peaked and is now rapidly on the decline.
What we need is a comprehensive housing policy that attends to the needs of people-the immediate needs of people and the emerging needs associated with population growth, structural change and relocation of people. More and more

Housing Finance

19 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 405

control is needed over the rate of speculative capital inflow and over the pace and impact of investment in resource development. Effective programs need to be developed to help a wider range of people secure access to home ownership at affordable interest rates. Included in that should be a program to assist first home buyers and a program which will protect those people already living in homes and paying off their mortgages. We also need a planned and substantial upgrading of the public housing sector. We also need a program which will maintain liquidity at all times in the housing sector. Were a government prepared to give housing a major priority we could achieve those objectives. But this Government has decided to scrap the housing industry for the interests of the resource sector.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Millar)- Order! The honourable member's time has expired.
Mr McVEIGH (Darling Downs-Minister for Housing and Construction) (3.32)-By their actions we shall know them. I thought the shadow Minister for housing and construction, the honourable member for Reid (Mr Uren), was in the wrong ring. I understood that this matter of public importance was about something fundamental to Australian society-home ownership. All the honourable member wanted to talk about was overseas capital coming into this country. Let us start, therefore, from the base, because he is not concerned about home ownership for Australian people; he wants only to mouth meaningless words and platitudes. A leader of the Labor Party once said: 'We do not believe in home ownership. That will make us a nation of capitalists'.
A previous Labor Minister for housingfortunately we have not had too many of themwhen he talked, under insistent and constant questioning by the then Opposition, about housing costs and about the inability of people either to get a job in those disastrous years from 1972 to 1975 or to purchase a house, said: 'We will build them smaller houses'. Does not the honourable member for Reid know that in the closing stages of the Labor Party's far too long reign on the treasury bench it commissioned a report the emphasis of which was: 'We are going to put a tax on home ownership'? That is the policy of the Australian Labor Party. The honourable member for Reid did not want to talk about it because he does not like the truth. I am disappointed. I have a great deal of time for the honourable member for Reid, but today unfortunately, because he is under pressure, he could not defend the situation and he flirted with the truth. I am disappointed and I am concerned that that has happened.

On the other hand, we have had a long commitment to home ownership and to support of the family because we believe the strength of this nation is based on the citadel of home ownership. We have the figures. The honourable member for Reid said that home ownership in Australia had peaked and was now rapidly declining. I do not know where he learned his mathematics, but the simple facts are that the present rate of home ownership in Australia is 71.9 per cent, marginally lower than the 72 per cent revealed by the last census. Yet the honourable member said that the rate was rapidly declining. Does he not know that there is a price to pay for home ownership? Why does he not talk to the migrants in Australia? If he talked to the Italian community and the Greek community he would find that 90 per cent of the Italian community and 84 per cent of the Greek community own their own homes. I utterly reject and throw back in his teeth his statement that home ownership in this country is rapidly declining.
The Australian Government at present, as always under the free enterprise way of life, is pledged to home ownership. I want to talk about some of the things that have happened in recent times to expose this myth, this shadow concern, this sham interest in Australian home ownership. The honourable member cited figures. I want to cite the real figures. At the end of the Labor Party's reign 104,110 Australian families were on the waiting list for welfare housing, which represented a significant increase in three years. The people of Australia had been hung, drawn and quartered with regard to housing. They had realised that they could not have home ownership, that they could not get jobs because of record unemployment in this country during those three years and they had to be placed on the welfare housing list.
At present on account of fundamental policies
of encouraging more people to own their little bit of Australia, the number on the welfare housing list has decreased to 85,391. Home ownership has always involved sacrifice. Mr Deputy Speaker, you know that; it is not easy. People have to have their priorities right. I want to take a little time to enlighten and to encourage so that the shadow Minister can observe. In December 1978 it took 27.2 per cent of average weekly earnings at that time to pay back the interest and redemption on a home loan of $30,000. But under this Government-a government under which real incomes last year increased by 4 per cent, inflation was held down to 8.8 per cent and the working opportunities of Australians in the last two years increased by some 320,000-we find that in

406 REPRESENTATIVES 19 August 1981

December 1980, two years later, it took only 25 per cent of average weekly earnings to repay that loan. The honourable gentleman talked about a $30,000 loan for first home buyers. Does he not know that at least 60 per cent of first home buyers in Australia are dual income earners? Does he not know that 60 per cent of Australian first home buyers seek a loan of considerably less than $30,000? Does he not know those things? I am sorry that I have to take the time of the House to try to explain to him the situation as it exists. He talked about the increase in interest rates. In 18 months I saw this country getting ripped apart, the heart and the muscle torn out of it, because interest rates under the Labor Government increased by 3 per cent in its first 18 months. In almost six years of free enterprise government interest rates have increased by 2 per cent. Yet the honourable member had the temerity and the cheek--
