Mr CADMAN (Mitchell) (5.49)-1 think that everybody in the House appreciates the interest of the honourable member for Corio (Mr Scholes) on matters such as this and the valuable role he has played in looking at the structure of Parliament "and making Parliament more effective. I thank him for moving the motion. I think a number of the honourable members who served on the committee which examined these matters-the Joint Committee on the Parliamentary Committee System-in fact involved themselves more closely with the operation of committees of the Parliament both in the Senate and in the House of Representatives. That was an extremely valuable committee which came forward wit~ a number of recommendations many of which have been adopted over a period.
The proposition being put by the honourable member for Corio is, indeed, an extension, as I understand it, of the work that was done by that committee on committees, as it was called. The

Representatives: Proposed Procedures Committee 19 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 427

report of the committee on committees, in fact, made recommendations on a number of important areas but I think the most significant ones related to the scrutiny of government activity by the Parliament. That scrutiny process, in fact, devoted itself to mainly financial matters, budgetary decisions, the decisions of government lending up to the Budget and the general administration of government. It also devoted itself at some length of the proposition of legislation committees.
Those two recommendations, whilst not completely in the form recommended by the committee on committees, have been adopted. They were pursued by a number of people. I remember the work of the honourable member for Scullin (Dr Jenkins) in this regard. In particular I remember the way in which the honourable member for Moore (Mr Hyde) followed through, in fact, to bring into place some of these ideas. Much of the work was developed privately within the ranks of the Australian Labor Party and also within the ranks of the coalition. We came to a consensus and, in fact, established legislation committees which have not been used in this Parliament as yet. But I hope that they will be used because I found the discussion and the effectiveness of legislation committees, whether they be for complicated legislation or for simple runof-the-mill provisions that can be dealt with quickly--
Dr Jenkins-Very valuable.
Mr CADMAN-Very valuable indeed. In fact, we have been able to allow members to examine and scrutinise legislation with care and proper attention to detail. That has resulted in more effective and better legislaion and in better laws being made for this country. The other element that has been taken up by the House has been the decision that we should establish committees that look at the Estimates in the Budget. The honourable member for Moore was particularly keen to pursue this matter. The House has adopted for the )iast two years the process of Estimates committees. I personally see value in that process. I think those committees will continue or not continue depending on the value that is seen for them by members of this House.
The matter that has been raised by the honourable member for Corio, however, goes beyond that point. The motion that he has moved seeks to establish a committee to maintain a continuing surveillance of the practices and procedures of the House of Representatives with a view to making recommendations which could enhance the role of the House and its operational efficiency. My colleague the honourable member for Wakefield

(Mr Giles), I think, put a compelling case as to why part of those activities should be, in fact, put to the Standing Orders Committee. The establishment of that committee is set out in our Standing Orders. Its role in fact should be to look at the functioning of this House and to enhance the role of the House through amendments to Standing Orders procedures. My view is that these matters should best be dealt with in the way that we have been dealing with them.
Parliamentary reform and the efficiency and the enhancement of the operations of the House of Representatives have occupied the time and the minds of many people, mostly unsuccessfully. One individual seeking to bring about reform or change has a very difficult problem in convincing
his colleagues that his ideas can be successfully adopted. But a number of indlViduals working together have been successful in having the Parliament look at a change to sitting hours. That has occupied the minds of parliamentarians on many occasions. Experiments have been tried; some have failed. In fact, they mostly have failed. But we are about to launch into an experiment for a change of sitting hours. I believe that a committee of the type envisaged by the honourable member for Corio. would suffer the same difficulties that an individual suffers because one would have a group of dedicated enthusiastic individuals who would spend all of their time convincing their colleagues of the wisdom of their recommendations.
There is great reluctance in many ways to change the manner in which we operate here in these chambers. It is difficult to do that. There are vested interests perhaps, with each individual having his own vested interest in the style of life that he has here, in the requirements of the chamber and in the requirements of the Parliament. I suggest that further consideration be given to this proposal so that, in fact, the basic thrust of the matter can be taken up to see whether it be by means of Standing Orders committees, whether it be by agreement between the parties or individuals to pursue certain matters with their colleagues, or whether it would be practical to extend the scope of the Standing Orders Committee of the House of Representatives. I am not sure whether this will meet the needs and the requests of the honourable member for Corio.
I see merit in what the honourable member has proposed. I see obstruction in bringing it to achievement with any real success. I think we all
know that in order to have enthusiastic membership of committees of this Parliament there needs to be some sense of fulfilment. There is not enthusiastic acclamation, I would think, for people to get themselves involved with the proposition of

428 REPRESENTATIVES 19 August 1981 Representatives: Proposed Procedures Committee

the honourable member. But I think there is interest in achieving the objectives he has laid down. I suggest to the honourable member that, privately and in discussion, a number of us may be able to pursue this matter to see whether we can establish a satisfactory method of working. Within the Government for five years we have had a committee which has worked to seek to establish parliamentary reform. There are a few successes on the board. Those successes are worthy successes. They relate to the factors that I have mentioned-the examination of Estimates and. the legislation committees. I would like to see this progress continue because it has been a satisfying part of my time in the Parliament to see changes made.
I would like to conclude on the note that whilst I appreciate the thrust of what has been proposed and the comments made by the honourable member, I think his proposition to establish yet another committee would not meet with the approval of the House at large. But the objectives should be something in the mind of every member. Perhaps other techniques can be adopted to achieve the honourable member's objectives.
Mr SCHOLES (Corio) (S.S9)-ln reply-I just want to respond to the debate on this matter. I want to thank all honourable members for the consideration they have given to the matter. I ,do not believe, as the honourable member for Wakefield (Mr Giles) has in,dicated, it is a satisfactory situation that because the. back bench members of the Government are satisfied with the methods by which legislation is considered outside this chamber, this chamb!!r or this Parliament should abrogate its responsibilities as .a parliament. Honourable members are, in fact, elected to serve in the Parliament. Whatever other activies they take are'consequential on that, not part of it. My motion follows a recommendation of the Joint Committee on the PariiamentarySommittee System. It deals with a practice which is adopted in other parliaments. It is highly successful. I believe that it would not become a lobbying exercise or an excercise whereby members seek to impose limited views. I think a broader consideration of matters and their practicality would have to be considered by such Ii committee otherwise its reputation would suffer. I am sorry that the Government is not prepared to adopt this procedure.
I say only one thing. The matters read out by the honourable member for Wakefield are listed for consideration by the Standing Orders Committee. The Speaker has taken the opportunity to

list for the Standing Orders Committee a number of matters which are, in fact, on the Notice Paper of this chamber and other matters which have been raised in debate by members in this chamber. The reason the matters are still listed for consideration is because the Standing Orders Committee is a cumbersome committee and its personnel are almost totally unable to deal with any sort of discussion other than in an extremely cursory manner. The matters will stay listed for consideration. If my memory serves me correctly, I think the time taken up by Standing Orders committees in the last three Parliaments would not add up to one working day.
Question resolved in the negative.
Sitting suspended from 6.1 to 8 p.m.
FREEDOM OF INFORMATION BILL 1981
Second Reading
Debate resumed from 18 August, on motion by MrViner:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
Mr BARRY JONES (Lalor) (8.0)-On 4 August 1822, in a letter toW. T. Barry, James Madison, who had been the fourth President of the United States, wrote these words:
A popular Government, without popular information, or the means of acquiring it, is but a prologue to a farce or a tragedy, or perhaps both. Knowledge will forever govern ignorance and a people who mean to be their own governors must arm themselves with the power knowledge gives.
Those wise words, 160 years old, are really the whole rationale of freedom of information legislation. But the legislation when it came in was a profound disappointment. Certainly, after it was worked over by the Senate it was a great improvement on its original form. It was still not as good, I think, as the American legislation of 1966 as amended in 1974 and certainly not asgood as the draft Bill that was prepared by the Australian Senate Standing Committee on Constitutional and Legal Affairs.
The long-awaited Freedom of Information Bill should be more accurately described as a 'Public Service Preservation from Disclosure' Bill. It is perhaps better than nothing. There are some guidelines which will enable citizens and organisations to gain access to information in the possession of the Government of the Commonwealth as set out in clause 3 of the Bill by, and I quote:
(a) making available to the public information about the operations of departments and public authorities and, in particular, ensuring that rules and practices affecting members of the public in their dealings with departments and public authorities are readily available to persons affected by those rules and practices; and

Freedom ofInformation Bill

19 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 429

(b) creating a general right of access to information in documentary form in the possession of Ministers, departments and public authorities, limited ?nly by exceptions a?d. exemptions necessary for the proteclton of essential public mterests and the private and business affairs of persons in respect of whom information is collected and held by departments and public authorities.
The tragedy is that the comparative breadth of the first paragraph of clause 3 is really cut back very considerably by the broad exemptions and exceptions created by paragraph (b). As Patrick Cook suggested in the National Times, perhaps the best way that the Bill could be described is as the 'Ask Me No Questions and I Won't Have to Waste Time Lying' Bill or, for those honourable members who follow the television program Yes, Minister, the 'Yes, Minister' Bill.
Mr Jacobi-An excellent program.
Mr BARRY JONES-It certainly is.
Mr Kerin-Frightening.
Mr BARRY JONES-It is frightening but I think a substantially accurate impression of what happens in Australia. There are a number of points about the Bill that are disturbing and I wanted to take up a number of the references made last night by the honourable member for Chisholm (Mr Harris) in his speech. The first question is that of retrospectivity. Originally there was to be no retrospectivity at all, but now there is a degree of qualified retrospectivity in respect of personal files-the capacity to go back and correct errors or omissions that are significant with respect to one's own personal file. The honourable member for Chisholm dismissed any claim for general retrospectivity. 'Why,' he said, 'people might be hunting back through files for the past five or 10 years'-as indeed they might. And so they should. I make the point that the United States legislation of 1966, as amended in 1974, does provide for retrospectivity and the result is that it has been possible to go back and to examine a number of very contentious matters on the historical record.
Mr Jacobi-So they ought.
Mr BARRY JONES-So they ought, as the honourable member for Hawker says. One could just mention very briefly some of the matters that have been raised of very general public concern. The Rosenberg case, for example, was noLa very popular case in 1953 and it still arouses a lot of sensitivity in American breasts. But it has become quite clear from the papers derived from the Rosenberg file that whatever the guilt or innocence of Julius Rosenberg, one can only say that the case against Ethel Rosenberg was absolutely threadbare-conceded in prosecution files-and that essentially her conviction was sought because

it was thought it would apply additional pressure on her husband to speak. But there was not even a prima facie case against her. Whatever one's view of the Cold War there has been a general concession that United States justice did not operate correctly in her case.
One might argue that the Ethel Rosenberg revelation indicates a weakness in the United States judicial system, but I think, to the contrary, it shows strength when it is prepared to make files available even in a case as sensitive as that one. The United States in fact has not suffered from these revelations. If we consider the case of Alger Hiss, we find very serious weaknesses in the prosecution case. It is quite clear from the. Federal Bureau of Investigation files that very significant information was withheld from the prosecution case. If it had been put to the jury in the way that Australian prosecutors put a broad case to the jury-that is, not just a narrow and segmented case-it is difficult to see that Hiss would have been convicted.
In the case of the assassination of President Kennedy, it is now patent on the basis of documentation brought out by the freedom of information legislation that there were grave deficiencies in the way that the Warren Commission operated, although the final conclusion reached by the Warren Commission may well be correct. What one can say pretty safely is that one could not safely reach that judgment on the basis of the evidence that the committee looked at. There may be other factors that have to be taken into account as well. We know a great deal more about the operation of the United States Government than we do about our own Government, partly because of the -access to the documents related to the Watergate affair. We know a lot more about the relationship of the Mafia and government agencies and, most significantly, because there are no exemptions granted in the United states freedom of information legislation, we know, and the United States citizens know, far more about the operations of the Central Intelligence Agency or the Federal Bureau of Investigation than we do about the Australian Security Intelligence Organisation or the Australian Security Intelligence Service or the Office of National Assessments.
Mr Jacobi-And the Government.
Mr BARRY JONES-And the Government. The point that the former honourable member for Fuller who is trying to interject will no doubt raise--
Mr Coleman-Wentworth.
Mr BARRY JONES-Yes, the honourable member for Wentworth. It just escaped me for the

- -f--

Freedom ofInformation Bill

minute. There has been a constituency in between as well. The honourable member for Wentworth will no doubt be able to take up the point as to why we should be so much more sensitive on these questions than the United States. Is it because the volume of secret information we have is so much more significant than it is in the United States? One has only to state that proposition to recognise its patent absurdity.

There are a whole number of issues, some of them historical, that one would like to know about in Australia. One of these, for example, would be the relationship of the Armed Forces and the civilian government in Australia during World War II-an issue I have raised before-to find out more about that potentially explosive situation where the Army was executing significant numbers of New Guineans, at least a hundred at the last count, without telling the Australian Government and also going to some pains to keep the Australian Government of the day from knowing. Another very contentious question concerns how it was that we got into the Vietnam War. Clearly, the whole question of the documentation-what contact there was with the United States Government and with the Government of the Republic of Vietnam-is of the greatest importance. And so it ought to be of the greatest importance. We have a right to know as citizens.

There are some major deficiencies in the legislation, some of which we will raise by way of
amendments. We are also told that after 12

432 REPRESENTATIVES 19 August 1981

Freedom ofInformation Bill

months or so there will be a review of the legislation. Two points need to be made in addition to our amendments. They are two fundamental issues of principle which will have to be looked at in the future and which make the United States Act worthwhile. Firstly, we have to be able to impose penalties directed at specific offices and officers. The real problem that often occurs when a person comes up against the bureaucratic maze is that he is not given a flat refusal. The officers do not say: 'No, you cann'ot have the file'. They say: 'Look, we had the file a few days ago but somebody else is using it. Come back next week'. When the person comes back the next week they say: 'We are terribly sorry. It had to go somewhere else. Perhaps if you come back next week you will get it'. A person is often not refused absolutely; he is just fooled around with.
The United States legislation provides that it is possible to go to a court and request it to impose a monetary penalty on an officer or a section of the access office which is procrastinating in a way that is unreasonable. Of course, an applicant has the onus of proof to prove that the officer is unreasonable in his procrastination, but it is an effective deterrent. The other factor is the provision of indices. The provision of a fine grained index is extremely important. Let me read what the legislation says. I do not regard this as adequate. Clause 8 states:
(I) The responsible Minister of an agency shall-
(a) ca use to be pu bIished, as soon as practicable , , . a statement setting out particulars of the organization . . . particulars of any arrangements that exist for bodies or persons outside the Commonwealth administration to participate . . . a statement of the categories of documents that are maintained in the possession of the agency . . .
It seems to me that the sheer setting out of a bland statement of heads of categories does not tell us enough. If a layman or an organisation which is not particularly skilled is trying to look for particular information, it is very easy to be snowed. There may be very valuable information in a document but the document may be of such imposing length and complexity that one does not know where to look. It is a matter of absolute necessity that there be an adequate fine grained index. Of course, with the computerised facilities available to the Government it should be possible for that to be provided very easily.
I do not doubt that the whole democratic system is under very serious challenge. We have an enormously powerful Public Service in Australia and the Parliament, relatively speaking, is losing its place in the sun under this Government. I think

freedom of information is a very powerful instrument that can strengthen the hand of the Australian voter, and if we have a more informed electorate we should have a better Parliament. I vividly recall, as you do, Mr Deputy Speaker, the Berthelsen case when we sat on the Privileges Committee of this House. It became patently clear that many senior public servants despised
their Ministers. They downgrade and disregard the Parliament, members of which they outrank arid outearn, and they feel that the electorate should be left in the dark. The attitude expressed so vividly in the British Broadcasting Corporation's television series Yes, Minister in which a
hapless Minister has rings run round him by his wily permanent head is as accurate for the Australian scene as it is a faithful representation of what happens in Great Britain. I conclude as I began by re-reading those words of Madison which are the whole justification, the whole
rationale:

A popular Government, without popular information, or

the means of acquiring it, is but a prologue to a farce or a

tragedy or perhaps both. Knowledge will forever govern

ignorance--

Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER-Order! The honourable member's time has expired.

Mr RUDDOCK (Dundas) (8.20)-1 welcome the opportunity to speak on the Freedom of Information Bill. The Bill is an important initiative of the Government of the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser). I wish strongly to applaud the fact that after a long period of gestation since the proposal for freedom of information was first advanced by spokesmen for the Australian Labor Party when it was in Opposition prior to its last being in government, the first Freedom of Information Bill introduced to any Parliament in Australia was a Bill introduced by the Government of Prime Minister Malcolm Fraser. It ought to be applauded because it was a very fine initiative and a very worthwhile reform of which Australians have every right to be proud. I admit that there might well be scope for argument about the clauses of the Bill. One might want to discuss the extent to which the Act, when it is in operation, ought to apply to particular departments and particular statutory authorities, and the manner in which we ought to apply the legislation to those authorities. But the fact is that this is a unique and admirable initiative of the Government of today.

The legislation was first proposed by the Labor Party when it was in Opposition. When it came to power in 1972 it did not come into the Parliament with a Bill of its own ready to proceed. It referred the matter first to an interdepartmental committee. We saw its first report in 1974. After the

Freedom ofInformation Bill

19 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 433

change of government in 1975 there was a further report from the interdepartmental committee and later we saw legislation in a draft form introduced into the Senate. It was the subject of consideration by a standing committee of the Senate. The Bill was the subject of reconsideration by the Government and the present Bill, in another form, was introduced into the Senate, was amended and has now reached this chamber. It comes here after contributions from the Opposition as well as from the Government.
I made those comments because I regard this Bill as a very worthwhile reform which one might hope would be discussed in this place in a bipartisan spirit. I was, therefore, exceedingly disappointed to hear the speech of the Deputy Leader of the Opposition (Mr Lionel Bowen) when he spoke on behalf of the Opposition on this matter. He was most grudging in whatever praise he had to offer in relation to the Bill and had some most uncomplimentary remarks to make about the Fraser Government. I must say that I could not let the moment pass without having a look at the Labor Party's record, which I propose to do. I think its record gives its members every reason to hang their heads in shame. I would not have done this except for the sorts of comments made by the Deputy Leader of the Opposition. I quote from the House of Representatives Hansard of 18 August, page 44. He said:
However. the Allorney-General was overridden by a very dominant Cabinet with a most dominant Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) who appears to be obsessed with secrecy. We will deal with some of these mailers as we go through some of the amendments later in the week.
We have seen the Government somewhat obsessed with secrecy.
I would suggest that members of the Labor Party hang their heads in shame when they listen to their Government's record on these matters, notwithstanding their rhetoric before 1972. On page 45 Mr Bowen was reported to have said:
There is also no doubt that many Ministers. including the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) are very wary of allowing the public to know much at all.
He says that is so notwithstanding the fact that the only Bill introduced into an Australian Parliament to give freedom of information was introduced by this Government, and notwithstanding that the Labor Party had three years in office after having promised in its policy speech to introduce legislation to give effect to freedom of information. I must say that I was somewhat alert to the fact that members of the Labor Party had an interest in this matter. There was a book written by a gentleman by the name of Spigelman. An Inside Dopester's Index of 100 Examples of Secrecy was included in that book by Mr

Spigelman, his book being entitled SecrecyPolitical Censorship in Australia. In relation to those 100 examples of secrecy I sought information from the then Prime Minister, Mr Whitlam. I asked him to specify which of those 100 items were not publicly disclosed up until 1972, how many of those items had been made publicly available since he took office and, if all of them were not readily available, what the reason was for the continuing secrecy in each case. I think that was a fair question to ask. There were 100 examples and I expected him to do a little work in relation to those matters which he had spoken of and which his private secretary had written so much of when the Labor Party was in Opposition. The then Prime Minister had the following remarks to make when he answered my question. They were published in the House of Representatives Hansard of 15 December 1974. He said:
I am, of course, aware of the book to which the honourable member refers. The author is now my Principal Private Secretary and I did most of the research for the book.
With respect to the items listed in this index and the many other examples of secrecy of which my Government became aware on assuming office. the honourable member would be aware that most are of historical interest only.
What an excuse to offer. He continued:
On 2 April 1973, I wrote to all Ministers suggesting that they exercise their discretion on items of current interest and in cases of doubt should await the criteria for release of documents in the proposed Freedom of Information Act.
How convenient! He never introduced it. He continued:
I believe a number of documents were released in 1973. If the honourable member is interested in any of the particular documents, I suggest he ask the Minister responsible.
That prompted my asking 100 questions of each of the Ministers in relation to each of the documents. Honourable members may be interested to know the results of those inquiries because they involve some prominent front benchers of the Opposition today and many prominent Ministers in the Labor Party at the time. I will take honourable members through a few of them. Mr Barnard, as Deputy Prime Minister, refused to make available the report of an independent firm of consultants on community attitudes to Service careers. Mr Barnard refused to make available a report on tests of anti-corrosive coatings carried out by the Central Testing and Research Laboratories. Mr Barnard refused to make available an interdepartmental study of housing problems of the Services. Mr Barnard refused to make public a report on the Defence Services Flying Training Program. Mr Barnard refused to make public the Morshead report on Army structure. Mr Barnard

434 REPRESENTATIVES. 19 August 1981

It would be clearly impractical to identify every unpub-

In my answer on 13 May 1975, (House of Representatives Hansard, page 2198)-

That was in answer to the question that I had asked-

Having sat in this place and listened to the way in which he harangued us about the Government's performance arid its record, I know exactly what he meant when he said that. He continued:
the Australian Government Liaison Service has been instituted to provide ready access to official government information and an abstract of government decision, legislation and Ministerial statements and speeches is regularly published in the Australian Government Weekly Digest.
That was the Government propaganda arm with which we were all so familiar. The foregoing was Mr Whitlam's idea of freedom of information. It was to set up a mechanism by which the Government could propogate those decisions about which it wanted the public to know and to enable it to do so in the most efficient way with the taxpayers' support. The Treasurer in 1975, was the honourable member for Oxley (Mr Hayden), now Leader of the Opposition. One would think that he would also have an interest in freedom of information, perhaps the same sort of interest that Mr Whitlam, the then Prime Minister, had demonstrated. One would have thought that, having been so critical of the then McMahon Government and its predecessor governments, when he was in Opposition, he would have been quite prepared to unlock all those secrets which his party had condemned the former governments for failing to disclose. But the fact of the matter is that Mr Hayden, then the Treasurer, was no different from the rest. He thanked me for my letter of 4 June which I had addressed to the Australian Loan Council concerning the confidentiality of

the proceedings of the Council. That was one of the items that Mr Spigelman had so carefully
selected. Mr Hayden's reply stated:

I have noted that in the first paragraph of your letter you draw on a reply dated 13 May 1975 given to you by my predecessor, in response to your question upon notice No. 2253.

Regarding the question of the confidentiality of Loan Council proceedings the facts are as stated in the answer to
that question. The Loan Council, as constituted under the Financial Agreement, comprises members of all State
Governments as well as the Australian Government, and al\ decisions taken by the Council, including those regarding the confidentiality of its proceedings and records are binding on
al\ members.

Well, fancy that! They were binding on Mr Hayden as well, even though he suggested, I assume along with his colleagues who were so committed to freedom of information, that they should be published. He went on to say:

It is not open to the Australian Government member or the Secretary to provide information in regard to them. It would therefore be inappropriate for me to provide further information except to say that al\ decisions on these aspects have
been carefully observed during the period that members of the present Government have participated in proceedings of the
Council.

and one standard when they are in government. I

would say that that was very plainly demon-

that some honourable members opposite will have

regard to the comments of their colleagues in con-

there are cases which, if they were faced with the

facts and the information, were in office and had

very carefully before they made them public. I

Mr ROBERT BROWN (Hunter) (8.40)-It is a matter of great regret that on this occasion the Parliament is considering legislation which is so clearly deficient in terms of the objectives that it purports to have. The concern about this legislation is not exclusively with the Opposition. In fact, the Opposition finds itself in a position where it shares this concern with very wide sections of

436 REPRESENTATIVES 19 August 1981

Freedom ofInformation Bill

the Australian community whose objectivity, fairness and concern could not be impugned. It is surprising to hear statements of the kind that have just been made by -the honourable member for Dundas (Mr Ruddock). His honesty in connection with this question will be determined when this House considers the amendments that will be moved and which have been foreshadowed by the Deputy Leader of the Opposition (Mr Lionel Bowen). At this time it will not be sufficient for the honourable member for Dundas to claim that the Opposition took a political stand on this because the amendments will commend themselves. At the time we will see the way in which the honourable member for Dundas, with all of his high sounding concern about this issue, responds to sensible and desirable amendments which will be moved by the Opposition. The Minister for Industrial Relations (Mr Viner) in his second reading speech said:
The crucial element in drafting the Freedom of Information Bill has been to strike the appropriate balance between openness and confidentiality.
The Opposition does not quibble with a statement of that kind. Despite the fact that such a significant proportion of the total time of the honourable member for Dundas was used in lis~ ing examples of Labor Ministers who, for very legitimate reasons, chose not to make public some documents, I am firmly convinced that the question of adequate balance between those two conflicting objectives in relation to legislation of this kind and to which the Minister made reference has, to a disproportionate degree, come down in favour of confidentiality. I am not surprised that the list that the honourable member for Dundas read out contained a greater number of reports that were not made public by Mr Barnard when he was Minister for Defence. That is the situation we would expect to find. It is certainly not the claim of the Opposition, any other section of the Australian community or any individual that the issues of government can be carried out in a goldfish bowl. No such claim has been made and no such claim is being made in connection with our consideration of this legislation.
The omissions of this legislation indicate quite clearly that the Government, which has been responsible for formulating it, is suspicious of the Australian people. It is suspicious of the Australian electors, fearful of open Government and feels threatened by public access to information. If it were possible for us to have full community participation in democratic decision-making that would probably be the most desirable form of

democracy we could have. The practical difficulties of that, of course, preclude it. We have settled for an alternative which involves representatives of the community taking the responsibility for making those decisions. There is no concern on our part that legislation of this kind would or could in any way damage the essential nature of
that decision-making process. The contrary applies. If details of the legislation were more effective, legislation of this kind would strengthen the operation of democratic government.
I emphasise, particularly to Government members, that they, like Opposition members, should realise that the question of freedom of information in connection with government operations-the operation of departments, bureaucrats and public authorities-is not a question for debate between the Opposition and the Government. In fact, it is a question of whether the Parliament will assert its authority over the bureaucracy and over the Executive and whether the Parliament-Opposition and Governmentwill ensure that people within the Australian community will have reasonable, ready and simple access to documents which are not matters of vital national security.
The Deputy Leader of the Opposition emphasised our concern about the exclusions in this legislation and the wording of some of the provisions of this legislation. He has already foreshadowed the fact that the Opposition will be moving amendments. If there are any Liberal democrats or any democratic Liberals on the Government side someone from the Government side will need to support the amendments that will be moved. If, in fact, no member on the Government side chooses to support those amendments, the political nature of this debate and the oolitical nature of the decision which will follow it will not be of the making of the
Opposition.
It is probably as well, because reference was made to the role of the Australian Labor Party when it was in government in relation to this legislation and in consideration of this particular issue, to have a look at the background to the situation that We now find ourselves in. Certainly before the election in 1972 the then Leader of the Opposition who became Prime Minister, Gough Whitlam, promised open government. In 1974 while the Australian Labor Party was in government the first "interdepartmental committee, which was appointed to investigate the provisions whichcould be involved in freedom of information legislation, delivered its first report. A subsequent report was delivered by the second interdepartmental investigation in 1976. In 1978 the

Freedom ofInformation Bill

19 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 437

first legislation was introduced. Certainly the Government and honourable members speaking for the Government have attempted to claim some credit for that legislation. It was suggested that members of the Opposition should hang their heads in shame. The same thing could be said about the Government in relation to the 1978 legislation. The attitude towards that legislation was reflected by all responsible thinking persons in Australia. It was a shameful piece of legislation. It was an apology for freedom of information and, in fact, it was referred to as a Bill which was designed to ensure freedom from information. That legislation, on the motion of an Opposition senator, Senator John Button, was referred to the Senate Standing Committee on Constitutional and Legal Affairs. That Committee produced a unanimous report recommending major changes in the Bill. The total number of recommendations made by that Committee was 93. This legislation we are considering now in no way accommodates all of the major matters of concern that were raised and decided unanimously by that Committee.
In a ministerial statement in 1980 Senator Durack rejected most of the recommendations despite the fact that senators on his side of the Parliament were parties to the unanimous decision that was made and the unanimous recommendations which that report brought forward. From 1972 until now-and probably into the futurethe parallel furrows that have been made by the dragging heels of those reluctant bureaucrats can be traced from one stage of the development in connection with this legislation to the next. The people who have resisted freedom of information have been the bureaucrats. The results of the two interdepartmental inquiries reflected very
unfavourablyon the extent to which the bureaucracy will go to ensure that the community does not have access to information which is vital to it.
For all the high sounding platitudes of the honourable member for Dundas, the simple fact remains that some of the most significant and valuable features of this legislation would not be here had it not been for the fact that Government senators crossed the floor in relation to some amendments and threatened to do so in relation to other proposals had the legislation not been changed. Those senators were Senator Alan Missen, Senator Kathy Martin, Senator Chris Puplick, Senator Neville Bonner and Senator David Hamer. They joined the Australian Democrats and the Opposition in voting for the amend-
ments. The result was, of course, that, even after the legislation had been introduced in the Senate, it was withdrawn and redrafted. No claim can be

made that the Government has been enthusiastic about ensuring that adequate safeguards and adequate provisions would be included in this legislation. In fact the contrary has been the case.
Ministers have, in consort with the bureauc-
racy, attempted at all stages to make sure that this legislation will be less effective than it should be and less effective than it might otherwise have been. That is the reason why the Parliament must assert its authority not only over the bureaucracy but also over the Executive and the Executive arm of Government. I include as the major component of the Executive arm of Government Ministers of the Government. One of the most important changes which resulted from that revolt in the Senate was the establishment of the Document Review Tribunal to review the conclusive certificates given to exempt documents. Of course the provisions in that respect certainly do not go far enough. But, of those changes that were introduced, certainly that was the most important and did overcome some of the reservations that existed in relation to the original legislation. The provisions for the correction of personal records that go back five years was another very important change introduced into the legislation. The Opposition certainly supports not only those changes but also others which have a less significant character. Changes were introduced also to ensure improvements in the mechanical means by which accessibility to documents would be facilitated. For example the indexing of documents was another desirable change and there were some relatively minor changes in the exemptions. One of the most disturbing aspects was that when the legislation was reintroduced in the Senate not only had there been some improvements built into it but also were additional exemptions for documents written into the legislation, which had not been considered in the 1978 legislation, and which had proved to be so insufficient that it was thrown out and reconsidered completely.
The Government resisted other well founded and desirable changes that were proposed by the Opposition in relation to such matters as the jurisdiction of the Ombudsman, the time limitation, prior documents, personal records and the exemption list. In relation to this legislation, I emphasise the fact that the Opposition, as I have said, does not find itself alone in its concern. For example, the Sydney Morning Herald in an editorial on 1 June this year in relation to this legislation stated:
On the whole, the Federal Government's Freedom of Information Bill, in the amended form in which it will go through the Senate this week, is probably better than nothing-but not much better. It cannot be greeted with enthusiasm, in spite of the many concessions-some of them fairly important, most

438 REPRESENTATIVES 19 August 1981

Freedom ofInformation Bill

of them minor-made by the Government in response to sustained pressure from critical senators, especially five dissatisfied Liberals. These five, in the words of Labor and Australian Democrat spokesmen, now seem to have capitulated to counterpressure from the Government, or to have done a deal with it.
They are not my words; they are the words of a responsible, national newspaper. Certainly that newspaper does not act as a spokesman for the Labor Party. If anyone is going to suggest that the Labor Party is conjuring up concern about the deficiencies in this legislation he needs only to refer to editorials of that kind and other types of comments that have been made by other individuals and interest groups within the community.
Mr Ruddock-Which one was that?
Mr ROBERT BROWN-It was the Sydney Morning Herald of 1 June.
Mr Ruddock-It gives the Labor Party a very good run.
Mr ROBERT BROWN-I could not have written a better editorial myself. There is one example that I will make particular reference to because it indicates better than any other example with which I am familiar the excessive secrecy, the obsession with secrecy, the fear of freedom of information which has accompanied decisions of this Government. I refer to the attempt by the Government to ensure that the book Documents on Australian Defence and Foreign Policy 1968-1975 would not be published, would not be distributed, would not be sold or be made available to the Australian people. That attempt on the part of the Government was defeated as it should have been defeated, but it was not defeated until the matter went before the court. Mr Justice Mason described the contents of that book, about which the Government had been so obsessive and so determined in its attempt to prevent publication, in these terms:
The book sets out a large number of Government documents. The book does not contain technical information of military significance; it does not deal with weaponry, armaments, military technology, logistical information or dispositions of forces. It is not affected by the '0' Notice procedures. Even the two documents referred to in the ex parte injunction contain no material of value to a hostile power.The appreciations made of Australia's strategic situation ahd the outlook in South-East Asia are based on information which is generally known. The judgments made are fairly elementary and they are now quite out of date.
Will we ever forget the attempt that was made by the Government on that occasion to frustrate the endeavour on the part of the publishers of that book to publish it and make it available to the Australian people? If that is not obsession with secrecy, I would need to be shown a better example.

I also quote a statement from the evidence of Mr Henderson, the Secretary of the Department of Foreign Affairs, in that case. He said:
. . . it is much more likely to facilitate our future relations if the Government has been seen to try its utmost to prevent that (disclosure) happening.
In fact it appears that the attempt was made to frustrate the publication of that book because of the Government's concern about its relationship with its powerful friends. The Sydney Morning Herald states:
This led to a suggestion, as Mr Justice Mason noted, that the Government acted as it did merely to demonstrate its bona fides to foreign Governments. If that is well founded-and Mr Henderson's evidence seems to bear no other constructionthen the Government's philosophy would seem to be: Keep our foreign friends happy-even if this can be done only by taking away Australian freedoms. Popularity at any price.
I will quote from another editorial of the Sydney Morning Herald because I find the writer of these editorials a very comfortable bedmate in relation to this question. I refer to an editorial of 3 December 1980 which relates to an attempt by the Government to prevent publication of the book Documents on Australian Defence and Foreign Policy 1968-75. It stated:
The Government acted in haste, if not panic, with a demonstrable lack of political nous. It acted to preserve not security but face and the convenience of ministers and public servants. And in acting it ignored from habit, if not arrogance, the principle affirmed by Mr Justice Mason, that against one kind of public interest, the confidentiality of government processes, there has to be weighed another, the need for the public to be fully informed of the actions of those who govern them.
The whole purpose of having effective freedom' of information legislation is to ensure that the processes of democratic government are strengthened, that the decisions mady by the bureaucracy which impinge so much on the lives of ordinary Australian people will be open to public scrutiny and that the electorate will have the opportunity to determine the nature of the evidence upon which decisions are made and the nature of the decisions that are made and then make some sensible and balanced judgment about the adequacy of those decisions and the purposes that those decisions are designed to fulfil.

Whilst this Bill may be seen to have some shortcomings and indeed whilst some senators of the Liberal Party of Australia and the National Country Party may have some difficulty in accepting the legislation in its final form, I ask . whether all members of the Parliament recognise that at least we have made steps forward. As far back as the early 1970s, while I was an external student of the University of Queensland and a member of Parliament, I was doing an assignment for the University on this very subject of freedom of information as it existed in the United States. I remember my heart beating fast and thinking how wonderful it would be. That was a decade ago. Those feelings in terms of reasonable access to reasonable information have not abated. However, I believe that honourable members on both sides of the chamber should concede that there is some information which is of a very vital nature and which, if the Government were to believe that it was to become public property the day after, would result in the functions of government suffering. We should be prepared to accept that this legislation goes a long way further than anything we have had before. We should also accept that in the years ahead after the nation has had an opportunity to assess the legislation there will be further chances to amend the legislation if it is found to be necessary and if it is found to have the

shortcomings that honourable members on the other side of the chamber are predicting. Indeed, they share a view which is shared even by some
members of my own party in the Senate.
I wish to make some criticism of the Bill in terms of the various schedules where exemptions exist. I refer particularly to Part 1 of Schedule 2,
which classifies exempt agencies. As all honourable members know, Schedule 1 sets out the courts and tribunals which are exempt in respect of non-administrative matters. In Schedule 2 there is a Part I and a Part II, and Part II classifies the agencies exempt in respect of particular documents. It is my view that the Government has been over sensitive in terms of classifying some agencies in Part 1 of Schedule 2. In regard to the
Australian Secret Intelligence Service and, indeed, the Australian Security Intelligence Organisation, I have absolutely no argument with the need for the operations of those two organisations to remain private and confidential. The responsible Minister is fully aware of what is going on, and indeed the Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden) at times is consulted and informed as to the events and happenings in those organisations. But I turn to some other agencies which have been given total exemption. When we give an agency total exemption we are saying that its operations are so vital to the security of the nation that we must expose nothing and that it must all be kept secret. Alternatively, what the Government might be saying when we consider some of the other organisations is that it has forebodings in terms of some of the activities of these organisations becoming known publicly.
I have already said that I completely accept that some agencies deserve exemption in terms of certain aspects of their operations, but let me read through this list of organisations which fall within the same classification as ASIO and the Australian Secret Intelligence Service. I shaH refer to them alphabetically. They are: The Aboriginal Lands Councils and Lands Trusts; the Auditor General; the Australian Industry Development Corporation; the Australian National Airlines Commission; the Australian National Railways Commission; the Australian Overseas Projects Corporation; and the Australian Shipping Commission. The next is the Canberra Commercial Development Authority. We must be careful there! The next ones are the Commonwealth Banking Corporation-it isperhaps a legitimate item in terms of the commercial operations of these entities-the Commonwealth Trading Bank; the Commonwealth Savings Bank; and the Commonwealth Development Bank. The next one is the Commonwealth Serum Laboratories

Commission. Nothing of its activities must be known by anybody.

I did not even realise that it was in it. During the recess I had the audacity to make criticisms of the Health Insurance Commission over its operations in the financial year 1979-80 which were nearly a bomber. Freedom of Information Bill because, in fact, he is very worried about it. One of the problems with conservative governments is the fact that basically they do not believe in divulging information. The present Government, without doubt the most conservative Federal Government since Federation, is no exception. Because members of this Government do not really believe in freedom of information they have produced a Bill which is full of exemptions and obstacles designed to block the attempts of persons wishing to investigate. the activities of government. What is more, despite protests from Opposition members in the Senate, the Government pushed the concluding stages of the debate through on the last day of sitting of the autumn session because it knew that this most unsatisfactory piece of legislation would not be approved by the Senate after I July.

It is interesting to note the report in the Age newspaper today which refers to an allegation by a member of the New South Wales Parliament that the Australian Security Intelligence Organisation planted the bomb which caused the death of three people outside the Hilton Hotel in Sydney in 1978. The aIlegation was accompanied by a request for an inquiry on the incident, particularly in view of the fact that no inquest had been held on the three deaths. It should be possible for a serious allegation of this nature to be investigated so that members of the public can feel satisfied that our security agencies are working in the best interests of Australians. It should also be possible in the case of the Hilton bombing to examine the substance of the documents which led to ASIO being granted far greater investigatory powers only three weeks after the bombing incident.

Mr Kent-It was so inept that it points to ASIO.
Mr MILTON-That is why the matter needs to be cleared up. There can be no justification for such secrecy. I submit that exempting these bodies in such a blanket way means that innocent people can be harassed, browbeaten or even lose their lives as a result of the ignorant and bigoted activities of officials without redress for the individuals concerned or for their families. Another matter which concerns me is the fact that clause 24 of the Bill gives the agency concerned, or the relevant Minister, the power to refuse to grant access to documents on the grounds that the resources of the agency are insufficient to deal with the request without interfering with its normal work. In effect, almost any request for s1jbstantial information could be refused on those grounds. It would appear that this clause has been inserted to ensure that any such request which may prove embarrassing to an agency or a Minister can be denied for seemingly innocuous administrative and financial reasons.

Mr BEAZLEY (Swan) (9.24)-The speech made by the honourable member for Fadden (Mr Donald Cameron) following earlier in the evening the speech made by the honourable member for Dundas (Mr Ruddock) was, by comparison, a breath of fresh air. I will not go into it in detail but I refer honourable members to the report on freedom of information by the Senate Standing Committee on
Constitutional and Legal Affairs. At pages 5 and 6 of the report, the Committee points out quite emphatically that the Labor Party was the first major political party in Australia to commit itself to freedom of information legislation. The Labor Party set up an interdepartmental committee. It needs to be remembered that a majority of Government senators are members of this committee. It was conceded by that majority of Government senators that that committee laid the foundations for the initial efforts of this Government in the freedom of information legislation in 1978. It must also be said that the Whitlam Government was only three years in office. This Government has been in office for six
years, and it has had all the advantages of having the groundwork laid by the Labor Government, but it has taken it six years to bring this legislation before the House. I submit that the Labor Party's record on this amply stands in comparison with the record of the Government. It stands particularly in comparison as we look at particular aspects of the legislation.
This is a Bill with many unsatisfactory features, some of which we shall attempt to deal with at the' Committee stage. When it was initially presented to the Senate earlier this year the Bill entailed only 20, of the 71 recommendations for a freedom of information Bill that would be compatible with Australia's procedures, Constitution and tradition, made by the Senate Standing Committee
which examined the matter in 1978-79. It was a Senate Standing Committee with a Government majority. The Senate amendments have picked up only a few omissions. Many omissions which continue to be ignored are quite critical to the Bill's effectiveness. Most of these omissions appear to be at their most glaring in Part IV of the Bill. I intend to deal with that later.
I now wish to address myself to the matters of principle which the Minister for Industrial Relations (Mr Viner) considered in his speech yesterday. As this debate proceeded over a number of years, a line of argument has been produced by Ministers, by sections of the Public Service and by the Queensland Government, in particular, in submissions to the Standing Committee, that this legislation is incompatible with the Westminster system by which we are governed. I still think that some of the trepidation on the other side bf the House in supporting the amendments that we will put forward at a later stage is based on similar misconceptions about the operations of the Westminster system as expressed by the Minister. In

Freedom ofInformation Bill

19 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 443

the course of the debate what is essentially a vague set of governing principles, when compared at least with those based on written constitutions extensively separating executive, legislative and judicial functions, has in this debate assumed a positively mystical aspect. It has inspired definitions of processes which those of us who are supposed to be at the core of the Westminster system in Parliament would find unrecognisable when applied to practice. The Minister has taken the trouble to define the Westminster process in such a manner. He has done so implicitly to excuse the Government's failures to implement substantially some very well thought out recommendations by the Senate Committee and by a number of public interest groups and to justify a vague promise to review the operation of the Act some years in the future.
The Minister's speech, as a lesson in primary school civics, has its merits. Here it is laughable. The Minister invites us to consider the relationship of Ministers to Parliament as an example of information dissemination-he went on about this at some length-which obviates the need for more stringent open government legislation. He said:
In this regard it is significant that Ministers are subject to questioning in Parliament on matters within their ministerial responsibility both without notice and upon notice by members of the Parliament. By second reading speeches on Bills, by their participation in debates, and by making of ministerial statements Ministers also give much information about government activity directly to Parliament.
He could have fooled me. This is a fine statement of theory. It does not take very long in this chamber to recognise that what takes place in this chamber during the processes that the Minister describes, except on the least controversial of matters, is a vigorous stoking of the boilers to lay the thickest possible smokescreen over most aspects of government decision-making. The evasive answer is admired by government supporters. Frankness provokes dismay. Paroxysms of delight ripple through Government ranks when a pointed, information-seeking opposition question draws a raving irrelevant ministerial counterattack chock full of appropriate ideological red meat. Standing Orders enshrine a Minister's right not to answer questions.
The whole process of a lack of information reminds me of what happened to an old friend of mine, Tom Hartrey, who was a former member for the State seat of Kalgoorlie in Western Australia. He was deputed by the citizens of Kalgoorlie who were then boycotting the pubs in Kalgoorlie to investigate reasons for a beer price rise in Perth. Tom Hartrey went down to investigate the reasons for a beer price rise and subsequently

reported back to his thirsty constituents. He said to them on that occasion: 'Ladies and gentlemen, I have been to the Premier to find an answer to the problem that we confront. I have been to the Government to find an answer to the problem that we confront. I have been to the breweries to find an answer to the problem that we confront. I have even been to the Opposition to find an answer to the problem that we confront. I have been everywhere but never has alcohol been so anonymous'. I would suggest that this is precisely the sort of problem that we find ourselves confronted with at Question Time. Never is any aspect of government activity more anonymous than it is when it finally hits this House in the processes so beloved of the Minister in justifying his position on what is supposed to be open government.
The basis of democracy is not responsible government, separation of powers or any other constitutional formula or legislative administrative process. They are tools to create the possibility of an orderly life and the advancement of democratic principles. The basis of democracy is that each individual in society has a right to determine how he or she is collectively governed. Implicit in this is a right of access to information on how decisions are made that directly and indirectly affect our lives. Proper freedom of information legislation would address those questions effectively.
The Westminster system of government as a model is not only vague but also fluid. It changes through time. It exists and its institutions exist in a dialectical relationship with society and are modified in their practice by social change. This process is usually cumulative and imperceptible so that Ministers who rely on textbook formulas may indeed not perceive changes though they are in fact obliged to act on them.
Let us take the question of ministerial responsibility. Westminster theory suggests a process of decision-making which moves from Public Service to Minister to Cabinet and then of explanation from Cabinet to Minister to Parliament to public. The Minister is responsible for his Department to the Cabinet, Parliament and the public. It is many years since this paradigm actually reflected reality. Ministers will not and cannot accept responsibility for all aspects of their Department's operations. How often have honourable members heard Ministers qualify answers to questions posed by the public or in this House with a statement that to the Minister's knowledge, such and such an answer is correct. How often in recent times have Ministers resigned when their inability to exercise fully control of their departments has

444 REPRESENTATIVES 19 August 1981

Freedom ofInformation Bill

been exposed? Indeed, in the area of national security policy, largely excluded from this legislation, Ministers have positively wallowedhistorically anyway in Australia-in their irresponsibility.
One former Liberal Prime Minister and Minister for Defence was pleased to say about one of the joint US-Australian installations under his direct jurisdiction:
I don't even know what Pine Gap is all about. I didn't then-
That was in 1969-
I could have asked but it didn't arise. I didn't ask about it.
More recently, the Minister for Defence (Mr Killen), who is an ornament to this House and one of the last vocal defenders of the doctrine of ministerial responsibility, was compelled to reveal considerable ignorance of the operations of his Department several years ago when he discovered from a reading of the Press, a significant upgrading of the North West Cape facility. Ministers cannot be expected to be so fully in command of the departments because the areas of government responsibility have expanded so massively since the nineteenth century when most contemporary definitions of the doctrine of ministerial responsibility were devised.
That is not a plea for small government. It is a vital argument for effective freedom of information type legislation as an aid to continued application of democratic principles in a modern industrial society. It is ironic that it is the democratic socialists in this House who are obliged on this legislation, as they are in so many other cases, to carry the liberal case for the rest of the Parliament, as I suggest we did strongly in the senate and as we are doing in this debate in this chamber.
I turn now to one part of the Bill which I think is most unsatisfactory and that is Part IV dealing with exempt documents in particular areas of government responsibility. Almost all the 15 exemptions leave unclear the criteria on which documents will be available to the public. The lack of clarity foreshadows heavy usage of the Administrative Appeals Tribunal and the Document Review Tribunal. In particular, I would like to discuss clause 33 dealing with questions affecting national security, defence, international relations and relations between the States. In the first place, it is positively ludicrous that communications between State and Commonwealth governments should be given the same exemption as documents involving national security. I do realise that conservative parties thrive on the

prospect of a return to pre-190 I constitutional arrangements. But it would be nice if they kept that strictly to statements of principle rather than incorporating it in their legislation. State conservative governments in particular are obsessive about secrecy and that is one of the major reasons why I suggest that they had this particular section added to clause 33.
Mr Coleman-Are you referring to the New South Wales Labor Government?
Mr BEAZLEY-I will come to it in a minute. The Queensland Government, for example, has already said that it will not abide by the decisions of the Freedom of Information Act and that it will do everything in its power to preclude correspondence it has from the Federal Government on it. Recently, in Western Australia, some of us were involved in an exercise to try to screw out of the Western Australian Government some information on the materials it had put before the Grants Commission when presenting the Western Australian case. Since the honourable member for Wentworth (Mr Coleman) is so interested in the New South Wales Government and its relationship with its public, I point out that the New South Wales public had a chance 12 months previously to look at their Government's submission to the Grants Commission. The New South Wales Government did not make it a closed document. But the Western Australian Government obsessively gripped the document to its heart and would not reveal a single detail of it to the public. So we were obliged to read through the Grants Commission report and make some sort of assessment of the type of case that the Western Australian Government put to the Commission. One can only say from the results that the case that the Western Australian Government put was extremely weak. So it is no wonder--
Mr Kent-No wonder.
Mr BEAZLEY-So it is no wonder at all that that document has not appeared before us. He has gone to the public and said that we can afford these cuts because we have one of the best student-teacher ratios in Australia. Yet when we go to the Grants Commission documents

Freedom ofInformation Bill

19 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 445

we discover that a case was made two or three months previously by the Western Australian Government in which it pleaded for extra funds because it said that Western Australians had one of the worst student-teacher ratios in Australia. It is no wonder, therefore, that the Western Australian Government does not want these documents revealed. What a sorry contrast the Western Australian Government is with the Government of New South Wales which includes its people on matters vitalto them. And how necessary it is that the Western Australian Government is compelled to face the light of public scrutiny with the sorts of cases it puts forward to the Grants Commission.
