I am concerned about the possibilities of a continuation of excessive secrecy in the foreign affairs and defence field. The Opposition is concerned not because it does not agree that a wide variety of intergovernmental communications, technical defence aspects and Public Service assessments ought not to be placed before the public view. Clearly they must not. We had an appaJling case last year when a diplomat serving in India had his position compromised by the premature revelation of quite appropriately frank assessments to his Department of the Indian Government. We are concerned, however, because in this area gross abuses have occurred where secrecy has not been based on national security but on nakeli political advantage. Recently I was privileged to accompany the Joint Parliamentary Committee on Foreign Affairs and Defence arOl,md the various joint United States-Australian installa~ions. I was interested to see a film which is regularly shown to tourists in Western Australia which connects quite directly the operations of the North West Cape installation with United States submarines. Yet, when the legislation to create the North West Cape installation was introduced in this Parliament in 1963, what any old tourist visiting the North West Cape installation now knows was not given to the Parliament or to the public at that time. A piece of information which was vital to an important foreign affairs debate and which was clearly not a matter of national security was concealed for deliberate political purposes.
Mr Armitage-And the commandant says himself that it was never classified.
Mr BEAZLEY-That is right. It was an Australian decision, not an American one. This secrecy, particularly When applied to key aspects

of the United States alliance, has massively hindered effective public debate on the American alliance and foreign policy and defence issues related to it. This has been despite the fact, or probably because of the fact, that governments have sought to win elections on the issue. We have the ludicrous situation in which any Soviet foreign affairs or defence official or United States congressman is far more likely to know details of many aspects of our relationship with the United States than are Australian parliamentarians and citizens.
The legislation will not make a great deal of difference to this situation. Where the Minister declares under clause 33 that the revelation of a document is not in the public interest there is no provision for overruling his decision. There is a provision whereby documents may be reviewed by a Document Review Tribunal but acceptance of the decisions of the tribunal on the classification placed on particular documents will not be binding on Ministers. We would much prefer a situation such as that which obtains in the United States whereby judicial review can overturn the security classification given to a particular document. The operation of the United States Act in this area provides us with a very useful insight into what the operation of such a system would be here. About 40,000 documents annually are sought from the United States Department of Defense by interested parties. Most of those documents are released. As to those which the Department wishes not to be released, the courts have been very restrained in compelling it to do so. They are loath, as they should be, to overturn a decision which the Department strongly supports. But as the moment of court action nears in the United States, the Department tends to review its classifications and release very substantial amounts of the material without a fight in court, revealing that the material to that point has been overclassified.
As the honourable member for Lalor (Mr Barry Jones) pointed out, this occurs not'only in the Department of Defense and the Department of State but also in much more security conscious departments, such as the Central Intelligence Agency. We ask for nothing more in this legislation than the same rights as are enjoyed by United States citizens, a nation so rightly beloved of honourable members opposite. We invite them to give the Australian public those rights. We will invite them to do that through the legislation we will put forward. We invite them to act like liberals and not people solely devoted to the possession of power, recognising that information is

446 REPRESENTATIVES 19 August 1981

Freedom ofInformation Bill

part of power, and wishing to preclude not only us but also the general Australian public from it.
Mr HYDE (Moore) (9.43)-The Freedom of Information Bill is significant for two reasons. First of all, the Bill is significant in its own right. It is also significant because the debate on it has been one of those occasions-regrettably they are rare occasions-when the Parliament has exercised its powers and authority to bring about significant improvement in legislation before it. During the last session of Parliament we debated this Bill, the Broadcasting and Television Amendment Bill and the Airlines Agreement Bills. On those three occasions the Parliament exercised more influence than it has been wont to do for many years. That is significant in itself.
I come to the Bill. It is useless for the Opposition to decry the Bill and say that it has no signincance because it does not do everything that it wishes. The point is that it will improve the democratic processes of this country. For that reason, it is to be supported. Perhaps in time in the light of experience we will make further amendments. Perhaps the government of the day will do so. But let us support this Bill for the time being. Let us use it for the time being. I will endeavour to demonstrate to the House that it will make a significant contribution. Perhaps this Bill would be less necessary if we did not have so much public sector intervention in private lives. In fact if we had less to be informed about maybe we would need the access to information less. It is government's capacity to interfere in the life and freedom of each citizen that makes this sort of democratic process necessary. We ought to think also about curtailing the over-interference of government in the first place.
I would like to quote briefly from the second reading speech of the Minister for Industrial Relations (Mr Viner). He said:
This Bill will establish for members of the public legally enforceable rights of access to information in documentary form held by Ministers and government agencies except where an overriding interest may require confidentiality to be maintained.
The basic principles of the Freedom of Information Bill are simple. Firstly, government departments and authorities should make their structures and functions known to the public and publish the rules and guidelines they apply in making decisions affecting members of the public.
Secondly, members of the public are entitled to access to documents held by government departments and authorities unless there are special reasons for not making those documentspublic.
That is a very good sentiment and I support it. Just to indicate to the House that my support has not come lately, because I wish to use the Bill and the freedom it will give me, I will quote again

quite briefly from one of my speeches. On an earlier occasion I said:
And when the Government assumes it is a better judge of individual welfare than are the consumer and producer working it out between them-consider taxi regulations, airlines, shopping hours-then it ought to say how that it is so, which is a reason why most free marketeers favour open government.
I just quote that to establish my credibility in this matter. I invite the House to consider a case study which will demonstrate that this Bill has relevance to the process of government and will in fact be useful. It will be known to honourable members that the jet trunk air routes in Australia are in the hands of two companies-Ansett Airlines of Australia and Trans Australia Airlinesand that these companies operate a duopoly that is to all intents and purposes a monopoly. They are protected from competition and are denied the opportunity to compete with each other except in respect of such relatively unimportant matters as hostesses' uniforms and meals. They are not able to compete with each other on any of the important things such as the price of travel and capacity loading of the aircraft. Even the aircraft they buy have to be approved by the Minister for Transport. There is no reasonable market to discipline these two bodies that provide so much service and disservice to the Australian people. There is no market that will work. The report of the Independent Public Inquiry into Domestic Airfares-the Holcroft report-made it quite clear that there was unlikely to be a functional market. In a letter to the Minister for Transport the Holcroft committee said:
. . . Ansett however proved far more spontaneous and the delay in settling the basis of agreement to supply information hampered the Inquiries' operations.
It went on to say:
As the Department of Transport is the regulatory authority for domestic airfares determination in Australia we also found it quite unhelpful that its submission was not received until 10 December, that is, some two weeks after the government had already moved to curtail the Inquiry. This made it impracticable to examine the Department in public hearing. Nor was any written submission received from the Bureau of Transport Economics.
It seems that the people who regulated this section of Australian economic life were less than forthcoming even when there was a government inquiry charged with the job of finding out what they were up to. The Holcroft inquiry at paragraph 1.56 of its report, said:
The Inquiry feels that progressive deregulation of all but safety requirements is the preferred solution.
The point is that the Holcroft inquiry did not believe, whatever happened, that the regulated environment could be made to work properly. It did not believe that a proper market discipline

Freedom ofInformation Bill

19 August J981 REPRE;SENTATIVES 447

could be applied to these two airlines. The relevance of that point is that in the absence of a market discipline, the only other discipline available is the democratic discipline of complete disclosure. When the Holcroft inquiry reported the Government was not happy with the findings and it appointed Price Waterhouse to make a further inquiry. Price Waterhouse recently reported and came down with a quite radically different formula, but it also agreed that the regulated environment was not likely to work very well. I quote again, this time from the Price Waterhouse report:
A rormula which provides a reasonable return to one airline could provide the other with an inadequate profit.
I remind honourable members that in a J2 month period air fares on these routes have risen by 16.7 per cent, a great deal more than the inflation rate, and that those fare increases have been justified from cost. There may be little doubt that the costs were actually incurred, but the relevant questions are whether the costs should have been incurred and whether there was waste and unnecessary featherbedding. However, there is a difference. Price Waterhouse reported its formula and sent it to the Government under separate cover in secret. Unlike the Holcroft formula, the Price Waterhouse formula is secret. Those of us who have been interested in this issue and who have called for deregulation are prepared to accept any reasonable formula. We want an unfettered and free environment, but if we are going to have a regulated environment and we cannot achieve a free environment we will accept a reasonable formula. However, it is necessary that all those calculations be made public or no member of the public should be expected to accept them, particularly when there is such prima facie evidence that it is all inefficient. An increase of 16.7 per cent is a very big increase for one 12-month period. The freedom of information legislation will enable me, other members of this House and all other members of the public to gain access to that fodemocratic discipline where this 'free enterprise' Government has not applied a market discipline. I intend to demonstrate that not from the Bill but from the explanatory memorandum because I believe that is easier to follow, at least for non-lawyers. I read from the explanatory memorandum:
Clause 51 places the onus or establishing that a decision given in respect or a request was justified on the agency or Minister to whom the request was made.
In other words, the onus is on the Minister if he refuses to provide documented evidence. If he does not co-operate, he must then demonstrate

that he had a reason not to. I will read again from the explanatory memorandum:
Sub-c1ause 22( I) accordingly provides that where it is possible and reasonably practicable to prepare a copy or the document with the exempt matter deleted, and the copy document so prepared is not thereby rendered misleading, the applicant shall be given access to such a copy . . .
In other words, if the Minister is able to delete sufficient material from the document so that it does not disclose the information that he is entitled to hold back, he is obliged to provide the rest. The onus is on the Minister to provide the document in as full a form as he can but there are certain areas in relation to which he can hold back information. The explanatory memorandum states:
Sub-c1ause 43 (I) provides that a document is exempt ir its disclosure would disclose trade secrets or other inrormation having a commercial value where the commercial value or that information would be diminished or destroyed by that disclosure. The sub-c1ause would also make exempt a document which contains inrormation concerning the business or proressional affairs or a person or the business, commercial or financial affairs or an organisation or undertaking where the disclosure of that inrormation would unreasonably affect adversely the person, organisation or undertaking in respect or the lawrul conduct or his or its business etc.
Sub-clause 43 (3) makes it clear that the protection or commercial privacy given by the clause extends to commercial undertakings carried on by the Commonwealth, a State or the Northern Territory.
However, as it is a regulated duopoly and as the profits of the organisation are guaranteed I do not believe that any information that could be disclosed could possibly be claimed to prejudice the organisation. The Minister must provide a document en request for information. Should he not do so the onus is on him to demonstrate that he is entitled under some clause of the Bill to withhold that information. The Government has commissioned a submission to be presented to it. Price Waterhouse has submitted in secret and yet, as I read the terms of this legislation, once the Bill becomes law the Government will not have the power to withhold the secret formula that ,has been put forward by Price Waterhouse. I believe that will make it easier for all those members of this House and those members of the public who have a genuine interest in this issue to argue their case, to look at the relevant information and, if necessary, to apply the proper democratic disciplines to the Government.
I understood originally that when Price Waterhouse was to report it was to report publicly. In fact, I understood that all information was to be made public and that the democratic disciplines were to be applied in the absence of the market disciplines. This piece of legislation gives

After making 12 payments for a total of $4,252.00 we have

is bad for business. The owners want to get off the hook. The simple and time honoured solution is for them to buy their way out of the situation. Consequently, before and during the recent strike

Adjournment

19 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 449

the industry continuously stated its desire and intention to pay its workers $20 a week and to provide some other benefits. However, the Federation also said that it required the increase to be approved by the Conciliation and Arbitration Commission. It has inferred that it takes this action in support of the arbitration system and the notion of the rule of the umpire. It has further inferred that as it is prepared to pay the court is being obstructive in continuing to refuse to rubber stamp the proposal. It encourages the public to see the Arbitration Commission and the Government as the villains of the piece who are unnecessarily obstructing the worker from being granted a legitimate wage claim. This is hypocrisy in the extreme.
The facts are that the trucking industry does not want to pay its workers an extra $20 a week plus benefits and, further, it is probably reasonable to say it cannot afford to do so. The trucking industry is trying to get its customers to pay the increase. In many cases this would follow the traditional and meekly accepted policy of announcing a price increase. The dilemma facing the truck owners is that a large amount of their business is conducted under contracts. These contracts have rise and Jail clauses which, in the case of wages, are tied to decisions of the arbitration system. If there is no Arbitration Commission approval there is no increase in contract payments. The change that has occurred since I made my statement in February is that the Arbitration Commission apparently has recognised the grave injustice it does the Australian consumer when it rubber stamps sweetheart deals. I congratulate it for its courage and new-found determination. The Australian consumer is where the buck stops being passed.
Many would argue that the companies that have these large contracts with the trucking firms make large profits and could afford to pay. Unfortunately, their customers, as users of truck freight services, generally deal in consumer goods. They do not have contracts with their clients and can pass on the increased costs at any time. They do so, just as the bread and milk industries, which are direct employers of TWU members, will do so now that they have agreed to pay the increase to TWU members. To overcome the court's resistance, the employer and employee representatives are both now arguing that there has been an increase in work value. To me, an increase in work value infers an increase in productivity. If the productivity increase is equal to the increase in wages there will be no need to pass on cost increases to anyone and the consumer will not suffer. If this is

the case, why are the trucking companies so determined to ensure that they can pass on the cost? Irrespective of the evidence now being presented to the Arbitration Commission, the total cost of the wage increase will be passed on. This proves to me that the productivity argument is equally fallacious. The trucking companies are not restricted from paying the wage increase provided they take it from their profits.
I close by saying to the arbitrators: Please continue to remember your responsibility under section 40 of your Act, the Conciliation and Arbitration Act, which says, in part 'the Commission shall act according to equity, good conscience and the substantial merits of the case'.
Mr JACOBI (Hawker) (lO.lO)-Last Tuesday the House paid tribute to a very personal friend, Fred Temperly. We reached agreement on both sides of the house that there would be only two speakers, and I honoured that. I would like to place on record my personal and sincere thanks to a very kind friend. I have known Fred since I became a member of this Parliament 12 years ago. He has had something like 26 years service in Hansard. He had a lengthy service inside this Parliament and a splendid military record. He has helped not only me personally as a new member but also many members both in this chamber and in the Senate for a variety of reasons and on a variety of problems which all new members face. I sincerely regret the reasons for his resignation. I think they were unfortunate and unnecessary and if appropriate action had been taken I think his resignation could have been avoided.
Fred joined the Department of the Interior in 1939 and was promoted to the Parliamentary Reporting Staff as Clerk and Accountant in April 1955. He was promoted to Assistant Reporter, a designation. which changed to Administrative Officer in 1968. He held that position until he retired in July of this year. Fred was an extremely dedicated and diligent officer. He helped everybody who went to him for .advice and help with courtesy and kindness. This Parliament has lost a very good officer. The members have lost a very good officer. I speak with a great deal of sadness and regret. I conclude by saying that honourable members, honourable senators and officers of the Parliament who worked with Fred and knew him, as I did personally, found him to be a very courteous, helpful and dedicated officer to the Parliament, the reporting staff and the people with whom he worked. I regret to say that unfortunately he was not appreciated at the level he ought to have been. This Parliament has lost a dedicated officer but fortunately I have not lost a

450 REPRESENTATIVES 19 August 1981

Adjournment

friend. I wish Fred and his wife good health and a long and happy retirement.
Mr BAUME (Macarthur) (10.13)-Tonight I want to talk about the functions of the Commonwealth Employment Service and the Department of Social Security in Kiama which is in my electorate. The reason I draw these matters to the attention of the House is that the honourable member for Cunningham (Mr West) whose electorate adjoins mine has done me the courtesy of discussing them in the local media with his usual incapacity to report accurately the facts. I advised the honourable member for Cunningham that I would be replying to his comments tonight. I regret that he is not in the House. I note that he is now entering the chamber. I welcome him. It is extraordinary that the honourable member should have found himself capable of discussing the closure of the CES office in Kiama without being able to bring himself to concede the fact that the Department of Social Security had opened an office in Kiama to deal with the very issues and the very subjects that he complained would no longer be dealt with because the CES office had been closed.
For the benefit of the honourable member for Cunningham, who may well have been outside both his and my electorate examining matters in the remoter parts of Australia which appear to interest him more than the realities in both our electorates, I advise him that the Commonwealth Employment Service has given up the functions about which he complained. In fact, they are now performed by the Department of Social Security. This is why there is no longer a CES office in Kiama and why the Department of Social Security has opened an office in Kiama to deal with the very problems that, in fact, the honourable member for Cunningham app.arently is unaware have been transferred from one department to the other. It may well be that the honourable member for Cunningham simply did not know that, or it could be that he chose to ignore it when making his statements to the local media.
I draw to the attention of the honourable member for Cunningham the fact that on the first and third Wednesdays of each month the Department of Social Security has a presence in its temporary premises in Kiama-I regret that they are temporary and I think the Department should do something about them-to handle matters relating to unemployment benefits, among other matters, raised by the people of that town. I am advised by the manager of the local social security office that despite the fact there is a big placard outside and despite the publicity generated by the honourable member for Cunningham in my local

media during the week, 12 people attended that office today. There is obviously a massive demand for the services of the local social security office. I hope that what the honourable member for Cunningham has done, by his mis-statements, may bring to the attention of many people the fact that this facility is available, rather than the contrary, which is the mistaken public view of the honourable member for Cunningham.
There is no doubt that there is a need for a greater and a more regular presence of the Department of Social Security in Kiama. However, I would point out to the honourable member one simple thing: When the Commonwealth Employment Service was in Kiama it was housed in the only available space, which was up a very sharp flight of stairs. After some discussion with me the Department of Social Security agreed that it would be unreasonable to require pensioners, who use the services of the Department, to walk up those stairs. Ground floor facilities have been established. I hope that those facilities will be more frequently available. It is possible to send matters to the Department of Social Security and CES offices by post. In fact the Department can backdate claims received by post for seven days. So there is no disadvantage to the residents of my electorate by this change of function. The fact that only 12 people managed to turn up to the Department of Social Security clearly indicates--
Mr West-You are more worried about paying the dole than finding jobs.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr MiIlar)- Order! The honourable member for Cunningham will resume his seat.
Mr BAUME-He is maintaining his usual level of high competence. I suggest we need better services but we have some now.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER-Order! The honourable member's time has expired.
Mr BEAZLEY (Swan) (10.18)-1 raise in the House tonight the situation in Poland and the need, as I believe it, for the Government to offer substantial assistance at this time to the Polish people. I think there can be no question that the substantial democratic gains made by the Polish people over the last 12 months, largely through industrial activity, are being placed in jeopardy by Poland's economic state. The Polish Government has had considerable trouble in convincing Western banks to re-schedule its debts. Poland has had considerable trouble in securing the amounts it needs from the most immediate Western nations in immediate assistance to feed its people. The problem of the shortage of food is a particular crisis.

Adjournment

19 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 451

Since World War II the Polish regime has been faced with popular revolt when food supplies are short or when prices substantially increase. In the past this has posed problems of survival for rigid communist regimes. It now poses a' problem of survival for the liberalised Polish regime and for the general reforms that have been achieved, largely in an orderly fashion. As it happens, the principal need of the Polish people in foodstuffs is meat and sugar. Meat and sugar are staples of the Polish diet. In this, Poland is different from other nations in central and eastern Europe, where cereals form the basis of the diet. In the Polish case, meat a,nd sugar are the staple diet. As I have said, it is no accident that when there have been shortages of meat or meat prices have risen that has been the point at which the Polish people have found the Government or the system in which they are operating least sustainable. It is no accident therefore that, at this point, the Polish people should be in a state of revolt.
I am reliably informed that at this time we have in this country reasonable stocks of meat and sugar. We are in a position to offer immediate and substantial aid to the Polish people. The Opposition has raised the Polish question in this House on a number of occasions. The Government has expressed sympathy for the plight of the Polish people. The time has come for people to put their money where their mouths are. In the developments in Poland, we have rightly seen a major advance in democratic principle, a major advance in the rights of people to organise industrially, the right of people to have a say in their own affairsrights it must be said in regard to which members on the other side of the House do not necessarily offer the same degree of sympathy when they are sought in this country. Nevertheless, members on both sides of the House have welcomed those developments in Poland. Those developments need to be sustained. They can only be sustained if the population is secure in the belief that the governing processes that they have recently established for themselves are capable of delivering to them their basic needs. We all understand that in a period of chaos there will be many areas of dislocation. These areas may continue for some considerable time. We need to be sympathetic to that and to adopt a generous attitude towards it.
We have made available to the Polish people about $40m in credit for wool sales. In fact that is not a particularly generous offer and I understand it has not yet been taken up, although negotiations are proceeding. We can do a lot better than simply offer the Polish people a guarantee which they will in large measure wish to meet and repay themselves. We can do better than that. I think we

need to make an outright grant, not a grant of credits, from our substantial stocks of meat and sugar.
Mr MILTON (La Trobe) (10.28)-1 wish to speak tonight about the Government's policy of taxing means tested pensions. Pensions in this country are without doubt only paid to those who are judged poor enough by a means test to qualify them.
Mr Howard-That was Gough Whitlam's policy. You introduced it.
Mr MILTON-A heavy burden of the Government's taxation policy on pensioners falls on lone parents who choose to work at casual or part time rates.
Mr Howard-Absolute nonsense.
Mr MILTON-I am very glad that the Treasurer (MrHoward) is here tonight to hear what I have to say. The present policy in fact acts as a disincentive for lone parents to join the work force and re-establish themselves as effective contributors to their family's well being. I wish to describe briefly the situations of two of my constituents who have called on me for help. These two women are both supporting parents with two and three children respectively. To augment their widows' pensions, they have obtained employment with a catering firm on a casual basis. This employment requires them to accept particular assignments at very short notice. By dint of accepting any work offered to them, both women have established regular work on a part time basis with this catering firm. This has enabled them to maintain a home and provide some of the requirements of their growing children. However, such employment by its very nature is casual. When the firm has a job, the employee is contacted and expected to take the work. Under these circumstances it is very difficult to average out a weekly income.
Mrs Darling-Impossible.
Mr MILTON-Some time may pass with no income at all. As the honourable member for Lilley says, it is impossible. At other times there is a lot of work. The arrival of the annual group certificate then becomes an object of dread for these women. If they are over the acceptable amount, they lose their fringe benefits-a vital security for all pensioners. The ftinge benefits scheme, which undoubtedly can be calculated to an amount each week in money, is far more than this to a lone parent. It is security for health care for their children and themselves, their travel and communication and in fact any disaster, which intrudes on

No amendment can be moved to question:
Provided that(a) if a division be in progress at the time fixed for inter-
ruption such division shall be completed and the result announced; (b) if, on the question-That the House do now adjourn-being proposed, a Minister requires the question to be put forthwith without debate, the Speaker shall forthwith put the question; (c) a motion for the adjournment of the House may be moved by a Minister at an earlier hour; (d) any business under discussion and not disposed of at the time of the adjournment shall be set down on the Notice Paper for the next sitting, and (e) iF the question-That the House do now adjourn-is negatived, the House or committee shall resume the proceedings at the point at which they had been interrupted:

Mr Sinclair to move-

That, unless otherwise ordered, the following sessional

orders to provide for the operation of estimates committees be

adopted:

.

Committal and consideration in estimates committee:
( I) After the speech of the Leader of the Opposition, or a
Member deputed by him, on the motion for the second reading of the main Appropriation Bill for a year, the proposed expenditures for ~he Departments and services contained in the Schedule to that Bill may be re, ferred to an estimates committee. Such referral sha~i
be on motion, moved by a Minister, of which notice has been given. A committee may be ordered to report by a specified date.
(2) There shall be 6 Estimates Committees to be known as Estimates Committees, A, B, C, D, E and F, which
shall not vote on, but shall examine and report upon proposed expenditure for the Parliament, Advance to the Minister for Finance and each Department of
State; such report may contain a resolution or expression of opinion of the committee but shall not vary the amount of a proposed expenditure.

Members: (3) Each estimates committee, to consider each proposed
expenditure, shall consist of the Minister responsible in
the House of Representatives for the proposed expenditure under consideration, together with 10 Members, excluding the Chairman.
(4) Members of an estimates committee shall be nomi-
nated by either the Prime Minister, the Leader of the House the Government Whip or the deputy Whip and either the Leader of the Opposition, the Deputy
Leader of the Opposition, the Opposition Whip or the deputy Whip and every nomination of a member of the committee shall be forthwith notified in writing to the
Speaker. (5) In nominating Members to'an estimates committee to
consider each proposed expenditure regard shall be had to the qualifications and interest of those Members nominated and to the composition of the House.

Either the Prime Minister, the Leader of the House, the Government Whip or the deputy Whip and either the Leader of the Opposition, the Deputy Leader of the Opposition, the Opposition Whip or the deputy Whip, shall have power to discharge from time to time any of those Members nominated by them and to nominate others in substitution for those discharged. The responsible Minister may nominate another Minister to act for him as required. (6) The nomination of Members to consider the proposed expenditure of each department or service shall be announced to the House as soon as practicable after the proposed expenditures have been referred to a committee.
Chairman: (7) The Chairman of an estimates committee shall be- (a) the Chairman of Committees; or (b) a Deputy Chairman of Committees to be appointed by the Chairman of Committees.
(8) The Chairman of Committees, a Deputy Chairman of Committees of any member of the committee shall take the Chair temporarily whenever requested so to do by the Chairman of the Committee during a sitting of that comittee.
Quorum: (9) The quorum of an estimates committee shall be 5, excluding the Chairman, and, if at any time a quorum be not present, the Chairman shall suspend the proceedings of the committee until a quorum be present, or adjourn the committee.
Participation by other Members: (10) Members of the House, not being members of the committee, may participate, at the discretion of the Chairman, in the proceedings of the committee, but shall not vote, move any motion or be counted for the purpose of a 'quorum.
Minutes of Proceedings: (II) All proceedings of a committee shall be recorded by the Clerk to the Committee, and such records shall constitute the Minutes of Proceedings of the committee, and shall be signed by the Clerk to the Committee.
Proceedings in estimates committee: (12) Consideration of proposed expenditures in an estimates committ'ee shall follow, as far as possible, the procedures observed in a committee of the whole with the following exceptions: (a) Speech time limits and the restriction on the number of times a Member may speak to each question (S.O. 91) shall not apply. (b) Voting shall be taken by a show of hands; tellers shall not be appointed.
Time limits on Report: (13) During consideration in the House of the reportsJrom estimates committees, the maximum period for which a Member may speak shall beEach question before the ChairMinisters-Periods not specified Any other Member-IO minutes.
Report from estimates committee and further consideration: (14) A report of an estimates committee shall be presented by the Chairman of that committee or a member of the committee deputed by him and shall contain any resolutions or expressions of opinion of the committee. (15) On a report from a committee being presented, a future day shall be appointed for taking the report into consideration. (16) On the calling on of the order of the day for the consideration of a report from an estimates committee, the

456 REPRESENTATIVES 19 August 1981
following question shall be proposed in respect of each proposed expenditure:
'That the proposed expenditure be agreed to (and that the resolution(s) or expression(s) of opinion agreed to by the committee in relation thereto, be noted)"
(17) Upon the completion of consideration of the reports of estimates committees, the question shall be proposed forthwith-'That the remainder of the Bill be agreed to',
(18) When an Appropriation Bill which has been referred to an estimates committee is finally agreed to by the House, a future day shall be fixed, on motion, for the third reading,

The following answers to questions were circulated:

Works of Art
(Question No. 116)
Mr Les Johnson asked the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment, upon notice, on 26 November 1980:
(I) How many works of art from collections under the control of the Commonwealth and Government instrumentalities have been lent or hired to (a) Federal, State and local government departments and instrumentalities or (b) private individuals and corporations.
(2) What are these works and where are they exhibited.
Mr Wilson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) I am informed that (a) as at November 1980, 1410 works of art were placed with Federal, State and local government departments and instrumentalities. This figure excludes those works loaned from the National Collection for placement in Official Establishments within Australia: as at 30 June 1981 there were 204 such works on loan from the National Collection; (b) 17 as at November 1980. I am also informed that, in addition, in November 1980, 31 works were placed on loan with bodies such as international organisations or on loan to the artists for exhibition purposes.
I am advised that the above figures exclude short-term loans to exhibition management bodies and exclude any loans which may have been made by agencies outside my portfolio responsibility.
(2) The works involved are too numerous for identification of works by artists, title and location to be incorporated in Hansard. I should be pleased to provide that information to the honourable member if he would call at my office.

German Age Pensions

(Question No. 282)

Dr Klugman asked the Minister for Foreign Affairs upon notice, on 4 December 1980:

(I) Has his attention been drawn to the claim in the periodical Die Woche of 23 September 1980 that the Constitutional
Court of Karlsruhe has ruled that Australian citizens of German origin are entitled to German old-age pensions.

(2) Is there any substance to this claim.

(3) If so, will he make appropriate representations to the

Government of the Federal Republic of Germany to ensure

prompt payments,

'

(4) Ifnot, will he make representations to the Government of the Federal Republic of Germany that contributory pension entitlements should not be lost when the eligible person becomes an Australian citizen.

Mr Street-The answers to the honourable member's questions are as follows:

(I) Yes.

(2) Yes. On 23 March 1979, the Federal Republic of Germany's Constitutional Court in Karlsruhe ruled that the present situation whereby people who renounce their German nationality and live abroad lose their entitlement to a German pension is not in accordance with the basic law of the Federal Republic of Germany.

The Constitutional Court has now asked the Federal Pensions Office in Berlin to work out a system enabling pensions to be paid to former German citizens abroad. The Pensions Office is at present working on this but cannot say when the system will be in place. It has stated that the proposed system would probably take the form of a pension based on contributions paid within the present borders of the Federal Republic of Germany since 1945, thus not including contributions paid before 1945 in the former German Reich or after 1945 in parts of the Reich not now part of the Federal Republic of Germany for example the German Democratic RepUblic. Applications for payment of such pensions cannot yet be approved since enabling legislation has not yet been drafted.
(3) and (4) See (2). Under the circumstances representations would not be appropriate at this time.

Recruitment of Skilled Labour from Overseas
(Question No. 284)
Mr Hawke asked the Minister for Employment and Youth Affairs, upon notice, on 4 December 1980:
Further to the commitments he gave in a speech to the Oil and Gas Conference on I December 1980 in Perth, Western Australia, what procedures or regulating machinery does he maintain to ensure that recruitment of skilled labour from overseas is restricted to those employers who maintain maximum apprenticeship levels and training effort.
Mr N, A. Brown-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
Approval of applications for immigration, including employment nominations, is the responsibility of the Department of Immigration and Ethnic Affairs. The Department of Employment and Youth Affairs assists in the provision of occupational information and is consulted, where appropriate, on matters relating to worker entry.
Overseas recruitment by Australian employers is dependent upon a prior canvass by them of the Australian labour market, together with clear evidence that they have a satisfactory training record. Where employers are not considered to have a satisfactory training record, approval to recruit overseas would not normally be given.

Rural Finance

(Question No. 314)

Mr Kerin asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on

4 December 1980:

-

(I) What are the total avenues of finance available to the rural sector.

(2) What are the interest rates and terms of the loans or facilities available from the instrumentalities referred to in part
(I ).

(3) What has been the increase in rates of interest and terms of loans over each of the last 10 years.

Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

(1) There is a wide range of sources from which finance is made available to rural producers in Australia. Rural producers have access to certain institutions and facilities de-
signed explicitly to provide finance for the rural sector, as well

Answers to Questions

as to sources of finance available to the community more generally.
For more detail I refer the honourable member to the Bureau of Agricultural Economics (BAE) publication 'A Review of Credit in the Australian Rural Sector (1977), which gives a comprehensive list of these sources to that time. (See in particular Chapter 4 and Attachment E.) Since that BAE study another institution-the Primary Industry Bank of Australia Limited-has been established to refinance loans made to primary producers by banks and other lenders. Another relevant summary reference is the occasional table 'Estimated Rural Debt to Specified Lenders' published most recently on page 331 of the Reserve Bank Statistical Bulletin for December 1980.
(2) and (3) Historical data of interest rates charged on term loans, farm development loans and overdrafts by major trading banks and on rural loans by the Commonwealth Development Bank of Australia are produced in Tables EIV and EX on pages 154 and 162 respectively of the BAE publication. The following figures provided by the Reserve Bank of Australia update these records. Loans refinanced by the Primary Industry Bank of Australia Limited currently carry maximum interest rates of 12.0 per cent per annum for loans of less than $100,000 and 13.75 per cent per annum for loans of $1 00,000 and above.

The terms of loans available vary between institutions and types of finance and depend on a number of factors, including,
for example, the purpose and size of the loan and the borrower's circumstances. Terms may also be renegotiated in the light of special factors such as drought.

The Term Loan Fund, established in 1962, initially provided loans for fixed periods varying from 3 to 8 years; this was changed to 3 to 10 years in 1978.

The Farm Development Loan Fund was establshed in 1966

with a view to facilitating access to medium to long term

finance for periods generally ranging from 8 to 12 years.

Finance from the Commonwealth Development Bank of

Australia is usually made available by way of medium to long

term loans repayable over a period suited to the circumstances

of each individual borrower; equipment finance is generally

for terms of 3 to 4 years. Loans refinanced by the Primary In-

dustry Bank of Australia Ltd are for terms of between 8 to 30

years (currently averaging about 16 years). Loans from other

sources would generally be for shorter terms than those pro-

vided by the above mentioned institutions.

National Indoor Sports Centre in Canberra
(Question No. 448)
Mr Adermann asked the Minister for the Capital Territory, upon notice, on 24 February 1981:
In the planning of the National Indoor Sports Centre in Canberra what facilities and opportunities will be provided for handicapped persons to participate in various sporting activities.
Mr Hodgman-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
There is no special access for handicapped persons to the playing area of the. National Indoor Sports Centre. The National Capital Development Commission, in conjunction with the Department of the Capital Territory, is currently considering the provision of sp~cial access for handicapped persons.

Importation ofCommun~cations Equipment
(Question No. 451)
Mr Innes asked the Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs, upon notice, on 24 February 1981:
What factors prevent the application of a general prohibition on the importation of those types of communications equipment, the use of which is illegal.

Answers to Questions

19 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 459

Mr Moore-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
There are no factors preventing the introduction of import controls on specific communications equipment which cannot be used legally in Australia. The regulations under the Customs Act, however, must set out complete details of the equipment under control and, therefore, a general prohibition would not be approp'riate.
An import prohibition was imposed recently on CB radios which do not comply with the specifications set out in the regulations.
In addition provision exists in the regulations to prohibit the importation of equipment which is considered to be dangerous to users.

Department of Transport: Special Arrangements for Disabled Persons
(Question No. 473)
Mr Holding asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 24 February 1981:
(I) In view of the fact that 1981 is designated as the International Year of Disabled Persons, what special arrangements has his Department made to assist disabled persons.
(2) Do any special arrangements made include (a) increased employment of disabled persons and (b) improved access for disabled persons to any building or other facility occupied by his Department; if so, what are the details.
(3) What budgetary allocations were made in 1980-81 to facilitate any special arrangements for disabled persons in his Department.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
( I) The special arrangements made to assist disabled persons can be seperated into two areas. First, programs specifically introduced because of the International Year of Disabled Persons (IYDP) and second, programs focusing on the needs of the disabled but not specifically initiated for IYDP.
(i) Programs specifically introduced because of IYDP:

The Department of Transport convened a workshop on transport planning and research and the handicapped in March 1981. The workshop enabled the transport planners who attended from all States and Territories to discuss Australian and overseas developments and generally review developments in making land transport services more accessible to the disabled. The workshop will report to the July 1981 meeting of Australian Transport Advisory council (ATAC) , and will advise on the need for a common code of practise to meet the transport needs of the disabled.
The Commonwealth undertook to disseminate to all State and Territory Governments results of current initiatives in this area ranging from staff awareness programs, paratransit trials, vehicle restraint research, parking provisions, and low-cost modifications to fixed route systems. As a result of the workshop, the Commonwealth will look at possible ways to encourage greater utilisation of existing fleets of specialised vehicles for the disabled, as well as the possibility of reducing the costs of audio-tactile aids through bulk ordering.
In February I held a meeting with members of the Australian Council for rehabilitation of the Disabled (ACROD) to discuss problems associated with the use by the disabled of airports and airline services. The agenda covered among other issues, classification of people with disabilities, certificate of fitness for regular air travellers, use of personal wheelchairs on aircraft, use of Row Number One seats in aircraft, adequacy of airport wheelchairs, modification of new aircraft, staff training for the disabled, and responsibility for terminal facilities. As a consequence of the meeting Departmental officers will act as liaison officers between the airlines and ACROD to discuss the issues raised.
The Department is also preparing a brochure to inform the disabled of facilities and services available to them at all major airports.
The Office of Road Safety is undertaking a research project on the restraint of the disabled in motor vehicles; the aim of the project being to increase the use of suitable occupant restraints by disabled persons through simple design improvements and propagation of information on 'best practise'. A guidelines booklet may be produced for circulation to hospitals, organisations for the disabled, and parents.
(ii) Programs focusing on the needs of the disabled but not specifically initiated for IYDP:
Under the Transport Planning and Research program, the Commonwealth has provided financial support to the States for a number of projects related to transport and the disabled. Between 1975 and 1981, Commonwealth support to the States for such projects, which is provided on a dollar for dollar basis, has amounted to almost $100,000.
New airport design and construction incorporates the requirements of the Australian Standard 1428 of 1977, 'Design Rules for Access by the Disabled'.
The Office of Road Safety has a continuing responsibility in accident and disability prevention and maintains a public awareness and education program.
There have been no special arrangements made to assist disabled persons employed in the Department.
(2) (a) Special arrangements made to increase employment of disabled persons in the Department:
The Department co-operates with the Public Service Board through its Special Placements Scheme. In 1980, six officers-three permanent and three temporary-were placed in Central Office, Canberra, under the program and a further three are employed in the New South Wales RegionalOffice.

460 REPRESENTATIVES 19 August 1981

Answers to Questions

Victoria/Tasmania Regional Office is participating in a survey initiated by the Regional Director's Office of the Public Service Board, to identify base grade clerical/administrative and keyboard positions which could be suitably staffed by the disabled.
Since August 1980 a permanent officer, Clerk Class 8, has been given duties covering the Equal Employment Opportunity (EEO) area. It is foreshadowed that policy recommendations espousing the EEO philosophy will emanate from this area and have a significant input in to recruitment, selection and personnel development procedures. The employment of the disabled and their needs as employees will come into this area.
An .ancillary function of the Departmental EEO officer has been to co-ordinate activities relating to IYDP. One significant project being planned is an education program directed at staff with the purpose of sensitising staff to an awareness of the disabled, their rights as individuals and citizens, and the causes and prevention of disablement.
(2) (b) Improved access for disabled persons:
The Department is aware of the needs of the disabled in their use of facilities for which it is responsible. The example given of Departmental adherence to the Australian Standard 1428 of 1977, 'Design Rules for Access by the Disabled', in the construction of airports has in recent years increased access for the disabled in their use of air transport.
The Department has notified airlines that certain procedures and training in respect to the carriage of handicapped persons are to be adopted. The procedures were introduced from 30 September 1979 and Air Navigation Orders, Sections 20.1 I and 20.16 are being amended accordingly.
(3) No budgetary allocations were made in 1980-81 to facilitate any special arrangements for disabled persons working in the Department.
All projects and programs undertaken either specifically for IYDP, or in consideration to the general needs of the disabled, have been funded within the normal budgetary allocations of the particular Division or Region involved.

(Question No. 709)

Dr Everingham asked the Prime Minister, upon notice, on 4 March 1981:
(I) Was it stated in a spe~ch delivered on his behaif in December 1980 that political self-interest requires developed countries to work energetically to solve the problems of north-south relations and that the humanitarian case based on terrible sulfering and degradation in the Third World is unanswerable.

Answers to Questio~'t

(2) Is it a fact that (a) Australia's import controls are growing and (b) there has been a declining proportion of gross domestic product (GDP) allocated by his Government to foreign aid for Third World suffering; if so, does this accord with his expressed view.
(3) Can he say whether Aboriginal communities in Australia are suffering degradation comparable to those of the Third World.
(4) Has there been a decline in real value and a decline in the share in total or per capita of GDP allocated to Aborigines and other Australians living below various poverty lines; if so, has this added to the suffering and degradation of Aboriginal communities in Australia.
(5) Is an increasing share of GDP being taken by large undertakings along with increased government allowances, exemptions and other financial incentives to them.
(6) Are levels of tax avoidance and evasion growing.
(7) Is a record proportion of Australia's GDP being expatriated to service capital investment and to cover transfer pricing arrangements and invisible imports such as freight and insurance paid to associates of foreign-controlled companies in Australia.
(8) Are Aborigines in Queensland and Western Australia suffering increasing encroachments in their land and are they un~ble to achieve equal payor voting rights through legal actIOn.

(9) See (5) to (8) above.
(10) Not explicitly.
Conservation of Endangered Species on Christmas Island
(Question No. 717)
Mr Barry Jones asked the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment, upon notice, on 5 March 1981:
What action has the Government taken on each of the 23 recommendations contained in the report of the House of Representatives Standing Committee on Environment and Conservation on the conservation of endangered species on Christmas Island presented to the House on 24 October 1974 (Hansard, page 2838).
Mr Wilson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
My Department has provided the following information in consulation with the Department of Administrative Services and the Australian National Parks and Wildlife Service, on the position as at June 1981.
(1) Fields 22A and 22B remain untouched and the sensitive areas of Field 22B and parts of Murray Hill have been included in a National Park which was declared in 1980. The remainder of Field 22B is not planned for cle'aring.
Any clearing in Field 22A is still subject to the outcome of any decisions which may flow from a detailed expert panel appraisal of a survey (1979-80) of Abbotts Booby. Decisions on the expert panel's recommendations are expected before a moratorium which has been placed on the clearing of Field 22A and certain other fields expires on 31 August 1981.
Sections of Fields 22c, 23A and 23B were selectively cleared between November 1976 and September 1978 in line with plans and procedures agreed between representatives of Government and the British Phosphate Commissioners (BPC) at the time. Fields 22c and 23B are now part of computer block area 22c with respect to which the expert panel has made re,commendations.
(2) This sllggested procedure was generally instituted and sections of Fields 26 and 27 were selectively cleared in accordance with plans mutually approved by the parties (on the advice on the Government Conservator and BPC Conservation Officer) commencing July 1977. These areas are quite large, and judicious and approved clearing in sections of these fields has continued from time to time except during relevant moratoriums. The recent expert panel report has confirmed and recommended that clearing in these two fields should be permitted to proceed according to agreed plans.
(3) Clearing is undertaken between late November and early March to the extent that this is practicable.
(4) An inquiry into the long-term future of the island is currently being undertaken, on behalf of the Government, by Mr W. W. Sweetland. The inquiry will, inter alia, examine and report on the resources of Christmas Island and on appropriate machinery for the planning and control of the use of resources, particularly land.
(5) and (6) Mining guidelines have now been in use for a number of years. An Australian National Parks and Wildlife Service (ANPWS) officer has been appointed to

(1) Is he able to say whether (a) Canadian Pacific Airlines Ltd arranged for advertising in Australia between 1965 and 1979, the payment for which was made by way of contra account credit notes and (b) these contra account credit

464 REPRESENTATIVES 19 August 1981

Answers to Questions

notes were issued under agreements made between Canadian Pacific Airlines Ltd and media organisations which provided that the airline would honour the contra account credit notes and permit travel as arranged by the media organisations up to the value of the credit notes.
(2) If so, were these arrangements approved by his Department under the appropriate Air Navigation Regulations.
(3) If the arrangements were approved by his Department, what were the circumstances of this approval.
(4) If the arrangements were not approved by his Department, why were they not approved.
(5) Is he also able to say whether persons have travel1ed on tickets in the manner referred to in part (1) who have no connection with Canadian Pacific Airlines Ltd or the relevant media organisations, by exchanging cash payments or goods and services for the tickets obtained by the use of contra account credit notes; if so, is this practice proscribed by the Air Navigation Regulations.
(6) Has his attention been drawn to allegations concerning this practice and that some media organisations still hold contra account credit notes issued by Canadian Pacific Airlines Ltd; if so, has he taken action in relation to this matter; if he has taken action, what action has he taken.
(7) Has he refused to instruct his Department to investigate the actions of Canadian Pacific Airlines Ltd in these matters.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) (a) and (b) My Department commenced recording contra arrangements, entered into by licensed carriers, on 3 June 1977. The first contra lodged by Canadian Pacific Airlines Ltd involving a media organisation, of which I am advised, was dated 13 October 1977. Further contra arrangements involving a media organisation were lodged by the same company on 31 January 1978 and 17 October 1979.
(2) Formal approvals are not given in individual instances.
(3) Not applicable.
(4) The contras were lodged with the Department and processed in accordance with procedures set down by the Department on 2 June 1977 and 3 January 1978 and notified to all international carriers.
(5) I am not able to say whether such travel has taken place.
(6) and (7) Certain allegations were made in representations received by myself and my Department. I am advised there is no basis for -action under the Air Navigation Regulations, as the statutory time period within which action could be initiated had expired before the matters were brought to attention.

Office of Women's Affairs
(Question No. 833)
Dr Theophanous asked the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment, upon notice, on 12 March 1981:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to a report by Anne Summers in the National Times of 1-7 March 1981, entitled Can Women's Affairs survive the Government's razor gang? in which it is claimed that the Committee of Review of Commonwealth Functions is considering abolishing the Office of Women's Affairs.
(2) Can he give a categorical assurance that the Government is committed to the continuance of the Office of Women's Affairs.
(3) Will he consider upgrading the authority of this Office, in view of the many-faceted needs of women in the community which are still to be met.
Mr Wilson-The answer to the honourable members question is as follows:
(1) to (3) I refer the honourable member to the Prime Minister's statement to the Parliament on Thursday, 30 April on the Review of Commonwealth Functions where it was stated that the functions of the Office of Women's Affairs in the Department of Home Affairs and Environment and the Women's Bureau in the Department of Employment and Youth Affairs were to be continued.
I would like to reassure the honourable member that the Government is fully committed to its policy of equality of opportunity, freedom from discrimination and equal status for women and that the Office of Women's Affairs will continue to monitor, co-ordinate and develop Commonwealth policies and programs to ensure that activites accord with the Government's objectives in women's affairs.

(Question No. 850)
Mr Uren asked the Minister for Foreign Affairs, upon notice, on 24 March 1981:
Further to the answer to my question No. 5472 (Hansard 22 May 1980, page 3211) has the Chief Justice been advised that he should not again use a diplomatic passport if he visits South Africa.
Mr Street-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
No. The Chief Justice, Sir Harry Gibbs, is entitled to a diplomatic passport in respect of all overseas travel under guidelines established by successive governments.

Mr Newman-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
Any person who is authorised to use car transport at Commonwealth expense may specifically request the provision of a Commonwealth car with driver. Whether such a request is conveyed to the booking office of my Department's Transport

and Storage Division is at the discretion of the ordering authority.

The entitlements to use car transport at Commonwealth expense are as determined by the Remuneration Tribunal, the Prime Minister, the Minister for Administrative Services, and by specific Government decisions.

Cost of Unemployment

(Question No. 914)

Mr Willis asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 26 March 1981:

What is the estimated cost to the Treasury in 1981 of an in-

crease of 10,000 in the number of persons out of work, taking

account of revenue lost, benefits paid and administrative

costs.

'

Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

[t is not possible to assess with any precision the net effect
on Commonwealth outlays and receipts of a hypothetical increase of 10,000 in the number of persons looking for work. Any such calculation would require a large number of
assumptions to be made, the nature of which would bear heavily on the figuring.

On the receipts side, the outcome would be inlluenced by
the causes that brought about the rise in the number looking for work. IF, for example, more people were entering the labour force and were looking for work because employment was expanding strongly (as it now is) then a rise in unem-
ployment could occur without any adverse effect on taxation receipts. ,On the other hand, a rise in unemployment
occasioned by a downturn in particular sectors of the economy could be associated with lower budget receipts.

rhe effect on expenditures also' would be uncertain and dependent on underlying assumptions. To illustrate, most married women looking for work, and people looking for part
time work, are not eligible for unemployment benefits, and

the payments received by beneficiaries are related to their age, the number of dependants and the extent of other income. Moreover, some unemployed persons eligible for benefits do not apply. Without knowing the characteristics of the unemployed in question, it would not be possible to estimate the benefits that might be paid. An indication of the average annual cost per unemployment benefit recipient can be gained from the 1980-81 Budget estimates,which provided for total unemployment benefit payments of $959 million on the basis of an average number of beneficiaries of 300,000 (that is, an average annual cost of about $3,200),
The number of unemployment benefit recipients varies considerably over the year and a small change in the number of recipients during the year would be unlikely to have a major impact on administrative costs.
Training of Tradesmen
(Question No. 965)
Mr Hawke asked the Minister for Employment and Youth Affairs, upon notice, on 31 March 1981:
(I) What are the current numbers of tradesmen being trained by major resource developers in those States where the Government has entered into agreements with State Governments to urge project developers to undertake substantial training efforts. '
(2) In what (a) States, (b) regions and (c) trades is the training taking place.
(3) What assistance (a) has been afforded or (b) is planned, through Commonwealth and State manpower programs.
(4) What is the level of demand For tradesmen likely to be generated in the next 2 to 3 years on the basis of the early assessments of manpower requirements by project dev~lopers.
(5) Is there any intention to involve relevant trade uRions as well as the resource companies in the liaison and cooperation process with the State and Commonwealth Governments.
Mr N. A. Brown-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) and (2) It is not possible to provide the level of detail sought. However, preliminary data provided by State and Territory apprenticeship authorities indicates that the level of training in the key metal, electrical and building trades is increasing significantly in those States most directly affected by major development projects. In New South Wales, Queensland and Western Australia total apprentice intakes in those trades in the period I July 1980-31 March 1981, were up 20 per cent, 35 per cent and 26 per cent respectively' on the corresponding period a year earlier.
(3) It is estimated that $65m will be spent in 1980-81 under the Commonwealth Rebate For Apprentice Full-Time (CRAFT) scheme. In addition, special assistance has been extended to support the Commonwealth/State trade training scheme in Western Australia and for the training of apprentice draughtsmen in New South Wales. Offers of special assistance have been extended to all States for the development of trade training programs directed at accelerated training for skills in short supply.
(4) InFormation on the likely level of demand for tradesmen generated by major development projects was detailed in the report of the DOLAC Working Party, 'Prospective Demand for and Supply of Skilled Labour 1980-83 with Particular ReFerence to Major Development Projects' published by my Department last September. The main conclusion drawn at that time was that major developments would add an

Answers to Questions

annual demand for a further 7,000 metal, electrical and building tradesmen over each of the three years examined.
(5) Yes.
Special Youth Employment Training Program
(Question No. 967)
Mr Hawke asked the Minister for Employment and Youth Affairs, upon notice, on 31 March 1981:
(I) On the basis of evaluative data available within his Department what proportion of Special Youth Employment Training Program (SYETP) trainees are retained by their SYETP employers.
(2) What proportion of SYETP trainees have obtained alternative stable employment after completing their SYETP training.
(3) What is the incidence of job substitution between SYETP trainees and other potential employees.
(4) What is the rate of dropout from the SYETP scheme, and what are the main reasons involved.
(5) What is the degree of improved employability as the result of SYETP training.
Mr N. A. BrownThe answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The latest" evaluation report of SYETP trainees is dated June 1980. The survey results in this report show that 50.7 per cent of trainees were still employed with the employer providing training some 4-5 months after the end of the subsidy period.
(2) The same report shows that 17.8 per cent of trainees found full-time employment elsewhere, and 2 per cent found part-time employment.
(3) One third of placements were assessed as net additional positions.
(4) The data shows that 33.9 per cent of trainees do not complete training. Most of these were dismissed but others left of their own accord, either because of dissatisfaction with the job, or to go to another job.
(5) The same study included employer assessments of trainees at the beginning and at the end of the 17 week period. These two separate assessments related to job skills, the need for supervision, and the willingness to help. When compared the assessments indicated 63.5 per cent improved in job skills; 51.3 per cent improved in the need for supervision and 18.5 per cent improved in willingness to help. Those still with the training employer or employed elsewhere included 62.7 per cent of trainees who had been assessed as less than satisfactory on commencement.

Service Personnel: Overseas Stationing
(Question No. 976)
Mr Holding asked the Minister. for Defence, upon notice, on 31 March 1981:
(I) How many Australian service personnel, both military and civilian were stationed overseas as at (a) 30 January 1980, (b) 30 July 1980and (c) 30 January 1981.
(2) Where were those personnel stationed, what services did they represent, what was the reason for stationing each unit in that particular location and what joint or unilateral exercises did each unit participate in while stationed there.
Mr Killen-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) (a) (b) (c) 2,665 servicemen, 94 civilians; 2,575 servicemen, 91 civilians, 2,796 servicemen, 100 civilians.
In addition, at each date, an average of approximately 300 members of the Royal Australian Navy were deployed on ships outside Australia, and an average of 54 school teachers' seconded from State Education Departments were employed in service sponsored schools in Malaysia and Singapore. .-
(2) The following tables show the locations, service affiliations and reasons for stationing personnel overseas at each date. Approximately one half of all Defence personnel overseas were stationed in Malaysia and Singapore on unit duties to fulfil Australia's obligation to the Five Power Arrangement. Other activities involving Defence personnel overseas included defence co-operation, representing at Australia's overseas missions, training and exchange with other Defence forces and Departments, communications related duties, materiel project work and United Nations commitments.
Participation by Units in exercises would have been limited to the Butterworth-based RAAF Units which took part in the following exercises during the period of31 January 1980 to 31 January 1981:
MAJORADEX 1/80-17-20 March 1980 MINOR ADEX 2/80-11 June 1980. MINOR ADEX 3/80-18 June 1980. MAJOR ADEX 4/80-6-9 October 1980 MINOR ADEX 5/80-3 December 1980 MINOR ADEX 6/80-10 December 1980.
Each of these exercises were Air Defence Exercises conducted and controlled by the Integrated Air Defence System established under the Five Power Arrangement.
Individual servicemen on exchange normally participate in the exercises of the units to which they are attached. Com" p1ete and accurate records of all such exercise participation are not maintained by the Service Offices.

Aluminium Processing
(Question No. 977)
Mr Howe asked the Minister representing the Minister for National Development and Energy, upon notice, on 31 March 1981:
(1) Is an expansion proposed of the aluminium industry in Australia from current levels of production of 283 000 tonnes per annum to 1.2 million tonnes by 1985.
(2) If so, what investigations have been carried out to assess the appropriateness of the conventional Hall/Heroult process to Australian circumstances.
(3) Has this process been in use since the 1880s without substantial modification.
(4) Does the process require the consumption of up to 17000 kilowatt hours of electricity per tonne of aluminium.
(5) Are there serious pollution problems associated with the Hall/Heroult process, particularly with the production of gaseous flourides.
(6) Has the Minister or the Department of National Development and Energy investigated alternative processes involving direct ore reduction, e.g. the production of Al alloy with 13 per cent Si which is apparently used for industrial production in the United States of America.
(7) Is the Minister able to say whether Alcoa was granted, by the American Research and Development Administration, a credit for the manufacture of AISi master alloys by oxygen blast furnace without electrical heating.
(8) Has the Minister or the Department of National Development and Energy investigated other new processes such as (a) the condensation of aluminium vapour, (b) carbothermic reduction of aluminium and (c) electrolysis of aluminium chloride.
(9) Has the minister or the Department of National Development and Energy investigated possible improvements in the Hall/Heroult process which might result in a substantial reduction in energy use or the emission of flourides.
(10) In view of the dramatic increase in aluminium production in Australia, has the Minister taken any steps to ensure that Australia has access to the latest technology and the technology which is most appropriate to Australia's circumstances.
Mr Anthony-The Minister for National Development and Energy has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:

(I) Expansion of the Australian aluminium industry of that order is proposed. It is expected that existing annual production capacity of around 280000 tonnes will rise to around 1.4 million tonnes by 1986.
(2) The adoption of a particular production process by a company remains, in Australia, the outcome of normal commercial considerations. Of course, GovernmentsCommonwealth, State and local-affect such considerations; for example, through their setting of environmental standards.
(3) It is true that the Hall/Heroult process was first developed in the l880s. It was the development of this process which facilitated the commerical production of aluminium. The process has, of course, been developed and refined over the last 90 odd years. The smelters it is proposed to construct in Australia will take advantage of recently developed technology relating to pot design, anode manufacture, control of airborne emissions from the potline, and reduced electricity consumption in the smelting process.
(4) The potlines in older smelters do use about 17 000 kilowatt hours of electricity per tonne of primary aluminium produced. However, as indicated in (3) above, the smelters it is proposed to construct in Australia will be taking advantage of the latest commercially available technology. Accordingly, it is estimated that the electricity requirement for the proposed smelters will be between 14-15000 kWh per tonne.
(5) As is the case with many industrial processes, there are environmental side effects associated with thejimelting of aluminium using the Hall/Heroult process. Advances in smelter design and technology have generally reduced such side effects, including the emission of gaseous 1I0urides, to acceptable levels. Nevertheless, before proceeding, each project is required to satisfy the provisions of environment protection legislation of the particular State in which it is to be located. Aluminium smelter proposals requiring Commonwealth decisions are also assessed in accordance with the provisions of the Environment (Impact of Proposals) Act.
(6) and (8) The Department of National Development and Energy is aware of reserach being undertaken into alternative processes to the Hall/Heroult process. The alternative processes referred to are still all in their developmental stages and it will be some time befo're any of these alternative processes attain commerical viability if, indeed, any do prove to be suitable for application on a large-scale.
(7) In 1977, Alcoa (US) entered a contract with the United States Department of Energy to investigate the technical feasibility of reduction of alumina-silica ores, based on combustion heating. Again, the success or otherwise of this long term project remains to be determined.

470 REPRESENTATIVES 19 August 1981

Answers to Questions

(9) As indicated in (3), (4) and (5) above, the smelters to be built in Australia will be utiising the most advanced commercially available technology.
