Air Navigation Charges
(Question No. 979)
Mr Morris asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 1 April 1981 :
Has his attention been drawn to repeated claims by operators that air navigation charges For international flights in and out of Australian airports are the most expensive in the world; iF so, is he able to Furnish details of the airport charges on international flights including all landing Fees, customs charges, holding room charges, passenger head charges, airport staff overtime charges, etc. on (a) DC8, (b) DCIO, (c) Boeing 747, (d) Boeing 747 SP, (e) Airbus A300, (F) Boeing 737 and (g) Boeing 727-200 series aircraft, at (i) Heathrow and (ii) Gatwick Airports, United Kingdom, (iii) FrankFurt and (iv) Munich Airports, Federal Republic of Germany, (v) Orly Airport, France, (vi) Tokyo Airport, Japan, (vii) Nadi Airport, Fiji, (viii) Auckland and (ix) Christchurch Airports, New Zealand; (x) Los Angeles, (xi) San Francisco, (xii) New York and (xiii) Honolulu Airports, United States of America and (xiv) Ontario and (xv) Vancouver Airports, Canada.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
My attention has been drawn to these claims. However such claims are normally based on the erroneous assumption that the landing charges imposed overseas equate with the Australian air navigation charge.
Australian air navigation charges, unlike most overseas countries, are a composite Fee covering landing Fees, parking Fees, terminal air traffic control and en-route aids and services. New Zealand has a similar single charge to Australia.
In overseas countries an aircraft normally pays:
airport charges which can include landing Fees, customs charges, security Fees, passenger head. charges etc (some of these charges can attract peak and off peak rates)
terminal area control Fees which are sometimes included under airport charges

an en-route navigational aids and service Fees.
As shown by the Following table, the single Australian air navigation charge compares Favourably with charges levied For operations to the UK. The table is based on a Qantas QFI flight Sydney to Heathrow by a Boeing 747-200 at 60 per cent capacity. It compares charges For operations out of Sydney and Australian en-route Facilities to charges For en-route Facilities For a similar distance From Heathrow (ie, approximately 3750 kms including charges For en-route Facilities of Syria, Cyprus, Greece, Euro-Control, and Yugoslavia) and Heathrow airport charges. The charges For operations into Heathrow were provided by Qantas.

2,4,5-T
(Question No. 985)
Mr Howe asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 1 April 1981:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to the research carried out by Dr Peter Hall and Dr Ben Selinger of t.he Department of Chemistry at the Australian National University on the chemical 2,4,5.T.
(2) What is his assessment of the researchers' hypothesis that substantial amounts of 2,4,5.T, imported in 1969-70 may have been purchased cheaply in Singapore and come from Vietnam.

(3) Will he assess the researchers' claim that there may be a correlation between an increase in deaths of young babies from certain types of congenital abnormalities and low level exposure of large populations to herbicides with high TCDD (dioxin) levels.
(4) What are the implications of these findings for the study of Agent Orange being carried out by the Commonwealth Institute of Health.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) My Department has no knowledge that 'Agent Orange' or 2,4,5.T from Vietnam has ever been imported into

Answers to Questions

19 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 473

Australia through Singapore or elsewhere. According to a report of the Tariff Board of 12 April 1973, imports of material used in herbicide manufacture, namely free 2,4,5trichlorophenoxyacetic acid, 2,4,5-trichlorophenol and its potassium salt (KTCP) occurred in the late 1960's and early 1970's. These materials appeared to have originated in Singapore, the United States of America, Japan and Britain.
(3) Dr Hall and Dr Selinger have claimed that there has been an increase in congenital abnormalities of the central nervous system in the years 1968-77 but the statistical significance of their claim is debatable. At the same time, they have also recognised that there is no clear evidence to link this alleged increase with the use of phenoxyacetic acid herbicides.
I am not aware of evidence indicating that low level exposure of large populations to herbicides containing high TCDD (dioxin) levels has ever occurred in Australia.
(4) The Commonwealth Institute of Health is investigating the possibility that the health of Vietnam veterans has been adversely affected by those chemicals used in Vietnam, including 'Agent Orange'.
It has always been recognised by the investigators at the Institute that in their studies the possible effects of exposure to agricultural chemicals will need to be assessed and evaluated in relation to the control group as well as the veterans to ensure that a meaningful comparison of exposed and unexposed persons is made.
Officers involved in the Commonwealth Institute of Health study are aware of the Hall-Selinger results.

Medical Practitioners' Records
(Question No. 1032)
Mr Groom asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 2 April 1981:
(I) Have officers of his Department entered the professional rooms of any medical practitioner and taken possession of records or other written material containing the names and other personal information relating to patients during the last 5 years.
(2) If so, how many medical practitioners have been involved.
(3) If he cannot provide the precise number of medical practitioners referred to in part (2) will he give an estimate of the number of medical practitioners involved.
(4) Have the records or material, or part of these, been retained in some instances for a period of 3 months or longer without formal charges being laid.
(5) What is the longest period that records or material have been held without formal charges being laid.
(6) How many patients of medical practitioners have been interviewed by officers of his Department in connection with the investigation of medical practitioners during the. last 5 years.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) and (2) Since the responsibility for monitoring Commonwealth Medical Benefits reverted to my Department on I November 1978, on twelve occasions (involving 16 medical practitioners) officers of my Department have been associated with the removal of records or other written material from professional rooms of medical practitioners.
Of these twelve occurrences there were eleven occasions when Departmental officers accompanied officers of the Australian Federal Police who were in possession of search warrants and who took possession of records or other written material.
In the other instance the medical practitioner voluntarily gave documents to a Departmental officer during the course of an interview. The doctor also delivered other documents to my Department.
(3) See (I) and (2).
(4) and (5) Yes; insofar as my Department is concerned, in only one instance. A medical practitioner offered copies of various documents. These were retained for six months before formal charges were laid.
(6) At least 4000 patients have been interviewed, if not substantially more. The exact number is not known as detailed statistics of the number of patients interviewed durin~ initial evaluation of cases are not kept in all States.
Aircraft Noise
(Question No.10S1)
Mrs Darling asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 2 April 1981 :
(I) Further to question No. 1029 asked by the Member for St George in the Notice Paper of Wednesday, 1 April 1981, (a) on which runways at the Eagle Farm Airport, Brisbane, Queensland, is an instrument landing system operative, (b) what is the glide slope on each runway served by the ILS and (c) what procedures have been introduced to ensure that the angle of the glide path is observed by pilots (i) when using the ILS and (ii) when using a visual approach.

474 REPRESENTATIVES 19 August 1981

Answers to Questions

Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) (a) The instrument landing system operates on runway 22 for landings over Moreton Bay.
(b) The glide slope is 3 degrees.
(c) There is no external monitoring of the actual glide path flown.
Pilot training in aircraft and aircraft simulators stresses the need to maintain stabilised approach on the electronic glide path of the ILS and on the T-Visual Approach Slope Indicator when making a visual approach. Indeed the whole landing procedure is based on the pilot consistently following this slope to the runway aiming point. A system of cockpit drills specifically alerts the pilot operating the controls if he allows the aircraft to deviate more than a stipulated tolerance below the glide path.

Mr Newman-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

Depo-Provera
(Question No. 1062)
Mr Campbell asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 2 April 1981:
(I) Is there evidence of the widespread prescription of the contraceptive drug, Depo-Provera, for Aboriginals and other groups of deprived women; if so, has the drug been approved limited use.
(2) If this drug has been approved for limited use, what is meant by this term.
(3) Is he satisfied that there are sufficient guarantees of the drug being absolutely safe.
(4) Does the approval for its limited use make adequate provision for ensuring the informed consent of patients using

this drug and, in particular, the informed consent of the many Aboriginal women for whom it is at present being prescribed.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) My Department only has access to prescribing data in relation to the Pharmaceutical Benefits Scheme (see (2) below). Data on use in hospitals and private prescribing data would only be available from the manufacturer. It should be noted that it is incorrect to refer to Depo-Provera as a contraceptive drug in the Australian situation where it has not been approved for this indication.
(2) Depo-Provera is available for use in Australia in the treatment of a number of indications including malignant neoplasia and precocious puberty and its availability as a Pharmaceutical Benefit is limited to these latter two indications. The use of Depo-Provera for contraceptive purposes would be limited to clinical investigational use only. To date, no application has been received by my Department in this regard. An application for its use as a contraceptive was rejected by the Australian Drug Evaluation Committee (ADEC) as there were unresolved questions relating to its safety for this indication.
(3) No potent therapeutic agent, such as Depo-Provera can be guaranteed as being absolutely safe. The product information for Depo-Provera distributed by the company contains the following statement:
"The use of Depo-Provera for contraception is not an approved indication and such use is investigational since there are unresolved questions relating to its safety for this indication" .
(4) The ADEC supports the concept of properly controlled clinical trials using Depo-Provera as a contraceptive provided they are designed to help resolve some of the questions raised and provided adequate measures are incorporated in the trial protocol to satisfactorily protect the participants. As with any drug which is marketed in Australia a doctor may decide that special circumstances warrant the use of the product for conditions other than those for which it has been accepted. In such cases the responsibility for adequately informing the patient or guardian and for any adverse consequences of the therapy must rest with the practitioner concerned.
Road Construction
(Question No. 1083)
Mr Morris asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 7 April 1981 :
(I) What studies have been undertaken or are proposed to be undertaken by the Bureau of Transport Economics or his Department into the comparative costs of road construction by private contractors and by Commonwealth, State and local government day labour.
(2) What are substantial quantitative comparisons of private and public road construction, over similar roads in Australian conditions.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) and (2) No studies have been undertaken which would enable firm conclusions to be drawn about the comparative costs of the two methods oflonstructing roads in Australian conditions. Because of the ~ifficulties involved in attempting an objective assessment, there al'd'no proposals at present by the Bureau of Transport Economics or by my Department to undertake a specific study to obtain comparative cost data.
However, the relative cost efficiency of these methods of undertaking roadworks would be established on a project by

Answers to Questions

19 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 475

project basis where day labour and private contractors are free to compete by tender for the work. The Government's decision to make grants to the States and the Northern Territory for National Roads construction in the Road Grants Bill 1981 conditional upon the application of tender arrangements will enable cost comparisons to be obtained for particular works on these roads. I intend to report the details to the Parliament when sufficient information becomes available.

Commonwealth Payment-of Air Tickets to and from Canberra
(Question No. 1120)
Mrs Darling asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 7 April 1981:
(I) What proportion of the total volume of air passengers travelling to and from Canberra with (a) TAA, and (b) Ansell in (i) 1977-78 (ii) 1978-79, (iii) 1979-80, and (iv) from I July 1980 to 31 March 1981, comprised persons whose

passage was paid by a Department or Statutory Authority of the Commonwealth Government.
(2) If the proportion of total tickets to and from Canberra paid for directly or indirectly by the Government is substantial, is an increase by the Government in the cost of tickets for air passage to and from Canberra for T AA and Ansell passengers tantamount to an indirect Government subsidy to those airlines.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) The gathering of information of the kind requested by the honourable member would require the commitment of considerable resources which I do not feel could reasonably be devoted to this task.
(2) The increase in fares to and from Canberra resulted from the Government's decision to adopt a new cost based, nationally consistent air fare formula to meet the thrust of the recommendations of the Independent Public Inquiry into Domestic Air Fares. I announced the Government's initial response to the Inquiry's Report and the revised air fare formula in Parliament on I April 1981.
Fare increases resulting from the adoption of the new formula on I April are an adjustment to reduce the extent of cross-subsidisation from passengers travelling on long distance routes to those on short distance routes as identified in the Holcroft Report.

Export of Minerals Sands
(Question No. 1170)
Mr West asked the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment, upon notice, on 8 April 1981:
(I) Has Moreton Island, Queen~land, been placed on the Register of the National Estate; if so, when was this done.
(2) Is he obliged under section 30 of the Australian Heritage Commission Act 1975 to refuse all applications for exports of mineral sands from areas listed on the National Estate.

Mr Wilson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows;
(I) Moreton Island was proposed for listing in the Register of the National Estate in the Commonwealth Gazette G2 of 17 January 1978. Objections were received to this listing and

Answers to Questions

19 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 477

following evaluation of these objections the Commission has made a decision to enter on the Register the whole of Moreton Island with the exception of the gazetted townships of Bulwer, Cowan Cowan, Tangalooma and Kooringal.
Moreton Island with the exc~ptiOh of these gazetted townships wiIl be officially advertised for entry in the Register in the early part of the 1981-82 financial year.
By virtue of section 31 of the Australian Heritage Commission Act 1975, the place, having been listed, has the same protection as it would have if it was on the Register.
(2) No. Neither I, as Minister, nor the Australian Heritage Commission, has the power to refuse any such application under Section 30 of the Australian Heritage Commission Act.
In respect of the export of mineral sands from Moreton Island Section 30 of the Act would require that Commonwealth Ministers or Authorities responsible for approving licenses for the export of mineral sands from Moreton Island shall not take any action that adversley affects the national estate value of the parts of Moreton Island proposed for the Regi~ter of the National Estate, uniess they are satisfied there is no feasible or prudent alternative, unless all reasonable measures are taken to minimise the adverse effect and un bless the Australian Heritage Commission is informed of the proposed action and given time to comment.
Subsidy of Air Travel for Inland Queensland Routes
(Question No. 1192)
Mrs Darling asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 8 April 1981:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to reports in the Press of a call by the Queensland Young Nationals for Commonwealth Government subsidy of air travel for inland Queensland routes; if so, wiIl he introduce this direct subsidy.
(2) Did the President of the Young Nationals call for National Party Members to adopt an aggressive stance on this issue; if so, will he reply aggressively to the President of the Young Nationals.
(3) Did the President of the YoungNationals call for a subsidy to rectify the anomaly where inland travellers were paying a 45 cent loading above that charged.on jet routes.
(4) Is an economy fare for air travel from Brisbane to Rockhampton, Queenslan~, approximately $35 less than for travel of an equivalent distance between Brisbane and Emerald, Queensland; if so, what is the reason for this apparent anomaly.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) to (3) The Government is aware of the keen interest of certain communities in inland Queensland air services. The new Airlines Agreement provides for an orderly withdrawal from uneconomic services it it is established that over a six month period the airline is not able to fully recover costs. In that event the airline will be able to give three months notice of termination. The new Agreement ensures stability 9f service by providing for notice of withdrawal and consultations on replacement operators. In circumstances where an airline serves notice that it proposes to withdraw from a route the Governments would have at least 3 months to consider whether arrangements to maintain the service, through a direct subsidy, were appropriate.
(4) The one-way economy fare. between Brisbane and Emerald (653 km) is $1 10.30 on a BPA Metroliner aircraft. The one-way economy fare between Brisbane and Rockhampton (530 km) is $75.60 on a TAAjet aircraft. The fare differential

is a consequence of different aircraft operators using different aircraft types and the distances involved.
Petrol Subsidy
(Question No. 1193)
Mrs Darling asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 8 April 1981 :
(I) What are the guidelines which apply to the payment of a petrol subsidy to consumer or traders in rural areas.
(2) Has his attention been drawn to claims that some oil companies are encouraging private carriers, or jobbers, to attract this subsidy by transporting fuel beyond city limits by claiming a rural destination, then returning to sell their supplies to city service station operators at lowered prices.
(3) Is he able to say whether any of the following trucking companies operate in such a way: (a) Tartan Transport (Moorooka), (b) Petroleum Products Pty Ltd (Moorooka), (c) Centenary Fuel Supplies (Jindalee), (d) Professional Products P/L (Eight Mile Plains), (e) Independent Fuel Supplies (Rocklea), (f) Teraco (Coopers Plains), (g) South Coast Oils Pty Ltd (Labrador), (h) Gold Coast Oils Pty Ltd (South Tweed Heads) and (j) Pottsville Trucking (Pottsville) .
(4) What consequences arise from this practice in relation to (a) safety standards governing the syphoning of fuel from underground tanks and (b) the purity and grade of petrol sold to consumers.
(5) Will he consult with his colleagues on the Australian Transport Advisory Council to determine a concerted approach to end this practice.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Under the provisions of the Petroleum Products Subsidy Scheme, which is administered by the Department of Business and Consumer Affairs, oil companies and fuel distributors sell fuel to consumers or traders in rural areas at a price that includes no more than 0.44 cents per litre of the delivery cost of the fuel to the customer. Where the transport costs exceed 0.44 cents per litre. the oil company or fuel distributor claims the remainder from the Government in accordance with the Scheme.
(2) and (3) My attention has recently been drawn to nonspecific allegations of this nature. In relation to these the Department of Business and Consumer Affairs has advised that it has found no evidence that these practices occur. I am advised by that Department that all subsidy claims relating to Queensland are checked by the Customs officers in Brisbane. During these examinations the possibility of false invoicing is one of the factors which is checked.
(4) Regulation of fuel handling from underground tanks and fuel quality is primarily the responsibility of Stare and Territory Governments.
(5) As these issues are not generally the responsibility of transport authorities it is not appropriate to refer them to the Australian Transport Advisory Council.
Uranium Mining
(Question No. 1198)
Dr Everingham asked the Minister for Trade and Resources, upon notice, on 8 April 1981:
(1) Has the Minister's attention been drawn to Dr Helen Caldicott's account of her visit to Mary Kathleen Mine in 1976 in her book Nuclear madness (Jacaranda Press, 1979),

478 REPRESENTATIVES 19 August 1981

Answers to Questions

in particular to claims that she found no monitoring of radiation levels between the mine face and the urine of miners despite clouds of dust enveloping workers.
(2) Can he say whether safety standards laid down before 1976 by the Depllrtment of Health were ignored by uranium mining authorities in (a) 1976 and (b) later years.
(3) Will the Minister table copies or provide displays in the Parliamentary Library of any information drawn to the attention of uranium workers in Australia in (a) 1976 and (b) 1980.
Mr Anthony-,-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) The honourable'member will no doubt recall that when he was Minister for Health in 1975, the Code of Practice on Radiatiol1 Protection in the Mining and Milling of Radioactive Ores (1975) was drawn up by his Department. The draft code was distributed to 94 relevant bodies including mining companies, the ACTU, State Trades and Labour Councils and relevant trade unions prior to finalisation. The final code was released in 1976. An improved version of the Code was approvc;d under the Environment Protection (Nuclear Codes) Act as the Code of Practice on Radiation Protection in the Mining and Milling of Radioactive Ores (1980) on 10September 1980 and is lIvailable for incorporation into State and Northern Territory legislation as' required. The Mary Kathleen mine in Queensland was the only Australian uranium mine inoperation in 1976. Responsibility for the regulation and control of all mining operations including the enforcement of radiation protection standards in Queensland, at that time: and now, rests with the State Government. I understand that all uranium mines in Australia which have received Commonwealth Government development approval as well as Mary Kathleen comply with the standards set out in the Code.
0) The Code of Practice on Radiation Protection in the
Mining and Milling of Radioactive Ores (1980) was approved under the Environment Protection (Nuclear Codes) Act 1978 and laid before both Houses of Parliament. A copy is available in the Parliamentary Library. Other relevant information would be available from the responsible State or Northern Territory authority.

Employment of Private Consultants: Department of Business and Consumer Affairs
(Question No. 1278)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs, upon notice, on 9 April 1981 :
Were any private consultants or consultancy firms employed by his Department in 1979-80; if so, (a) how many were employed, (b) who were they, (c) for what purpose was each employed and (d) what payments were made to each consultant or consultancy firm.

Mr Moore-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
During 1979-80 the Department of Business arid Consumer Affairs used the services of eight consultants/consultancy firms at a total cost of $8,066.
Cummins Diesel Sales and Service (Aust) Pty Ltd of Western Australia and Onan Auxiliary Power Unit Agency of Western Australia assisted in the conduct of a Customs Patrol Boat course at a_cost of $500 and $366 respectively.
ANACAPA Sciences Inc of the USA assisted in conducting a narcotics training course, the fee for the course was $2,917.
AWA (A'Asia) Ltd of NSW assisted in the training of a Nomad Observers Course for Radar equipment at a cost of $2,500.
Messrs E. Davis, E. Barbour and P. Hanford were-engaged for a Senior Management Conference at a total cost of$I,783.
Employment of Private Consultants: Department of Home Affairs and Environment
(Question No. 1283)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
Were any private consultants or consultancy firms employed by his Department in 1979-80; ifso, (a) how many were employed, (b) who were they, (c) for what purpose was each employed and (d) what payments were made to each consultant or consultancy firm.
Mr Wilson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
I am advised by my Department:

Answers to Questions

19 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 485

(a ) Yes; three.
(b) (i) Mr D. F. Collins.
(ii) Mr H. G. Houstein.
(iii) Peat, Marwick, Mitchell Services.
(c) (i) To introduce a system of guaranteed land title to Norfolk Island to replace the old systems title.
(ii) To construct and establish an official and technically reliable index suitable for Christmas Island conditions, the purpose of which is to measure average movements in 'retail prices on the Island.
(iii) To undertake a feasibility' study of a National Sports Lottery.
(d) (i) In 1979-80 the total costs incurred for the Norfolk Island Torrens Title consultancy amounted to $4,670. Fees payable to the consultant, Mr D. F. Collins were $3,875 and other consultancy expenses, that is, fares, accommodation and incidentals were $795.
(ii) In 1979-80 the total costs incurred for the consumer price index study on Christmas Island amounted to $11,895. Fees payable to the consultant, Mr H. G. Houstein were $6,210 and other consultancy expen_ses, for example fares, accommodation and incidentals were $5,685.
(iii) In 1979-80 the total costs incurred for the Sports Lottery Feasibility Study amounted to $21,403. Fees payable to the consultancy firm Peat, Marwick, Mitchell Services were $18,644 and other consultancy expenses, for example travel, advertising and incidentals were $2,759.

Recruitment Through Private Employment Agencies: Department of the Treasury
(Question No. 1291
Mr Bungey asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 9 April 1981 :
Were any staff of his Department or statutory authorities under his aegis recruited through private employment agencies in 1979-80; if so, for what position and at what cost.

Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
I. The Reserve Bank of Australia used a private employment agency on one occasion in 1979-80 to recruit a security systems analyst. The fee charged was $2,962.50.
,2. The Australian Industry Development Corporation has advised that it recruited a small number of staff through private employment agencies in 1979-80. However, as the Corporation is a business enterprise operating in the competitive environment of the private sector, it would not be appropriate for commercial confidential details to be publicly divulged.
3. The Treasury, Australian Taxation Office, Australian Bureau of Statistics and the Commonwealth Banking Corporation did not engage private employment agencies in 1979-80.
Recruitment Through Private Employment Agencies: Department of Defence
(Question No. 1293)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
Were any staff of his Department or statutory authorities under his aegis recruited through private employment agencies in 1979-80; if so, for what position and at what cost.
Mr Killen-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) One staff member wasrecruited through a private employment agency in 1979-80.
(2) The position was a Typist Grade 2 and the cost involved was $570.18, that is, 6 per cent of the annual salary of a Typist Grade 2 at that time. This is the standard fee charged by the agency.
Recruitment Through Private Employment Agencies: Department of the Capital Terrihlry
(Question No. 1305)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for the Capital Territory, upon notice, on 9 April 1981.
Were any staff of his Department or statutory authorities under his aegis recruited through private employment agencies in 1979-80; if so, for what position and at what cost.
Mr Hodgman-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
No staff employed in the Department of the Capital Territory were recruited throught private employment agencies in 1979-80. Some industrial staff are recruited through the Commonwealth Employment Service.
The A.C.T. Electricity Authority in [979-80 recruited one Steno-secretary, Grade 2 and one Steno-secretary, Grade I through private employment agencies. The cost was between $200 and $250 per person.
The Parliament House Construction Authority recruited its Executive Officer through a private agency in the same financial year at a cost of $11,500.

Industrial Action: Department oflndustry and Commerce
(Question No. 1364)

Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Industry and 'Commerce, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:

(I) How many officers or employees in his Department have been (a) on strike and (b) engaged in go slow, work to regulations or other restrictive work actions, in each of the last two years.

(2) How many man-hours were involved in these actions.

(3) Were any officers or employees on strike action paid by the Commonwealth during the strike; if so, on what occasions.

(4) Were any pay reductions or disciplinary actions taken

in respect of officers or employees on go slow, work to regu-

lations or other restrictive work actions; if so, on what

occasions.

'

Sir Phillip Lynch-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

(I) (a) In the period April 1979 to March 1980 there were 44 occurrences of strike action involving a total of 9,386 officers and employees.

In the period April 1980 to March 1981 there were 46 occurrences of strike action involving a total of 2,142 officers and employees.

(b) In the period April 1979 to March 1980 764 staff engaged in go slow, work to regulations or restrictive work actions.

In the period April 1980 to March 1981 44 staff engaged in go slow, work to regulations or restrictive work actions.
(2) In the period April 1979 to March 1980 there were 147,788 man-hours lost due' to strike action. From April 1980 to March 1981 there were 30,376 man-hours lost due to strike action.
During the period April 1979 to March 1980 720 staff engaged in a work to regulations situation for one continuous period of 35.4 hours. During the period April 1979 to March 1981 44 technical staff have continued, over the full period, with a refusal to relinquish duties which management considers could be more efficiently performed by another employment category.
(3) No.
(4) No.
Note: The above information includes details in respect of the former Department of Productivity for the period April 1979 to November 1980. Information relating to the functions transferred to the Department of Industry and Commerce from the former Department of Productivity is not separately available, therefore the information provided may include details relating to staff who were transferred to other Departments upon abolition of the Department of Productivity.
Industrial Action: Department of Communications
(Question No. 1365)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Communications, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
(I) How many officers or employees in his Department have been (a) on strike aild (b) engaged in go slow, work to regulations or other restrictive work actions, in each of the last two years.
(2) How many manhours were involved in these actions.
(3) Were any officers or employees on strike action paid by the Commonwealth during the strikes; if so, on what occasions.
(4) Were any pay reductions or disciplinary actions taken in respect of officers or employees on go-slow, work-toregulations or other restrictive work actions; if so, on what occasions.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) The number of officers who have been on strike over the last two years to date is 118. During the same period there have been a number of industrial actions involving some restrictive work actions. The impact of these actions have not warranted the maintenance of records [0 identify specific persons involved. Consequently, it is not possible to provide a meaningful response in terms sought.
(2) During the period in question a total of 1687, manhours were lost due to strike action or the stand down of staff.
(3) No payments were made to any of the officers for the periods in which they were engaged in strike action or were stood down.
(4) All disputes are handled in accordance with the Public Service Board guidelines and accepted industrial practice. None of the disputes in this Department have necessitated the actions suggested.

Financial Directive to Qantas
(Question No. 1689)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 9 April 1981 :
(I) Was a financial directive issued to Qantas by the Government in 1947.
(2) If so, has it been kept secret and why.
(3) What commercial constraints does the i:lirective place on Qantas.
(4) Will he release the text of the directive; if not, why not.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's questions is as follows:
(I) to (4) The Qantas Financial Directive has not been publicly released since it was first issued in 1947. Certain provisions in the Directive are now outmoded and it is currently being revised according to the guidelines outlined by the Prime Minister in his statement to the Parliament on 30 April. This revision is aimed at enabling Qantas to conduct its operations in a more commercially competitive manner. When this revision is completed, it is anticipated that the new Directive will be tabled in Parliament.
Fire Extinguishers
(Question No. 1692)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 9 April 1981 :
What action has been taken by his Department concerning the finding by the Department of Housing and Construction reported in the 1979-80 Annual Report of the Commonwealth Fire Board, that self-contained automatic halon gas fire extinguishers installed in fume cupboards where acid chemicals are used deteriorate rapidly.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
None is necessary. A check within my Department revealed no instances in which halon gas fire extinguishers are installed in fume cupboards where acid chemicals are used. Halon gas is used in the Capital Territory Health Commission's new Central Health Laboratory, but the gas is piped into the fume cupboards from cylinders IQcated outside the cupboards, in accordance with the latest Commonwealth Fire Board recommendations.
Quarantine: Rabies
(Question No. 1696)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 9 April 1981 :
(\.) Has his attention been drawn to a report in the Age newspaper of 24 January 1981 attributed to Mr Harry Butler that dogs with rabies were found on small boats intercepted along the northern Australian coastline.
(2) If so, will he provide details of the cases of vessels deteeted in this area carrying dogs with rabies.
Mr MacKellar-The Minister for Health has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(I) Yes. The honourable member will know that Mr Harry Butler, CBE., is spearheading a major Quarantine Awareness Program launched by the Government in 1980-81 to encourage individual self regulation and responsibility among

Australians on quarantine matters. I am advised that the report followed an address by Mr Butler in Darwin which he visited as part of this Program.
Mr Butler has advised that the article in the Age has misinterpreted comments made by him at this and on many other public occasions about the rabies risk from dogs carried on small boats of overseas origin in north Australian waters. Also, he was aware that dogs are known carriers of rabies and that rabies exists in countries to Australia's north. Mr Butler advises that in the light of these considerations he drew attention during his Darwin address to the simple way in which rabies could be introduced into Australia.
(2) Rabies has not been diagnosed in relation to any dog found on a vessel detected along the northern Australian coastline. However, any rabies susceptible animal, which in past months could have been in a country where rabies occurs, must be regarded as a rabies risk.
Quarantine: Cost Recovery for Expenditure on Facilities
(Question No. 1698)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
Furtherto the answer to question No. 701 (Hansard, 8 June 1978, page 3377), what action was taken in relation to capital cost recovery for expenditure on quarantine facilities.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The Government decided that quarantine charges should not be imposed to achieve capital cost recovery for expenditure on quarantine facilities.
Animal Quarantine Station
(Question No. 1708)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
(I) What is planned for the new animal quarantine station at Guildford, WA.
(2) What is the proposed timetable.
(3) What animals and numbers will it be able to handle.
(4) When is it anticipated that the Bicton site will no longer be required for quarantine purposes.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) A number of new sites are now being considered for the new animal quarantine station in Perth rather than the site at Guildford as originally planned. The change in site was prompted by problems associated with the effect of the noise levels at the Guildford site on both animals and staff. Arrangements to select and acquire the site are currently under way and preliminary planning of the facility has been completed.
(2) The timetable fOr the new facility has not yet been finalised due to the change in site. It is anticipated that following finalisation of site acquisition, a period of IS to 20 months will be required to complete the new facility. On this basis completion date will beilpproximately December 1982.
(3) The station is being designed to accommodate 48 dogs and 14 cats and will include a holding facility for up to 20 horses or 2S cattle.
(4) The agreement with the cWestern Australian State Government is that the station at Bicton will be vacated by July 1982.

(2) Consideration is given to factors such as climate and lo-

Cost of the Perth-Cocos Island-Christmas Island Air Route
(Question No. 1857)
Mr . Bungey asked the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
What was the cost to the Commonwealth in 1979-80 of the Perth-Cocos Island-Christmas Island air route.
Mr Wilson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
Actual expenditure in 1979-80 was $1,070,261 and receipts paid into Consolidated Revenue amounted to $1,061,272. This reflected a net cost to the Commonwealth in 1979-80 of $8,989.
Under the terms of the Government's approval for the operation of the Perth-Cocos Island-Christmas Island air charter, the operation is required to be self supporting.
However, because of the nature of the Commonwealth accounting system, it is difficult to compare results for a particular financial year. For example, recovery of debts totalling $63,372 incurred in 1979-80 occurred during 1980-81.

Imports of 5.5 in. Artillery Ammunition

(Question No. 1918)

Mr Scholes asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 28 April 1981:

(I) Is it a fact that ammunition for Australian 5.5 in. artil-

lery is being imported from Pakistan.

.

(2) Are Australian stocks of 5.5 in. ammunition sufficient , to enable sustained action should the Army be called upon to
perform a military task involving medium artillery.

(3) Does capacity exist for manufacture of 5.5 in. ammunition in Australia; if so, is that capacity being used.

Answers to Questions

19 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 497

(4) What quantity of ammunition has been imported into Australia.
Mr Killen-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) No.
(2) No. Due to age and condition the 5.5 in. medium gun no longer has an operational role. The remaining serviceable weapons are used for training and stocks of ammunition are adequate for that purpose.
(3) Yes. Both the projectiles and propellant can be manufactured in Australia but because use has been limited it has not been economic to manufacture projectiles in Australia for many years. Propellant is currently being manufactured in Australia.
(4) The last import was of approximately 14,000 projectiles from the United Kingdom in 1970.
Financial Assistance to the New Zealand Dairy Industry
(Question No. 1934)
Mr Humphreys asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 28 April 1981 :
(I) Has his attention been drawn to the various forms of financial assistance given to the New Zealand Dairy Industry by the New Zealand Government including (a) interest rate concessions provided by the New Zealand Reserve Bank and calculated by the New Zealand Dairy Board to be worth $50 million per annum to the industry and (b) the exemption of co-operative dairy companies from the payment of income tax.
(2) Will financial concessions similar to those available to New Zealand producers be made available to Australian Dairy Producers given the present domestic shortage in but~er production and the threatened Hood of New Zealand dairy food imports.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Following the placing of the honourable member's question on the Notice Paper, I sought information on the matters raised in this part. The information indicates that New Zealand does provide assistance to the dairy industry in that country in a number of ways.
(2) This part seems to be based on the premise that the Australian dairy industry is not in receipt of assistance. That, of course is far from being the case. However, because the forms of ihis assistance differ from those which apply in New Zealand, any attempt at a quantified comparison would raise considerable practical problems. I understand that the Australian Dairy Corporation is confident, after discussions with dairy product manufacturers, that adequate butter will be produced within Australia for dome~tic requ.irements. In the circumstances, safeguards or subventions against a hypothetical threat of imports are not necessary. The Government and the Corporation will, of course, continue to monitor closely the butter supply position.

Film Industry
(Question No. 1946)
Mr Hurford asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 28 April 1981:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to the grave disquiet in the community that a Liberal-National Country Party Coalition Policy promise at the last election in relation to the film industry, whjch was said then to cost only $2 million, is now to cost $20 million.
(2) Will he now give the House an assurance that the estimated costs of all Coalition promises at election time should not be multiplied by 10 to arrive at their true cost. _ .
(3) Is he able to say whether the $20 million being made available for the Film Industry is causing extreme concern to responsible industry leaders and others, not only because of the tax avoidance possibilities but also because of the great number of indifferent films which will now be made from resources in short supply for markets which do not exist.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I), (2) and (3) There has been a very strong response to the Government's important initiative in this matter. To the end of May applications for certification as' Australian films has been made in respect of 203 films expected to cost sonie $145 million in all. It may be expected that this will place some strain on film production resources and some concern has been expressed as to the quality of films that may result.
Nevertheless it is clear that the industry has generally welcomed the Government's initiative. The generous taxation benefits conferred by the scheme, plus the, ordinary economic incentive to obtain a maximum return on investment should result in the attraction of investment moneys to quality films having the potential for significant box-office success. This incentive to quality is in fact reinforced by the second limb of the concessions, the income exemption, which in itself is a strong incentive to investment in films with potential for success. The operation of the scheme will, however, be carefully monitored over the next six to twelve months.
In addition, the attraction of the scheme to those concerned only with its exploitation has not been overlooked. Indeed, responsible film authorities have registered their concern at the intervention of these elements. Significant safeguards have accordingly been incorporated in the legislation. In particular, the availability of deductions is to be limited to expenditure for which an investor is at a real risk of economic loss and,

Answers to Questions

with effect From 28 May 1981, deductions under the scheme will become available only when a film is completed and marketed.

Radio Australia: Mail from the People's Republic of China
(Question No. 1953)
Mr Duffy asked the Minister for Communications, upon notice, on 28 April 1981:
(I) Approximately how many letters to Radio Australia arrive from the People's Republic of China each month.
(2) Approximatelyhow many of these letters are answered each month and what happens to those letters not replied to.
(3) How many staff members of his Department are employed in handling the inflow of mail from China and what are their (a) classification and (b) special qualifications in each case.
(4) [s consideration being given to increasing the staff ceilings in Radio Australia; iF so, what is the proposed increase in employees and when will appointments be made.
(5) If no increase is being contemplated, will he consider raising the staff ceiling in this area.
(6)- What Radio Australia offices have been established overseas and what are the (a) number of staff and (b) approximate number of letters handled in each office.
(7) Is consideration being given to the establishment of a Radio Australia office in Peking; iF not, will be consider this proposal.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) On an average 10,000 letters are received each month from the People's Republic of China:
(2) Approximately 3,000 letters a month are answered, 2,000 by having their requests for musical items or questions answered in Chinese language programs broadcast by Radio Australia and 1,000 by the despatch of a program guide: The contents of the remainder are noted. Some letters do not call fora reply.
(3) No members of the Department of Communications are so employed. The Chinese Section of Radio Australia employs two Program Officers, whose duties include the handling of correspondence From the People's Republic of China and S.E.Asia.
In addition, two correspondence officers are employed on a casual basis to receive and read mail From the People's Republic of China and to sort and despatch letters to producers for inclusion in Chinese language programs. The Program Officers are required to have linguistic fluency in English and Chinese; ability to type accurately and with speed. To proceed beyond a salary barrier they are also required to be capable of presenting programs-in Chinese; and of accurate and speedy translation.
(4) No increase in staff allocation to Radio Australia is contemplated within the ceiling limits currently applying to the ABC.
(5) While the Government has established a staff ceiling For the ABC it is left to the management of the Commission to allocatestaffwithin ceiling limits.
(6) The only Radio Australia office overseas is in Jakarta. Six locally employed staff there handle approximately 10,000 letters a month.
(7) Yes.

Insurance Agents and Brokers
(Question No. 1962)
Mr Jacobi asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 28 April 1981:
(I) Will he undertake to place before Cabinet the main recommendations of the Law Reform Commission in its report entitled Insurance Agents and Brokers.
(2) Is he considering, as one of the options available to the Government, a system based only on those recommendations which were unanimous and not those like disclosure of commission, on which there was disagreement in the Commission.
(3) Can a system based on the unanimous recommendation of the Law Reform Commission be far less onerous than that originally approved by brokers and enacted in the United Kingdom.
(4) Will the main aim of such a system be to prevent and punish dishonest and reckless conduct by brokers.
(5) Will it involve minimal costs and minimal interference with the broking industry.
(6) Have losses through broker insolvency of roughly $7.5m in the last 15 months increased markedly since the data on earlier insolvencies was collected by the Commission.
(7) Are losses a cause of enormous concern, irrespective of the percentage which they form of total insurance premiums.
(8) Is he able to say whether disagreements still exist between the broker organisations; if so, in what areas; if not, do both organisations support the need for national legislation, based upon the recommendation of the Law Reform Commission.
(9) Has any organisation opposed in principle the Commission's main recommendation that premium moneys should be required to be kept separate from the broker's own funds.
(10) Is he able to say what was the general tenor of the responses of State governments and of the insurance industry to the Commission's recommendations.
(II) Were any bodies representative of the general body of policy holders asked for their views when the Government sought the views of the industry.
(12) Has he noted the introduction of similar legislation by the Western Australian State Government.
(13) Will other States be obliged to follow this lead if he fails to act.
(14) If so, will this be disruptive to the industry, wasteful of resources and give rise to a plethora of red tape for the industry throughout Australia.
(15) If the Government fails to act in this matter, will it be subject to the charge of inconsistency in seeking to control dishonesty in the social welfare field but not dishonesty in business practices.
(16) Has his attention been drawn to recent press reports in the Business Review, of 4-10 April 1981, which indicate that Australia's troubled insurance broking industry expects more broking companies to collapse in the coming months.
(17) If both industry and policy holders are to be given the protection they deserve, must Commonwealth legislation based upon the recommendations of the Law Reform Commission be introduced as quickly as possible; if so, will he give an assurance that the necessary legislation will be introduced soon.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

(1) to (17) The honourable member has raised a number of issues of relevance to the Report of the Law Reform Commission on Insurance Agents and Brokers (tabled in Parliament on 16 September 1980) and to the question of regulation of insurance brokers. After careful study of the Commission's Report and the views put forward by many inter.ested parties the Government has decided not to introduce legislation to regulate the activities of insurance brokers operating in Australia. The factors involved in the Government's decision were outlined in thestatement I made on this matter in the House on 10 June 1981.
Commonwealth Grants Commission
(Question No,1965)
Mr McLean asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 28 April 1981:
Which States are represented through membership of the Commonwealth Grants Commission.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The Commonwealth Grants Commission consists of a Chairman and two members, none of whom represents any particular State or States.
For the review of States' tax sharing relativities, a special Division of the Commission was created consisting of the Chairman, the two members referred to above, one associate member appointed on the nomination of the Premiers of New South Wales and Victoria, and two associate members appointed on the nomination of Queensland, South Australia, Western Australia and Tasmania. While the associate members were nominated by particular States, they were neither required nor expected to 'represent' those States.

Kidney Dialysis Machines
(Question No. 1983)
Mr O'Keefe asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 29 April 1981:
(l) Is there a shortage of kidney dialysis machines in Australia.
(2) Is this shortage causing considerable concern to kidney sufferers, hospitals and the medical profession.
(3) Will he investigate this situation with a view to giving assistance to eliminate the shortage.

500 REPRESENTATIVES 19 August 1981

Answers to Questions

Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The Australian Kidney Foundation has advised that there is an adequate supply of haemodialysis machines in Australia.
(2) and (3) Under the current hospital cost-sharing arrangements between the Commonwealth and State Governments, the Commonwealth Government has undertaken to meet 50 per cent of the agreed net operating costs of each State's recognised (that is, public) hospital system. The States for their part have undertaken to ensure, to the best of their ability, that the accommodation, Facilities, staff and services needed for the care and treatment of all eligible persons are provided by recognised hospitals. It is a matter for the hospital authority in each State to determine expenditure programs within the hospital cost-sharing arrangements.
Under the new health funding arrangements announced recently, the Commonwealth Government will provide funding to the States for public hospitals, the Community Health Program and the School Dental Scheme through general revenue grants at the level of funding provided in 1980-81 plus 10 per cent, less a share of the revenue that the States could reasonably be expected to raise by the introduction of appropriate inpatient and outpatient charges in 1981-82.
There will be no requirements laid down as to the services to which the funds should be allocated. The States should have at'their disposal increased resources under the new arrangements. The future provision of dialysis machines through the public hospitals is a matter for consideration by the States in accordance with their priorities.

Intra-corporate Imports and Exports

'

What proportion of total Australian (a) imports and (b) exports are intra-corporate.

Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

The Australian Statistician has advised that, partly because details of trade between Australian companies and their over-
seas branches and subsidiaries are not available to the ABS, it is not possible fully to answer this question.

However, information is available on trade by Australian subsidiaries and branches of foreign companies with their
foreign parents and other related companies abroad; it
suggests that about 40 per cent of imports to Australia and
about 20 per cent of exports from Australia come into this category.

It must be noted that details of trade by Australian subsidiaries and branches of foreign companies with their foreign
parents and other related companies abroad are obtained
from foreign investment surveys and are not directly comparable with total trade data which are based on customs documents. The ratios mentioned above, therefore, must be taken as indicative only.

Solar-powered Heating Systems, Commonwealth Buildings
(Question No, 2031)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister for Housing and Construction, upon notice, on 5 May 1981:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to a report in the Daily Telegraph of 7 April 1981 in which the New South Wales Minister for Education was quoted as saying that he anticipated that Australia's first solar-powered school will save up to $5,000 a year in fuel bills.
(2) Is this figure an accurate indicator of the savings on fuel bills for the average school.
(3) How many new Commonwealth buildings have had solar-powered heating systems installed since 1975.
(4) In how many existing Commonwealth buildings have solar-powered heating systems replaced oil-powered heating systems.
Mr McVeigh-The answer to the honourable
member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes. The Department of Housing and Construction is aware of the proposal by the New South Wales Government for the solar-powered Bangor public school at Menai.
(2) Fuel bills for space heating are largely determined by the climate, and in Canberra vary from $3,000 per year for primary schools to $14,000 per year for large high schools and colleges. A solar assisted heating system could be expected to contribute approximately 50 per cent of a school's heating requirements, giving potential annual savings of between $1,500 and $7,000 but at considerable additional capital cost.
A recent national study by the Schools Commission found that the major factor influencing energy consumption in existing schools, is the users. Annual savings of $1 Om in expenditure on energy for all purposes including heating were predicted if this problem of poor operational practice was addressed on a na tionallevel.
(3) No new Commonwealth buildings have been equipped with solar-powered space heating systems since 1975.
(4) No existing oil powered systems have been replaced or supplemented with solar systems.
The CSIRO Division of Building Research experimental building at Highett in Victoria is equipped with a solar assisted heating system. The Department of Housing and Construction office building in Townsville is fitted with a solar assisted air-conditioning system (without heating). Both installations are of a developmental nature and were not intended to be cost effective.
Active solar space heating systems are not yet proven to be commercially 'viable in Australia because the energy savings are not sufficient to recoup the additional capital expenditure over the life of the equipment. The capital costs of such a system are appreciably higher than conventional systems because of the relatively high cost of the solar component and the need to install alternative back-up equipment using conventional energy sources.
Attention to passive solar heating techniques in the design and siting of buildings is considered to be a worthwhile method of using solar energy to assist with space heating.

Exports to Thailand
(Question No. 2038)
Mr Milton asked the Minister for Foreign Affairs, upon notice, on 5 May 1981:
(I) Do many companies involved in the scientific industry which attempt to ellport their products to Thailand face a 30 per cent duty on the products.
(2) If so, can he say whether this is a result of the Australian Government's attitude to UNESCO and the Thai customs issue.
(3) Will he provide a clear statement of Australia's attitude to UNESCO and the Thai customs issue, together with some positive plans within a set time frame for a resolution of the problems involved for potential ellports to Thailand.
Mr Street-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) There are.lwo Australian companies which are known to have an interest in ellporting scientific equipment to Thailand. Broadly speaking scientific equipment entering Thailand faces a 30 per cent import duty.
(2) Thailand is a signatory to the UNESCO Florence Agreement and goods covered by the Agreement may be imported free of duty provided they are for use in Government research related activities and are sourced from countries signatory to the Agreement. Australia has not signed the Agreement and Thailand applies the normal duties to such goods imported from Australia.
(3) The Florence Agreement was adopted by the General Conference of UNESCO in 1950. Australia has not signed the Agreement because of conti,nuing industry assistance and tariff policy problems of a fundamental nature.

1981 ProoCCoin Sets

(Question No. 2049)

Mr Mildren asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 6 May 1981:

(I) How many 1981 Australian Proof Coin sets have been minted.

(2) How many orders for the 1981 Proof Coin sets have been received from private collectors.

(3) How many sets of 1981 Proof Coin sets have been

allocated to private coin collectors.

.

(4) Was a quota of 1981 Proof Coin sets allocated to each private coin collector; if so, what constituted a quota.

(5) What proportion of the 1981 Proof Coin sets distributed was allocated to private coin dealers.

. (6) How many orders for the 1981 Proof Coin sets were
received from coin dealers and hoW many sets were allocated to coin dealers.

, (7) Was a quota of 1981 Proof Coin sets allocated to each coin dealer; if so, what constituted a quota.

(8) Were orders for 1981 Proof Coin sets received from
foreign or overseas collectors or dealers; if so, were these orders given equal treatment to Australian orders.

(9) How many 1981 Proof Coin sets were allocated to foreign or overseas coin collectors or dealers.

(10) What proportion of 1981 Proof Coin sets distributed w~re allocated to foreign or overseas collectors or dealers.

Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

The Controller of the Mint has advised me as follows:

(I) The number of 1981 Australian proof coin sets minted as at 28 May 1981 was 29,751, against a planned total of around 83,000 sets.
(2) to (7) A total of 35,120 orders for 99,216 sets of coins were received for 1981 Australian proof coin sets up to 28 May 1981. The offer of these sets has been closed for some time. Orders from whatever source are being met in the order of receipt of remittances, subject to a limit of 100 per order from private collectors and, in the case of dealers ordering more than 100 coins, a level held at or below the level of the previous year. Comprehensive statistics are not maintained separately for private collectors and dealers. However, of the 18,362 sets despatched to Australian customers up to 28 May 1981 3,300 sets have been despatched to dealers ordering more than 100 coins.
(8) to (10) Orders received from overseas were given equal treatment to Australian orders. As of 28 May 19817,000 sets have been despatched to the Mint's overseas sales agent for distribution to customers in North America and areas of Europe including the United Kingdom. This will account for the bulk of orders accepted for overseas sales.

Total quantity (million Iitres)-14,869. - For import statistics the 'Country of Origin' is defined as the 'Country of Production', that is, in respect of refined petroleum products, the country responsible for the last phase of the substantial manufacturing operation before the goods take their final form. This is normally the country in which the goods were loaded for export to Australia. Imports of petroleum products, however, present a special case because trans-shipment of these goods occurs at Singapore en route to Australia. It is usual practice for petroleum products to be held in common storage at Singapore, where there is comingling of the goods originating from a number of different countries. On exportation to Australia, it is not possible to differentiate where the petroleum products originated and thus Singapore is shown as 'Country of Origin '.

Reduction of Staff and Functions: Department of the Treasury
(Question No. 2126)
Mrs Kelly asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 6 May 1981:
Will he provide a detailed list of the: (a) numbers of staff and (b) functions to be eliminated in all areas of his Department that are located in the Australian Capital Territory, when the recommendations of the Committee of Review of Commonwealth Functions are implemented.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(a) The decisions relatihg to Treasury functions which were among the decisions arising from the Review of Commonwealth Functions that were announced by the Prime Minister in his statement to the Parliament on 30 April 1981 will not have immediate staffing implications for the Treasury. The longer term staffing implications of those decisions cannot be assessed at this stage.
(b) The ACT Companies (Life Insurance Holding Companies) Ordinance 1968, and related amending Ordinances are to be repealed. The Ordinance was introduced to restrict the number and value of foreign shares that may be hetd in certain life insurance holding companies incorporated

Answers to Questions

19 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 505

in the ACT. The Ordinance has related to only two companies; upon its repeal it will no longer be necessary to monitor foreign ownership and control of those companies for the purposes of enforcing the restrictions provided for in the Ordinance. Proposed investments in those companies will, however, remain subject to the Government's foreign investment policy.
Concerning the Royal Australian Mint, it has been decided that there is to be a review of the scope for further subcontracting for coinage blanks to the private sector. It has also been decided that private sector tenders are to be invited for the sole rights to market and distribute all Australian numismatic coins within Australia.
The function of the Co-ordinator General of Public Works has been terminated.

Survey of Roads to Perth Airport
(Question No. 2197)
Mr Beazley asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 12 May 1981:
(I) Has an origin and destination survey of roads to Perth Airport been conducted by his Department or on its behalf.
(2) If the survey has been completed, or if it is still being conducted, does the Government intend to release its findings, ifso, when.
(3) If the findings of the survey will not be released, what is the reason.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) A road traffic study is currently being carried out by consultants on behalf of my Department. The study incorporates a vehicle interview survey at the airport in early April 1981 in which information was obtained on vehicle origins and destinations.
(2) and (3) It is not my Department's normal practice to formally release the findings of isolated surveys of this k0d. The findings will be incorporated into the airport master plan:. ning activity and a summary will probably be included in the master plan document. Because of its importance to State planners it will be made readily available to those concerned. However, the findings will be in no way confidential and I will provide Mr Beazley with a copy when available if he so wishes.

Reserve Bank Credit Finance to Public
(Question No. 2246)
Dr Everingham asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 13 May 1981:
([) Has his attention been drawn to The Story of the Commonwealth Bank by D. J. Amos, FA[S, which suggests at page 7 that Sir Denison Miller's 1920 issue of 23 million of bank credit caused private banks to abandon in Australia their world deflationary policy.
(2) Did the issue of Reserve Bank credit to finance public works, for which resources are idle and in ample supply, occupy profitably for the community persons who would otherwise have been a financial charge and emotional debit to the community.
(3) Did the issue of money to subsidize costs of essential Consumer Price Index items, for which demand was inelastic and productive capacity was in excess of demand, create or decrease cost and wage inflation in 1943-45.
(4) [s the money supply inflationary if (a) selectively spent to avoid demand-pull inflation to stimulate production in slack economic sectors and demand by the poor and (b) restricted in volume to final prices of goods and services produced by the community using that money and Reserve Bank issues of interest free capital for profitable public works and enterprises which do not compete for resources and which take up idle resources and productive capacity.

Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) and (3) I do not think any good purpose would be served by attempting to make judgements called for in parts (2) and (3) of the question, which involve events of up to sixty years ago.
In relation to part (3), however, [ would point out that from 1942 until the end of the war there were extensive wage and price controls. There was also extensive rationing. [n the short term, therefore, the subsidies for Consumer Price Index items (principally clothing, some food and other commodities in short supply to the civilian economy) would have had little effect upon recorded prices. These subsidies, together with other, measures of wartime government finance, did however add to the amount of money held as idle bank deposits. The activation of these deposits, in conjunction with post-war shortages, helped to raise the price level some 30 per cent between 1945-46 and 1948-49.
(4) Excessive increases in the money supply would be inflationary in the circumstances postulated, even assuming that slack areas of the economy could be pinpointed in the manner proposed. The economy is an inter-related whole, and action taken in one area inevitably has consequences for others. For example, the resulting increases in personal incomes would give rise to demand for a wide spectrum of goods and services, including those in short supply. In addition, some of the measures referred to (selecti~e spending in slack economic sectors, issues of interest free capital) would inhibit the capacity of the economy to adjust to changing circumstances, and thus reduce its efficiency and increase its susceptibility to inflation.
Exports of Bauxite and Alumina
(Question No. 2257)
Mr Uren asked the Treasurer, upon nqtice, on 13 May 1981:
Further to his answer to question No. 176 (Hansard, 24 February 1981, page 72) can he say (a) for what reason is information on exports of bauxite and details of exports of alumina by country of consignment subject to secrecy, (b) under what provision of the law is this secrecy prescribed and enforced and (c) which departments or authorities have access to this information.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(a) The information referred to is not made available by the Australian Statistician because to do so would reveal the transactions of individual exporters.
(b) Section 24 of the Census and Statistics Act 1905.
(c) The Australian Bureau of Statistics compiles export statistics from export entries and periodic returns submitted to the Bureau of Customs. The Bureau of Customs also provides copies of all entries and returns to the Reserve Bank, solely in connection with its responsibilities for supervising the receipt of export proceeds under the Banking (Foreign Exchange) Regulations. In addition, it provides to the Department of Trade and Resources copies of relevant entries and returns for .the purpose of monitoring exports of commodities, including bauxite and alumina, which are subject to export control pursuant to Regulation 9 of the Customs (Prohibited Exports) Regulations. I am advised that the Department also receives information on exports of these commodities direct from exporters. Consistent with undertakings given by successive governments, information obtained

Communication Between Rival Powers
(Question No. 2313)
Dr Everingham asked the Minister for Foreign Affairs upon notice, on 26 May 1981:
(I) Does the Government recognise the increasing risk of world war involved in the arms race and in failures of communication betweenrival powers.
(2) Has the Government supported moves to increase discussion of matters in dispute between the super-powers.
(3) Will the Government acknowledge that traditional responses to increased tension between countries, including reduction of diplomatic exchanges, (a) accelerate and exacerbate escalation of that tension, (b) are a virtually universal factor in war and (c) are put into reverse when peace is sought with an enemy.
(4) Will the Government reconsider its measures seen as being imposed against the Soviet Union but in fact imposed against human survival as referred to in part (5) of his reply to my question on notice No. 1923 (Hansard, 14 May 1981, page 2505).

(5) If the Australian Government was consulted, what response did it give.
(6) If the Australian Government was not consulted on this matter, (a) is this a cause of concern for the Australian Government and (b) what guarantee is there that the USA will consult Australia on any Future foreign policy initiative involving countries in the area.
Mr Street-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) An alliance between the United States and Japan has not been announced recently. In the joint communique issued by Prime Minister Zenko Suzuki and President Ronald Reagan on 8 May, it was stated that "the Prime Minister and the President, recognising that the alliance between Japan and the United States is built upon their shared values of democracy and liberty, reaffirmed their solidarity, Friendship and mutual trust". Although there has been debate in Japan about the interpretation of the word 'alliance' in this sentence, in particular as to whether it committed Japan to a new or altered security relationship with the United States, the Government of Japan has made it clear that its relationship with the United States remains as it has been.

Mr Street-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) and (2) Yes.
(3) Yes, in certain circumstances.
(4) No.

Mr Thomson-The answer to the honourable member's question is:

In insuring peace and stability in the region and the defence of Japan, they acknowledged the desirability of an appropriate division of roles between Japan and the United States. The Prime Minister stated that Japan, on its own initiative and in accordance with its constitution and basic defence policy, will seek to make even greater efforts For improving its defence capabilities in Japanese territories and in its surrounding sea and air space, and for further alleviating the financial burden of US. forces in Japan. The President expressed his understanding of the statement by the Prime Minister."
These positions outlined in the communique do not alter our perception of Japan's defence and security policies nor of Japan's relations with the United States. The Government sees cooperation under the Japan-United States Treaty of Mutual Cooperation and Security as a vital part of the defence of the Asia/Pacific region, as are the separate arrangements under ANZUS. It is in Australia's interest that the defence relationship between Japan and the United States remain a harmonious one.
