Ravna Gora Chetniks

(Question No. 2332)

Mr Fry asked the Minister representing the Attorney-General, upon notice, on 27 May 1981:

Is the Attorney-General able to provide any information on

an Australian-based organisation calling themselves the

Ravna Gora Chetniks?

.

Mr Viner-The Attorney-General has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:

I am informed that Ravna Gora is a range of mountains in Yugoslavia where,- during World War II, the Chetniks or Serbian Royalist troops, fought against Ustashi and German
troops.

I understand that there reside in Australia as well as in sev-
eral other Western countries some former Chetniks who are anti-communists, royalist and oppose the Yugoslav Govern-
ment. However, I am informed that the membership of the Ravna Gora Chetniks in Australia is quite small.

Foreign Registered Ships: Permits to Operate to Western Australia
(Question No. 2334)
Mr Rocher asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 27 May 1981:
(I) How many foreign-registered ships sailing to or from Western Australian ports or from or to (a) Cocos (Keeling) Islands and (b) Christmas Island were granted permits to operate during the last 12 months.
(2) Were any foreign vessels permitted to operate over the routes without permits during the same period; if so, (a), why, (b) how many were given permission, (c) where were they registered and (d) what were the names of those vessels.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

(I) None.
(2) (a) to (d) Permits are not required for foreignregistered ships to carry traffic between Western Australian ports and the Cocos (Keeling) Islands because the Cocos (Keeling) Islands are exempted from the coasting trade provisions of the Navigation Act. No permits were granted in respect of Christmas Island which is included in the Australian coasting trade. Ships carrying traffic between Western Australian por-ts and the Cocos (Keeling) Islands have been the Singapore registered generaL cargo vessels 'Wild Rover' and 'Tropic Star'; and between Western Australian ports and Christmas Island have been the British registered bulk carriers 'Baron Murray', 'Cape Hawke' and 'Cape Otway" which are authorised to operate by virtue of their being licensed under the Navigation Act for the Australian coasting trade.
Adelaide Airport
(Question No. 2337)
Mr Jacobi asked the. Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 27 May 1981:
(I) In what year was land purchased for Tullamarine airport in Melbourne.
(2) How many passengers passed through Melbourne in that year.
(3) How many passengers passed through Adelaide airport in 1980.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The site for Tullamarine airport was purchased in 1961 by the Department of the Interior, as it was then known on behalf of the then Department of Civil Aviation. The acquisition was promulgated in Commonwealth Gazette No. 70 dated 7 September 1961.
(2) During 1961, 21,661 international passenger movements and 1,145,704 domestic passenger movements, a total of 1,167,365 passenger movements, were recorded at Melbourne (Essendon) airport.
(3) During 1980, 1,962,311 domestic passenger movements were recorded at Adelaide airport, with a further estimated 71,200 passenger movements on third level (commuter) airlines.
Comprehensive Statement on Taxation
(Question No. 2338)
Dr Klugman asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on27 May 1981:
Will value added tax or other kinds of indirect taxes instead of high income taxes enable Australian manufacturers to compete better on the export market; if so, why did he not mention this in his comprehensive statement to the House on taxation on 12 March 1981 (Hansard, pages 760-70).
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
.Many factors have to be taken into account in any consideration of major changes to a taxation system, including the possible implications for exporters of any such changes. Not all factors would carry the same weight in such consideration.
My statement to the House on 12 March 1981 was not intended as an exhaustive primer of taxation principles and practices-nor, indeed would I seek to strain the patience of the House in that way. However, since the matter has been raised, I note that under the present sales tax (an important element in our present taxation system) exports are exempt,

Answers to Questions

19 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 511

and similar exemption could be expected to apply with a value-added tax (VAT).
I add that, unless exemption were provided for export sales, a VAT could not, in itself, be expected to enable manufacturers to compete better on export markets. [ understand that many countries that impose VAT do indeed grant exemptions of this kind. At the same time, they impose VAT on imports without granting credit for tax paid in the country of production. Where trade takes place solely between countries that impose VAT on this basis and at the same rate, the ultimate effect would be neutral on imports and exports, each country's VAT amounting to a tax on the consumption of its residents only. [n the interests of fiscal harmonisation, and the avoidance of double taxation, the member countries of the EEC have moved towards a uniform practice of this kind.
Traffic Accidents
(Question No. 2339)
Dr Klugman asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 27 May 1981 :
(I) Were traffic accidents responsible for approximately 3508 deaths and for over 91 ,600 injuries in Australia in 1979.
(2) Did they also account for-3 per cent of total deaths in Australia and more than 50 per cent of all deaths in the 15-25 years age group..
(3) [n what proportion of these accidents was at least one participant affected by alcohol.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) Yes to both parts.
(3) The exact proportion of casualty accidents in which at least one participant was affected by alcohol is not known. Research suggests that alcohol is a factor in 50 per cent of road accidents involving a fatality and in 34 per cent of personal injury road ac<;idents. Since not all drivers, riders and pedestrians involved In casualty accidents are tested for blood alcohol content, these figures necessarily understate the proportion of such accidents in which at least one participant was affected by alcohol.

Dumping of Radioactive Waste
(Question No. 2348)
Dr Everingham asked the Minister for Foreign Affairs, upon notice, on 27 May 1981 :
(I) [s it a fact that from 1946 to 1970 the United States of America authorised dumping of more than 50,000 drums of radioactive waste in the Pacific Ocean, some in the Farallon [slands fishing grounds off San Francisco, where up to 8,000 times normal radioactivity from cesium and strontium isotopes and plutonium have been found from waste described by the US Environmental Protection Agency as-low level, and some at sites never made public.
(2) [fso, does the Government support recent USA Government estimates that Pacific radioactive waste could total up to 300,000 curies, 10 times previous estimates.
(3) Has the USA Atomic Energy Commission disclosed to Australia, that it knew 20 years ago that drums holding nuclear waste when dumped were broken open by water pressure at great depths; if so, when.
(4) Did the United States Navy recently agree that 23 nuclear submarines homeported at Pearl Harbour from 1964 to 1973 dislodged 4.8 million gallons of radioactive waste there; if so, did Hawaii recently establish State monitoring of the effects on marine life.
(5) Are any results of this monitoring available.
(6) [s he able to state whether Japan has a program to dump initially 5,000 to 10,000 drums of radioactive waste less than 600 miles north of the Marianas.
(7) What offers has Australia made to Japan to develop alternative waste disposal techniques and with what result.
(8) Will the Government promptly publish all details of any use of Australian inventions for nuclear waste disposal including dump sites and all known information on military and industrial nuclear contamination and dumping plans in the Pacific Ocean.
(9) Will Australia support moves for extension of the Antarctic nuclear-free zone; ifso, in what regions; if not, why not.
Mr Street-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The locations of all sites where radioactive waste has been dumped by the United States of America were made public in 1971 a.t which time 41 sites were identified. Subsequent searching of records has added several more sites, and all site locations have been published. About ninety per cent of all activity dumped by the US has been at four main sites:

Site

512 REPRESENTATIVES 19 August 1981

Answers to Questions

The United States Environmental Protection Agency has advised that the number of drums of radioactive waste dumped by the United States of America from 1946-70 at the above Atlantic and Pacific Ocean sites was 76,301. The number of drums dumped in the Pacific (at the Farallon Island sites) was 47,500. No strontium release was detected by monitoring at either of the Atlantic or Farallon Island sites. Plutonium 239/240 was detected at the Farallon Island sites (not at the Atlantic sites) at concentrations 2 to 25 times greater than would be expected from background attributable to fall-out from nuclear weapons testing. Cesium was not detected at the Farallon Island sites. It was, however, detected at Atlantic sites at concentrations 3 to 70 times greater than background.
(2) According to the United States Environmental Protection Agency, present activity at the Farallon sites is estimated to amount to 14,500 curies, not 300,000 curies as suggested.
(3) The United States authorities have advised that their experience is that drums holding nuclear waste actually remain intact when dumped and that any release takes place over a long period of time. The US Atomic Energy Commission has always taken the position, however, that it needs to be assumed for the purpose of analysis that drums holding nuclear waste would .fracture on impact when dumped, and that activity would be released..
(4) United States official sources have confirmed that releases of low-level r!ldioactive liquid wastes from US nuclear submarines in Pearl Harbour from 1964-78 totalled 4,843,000 gallons, containing about 1.0 curie of activity. Most of this release (0.98 curie) occurred in 1964 in a volume of 405,000 ga,lions.
The State of Hawaii established an environmental monitoring program for Pearl Harbor by granting a one-year contract to the Gamma Corporation. This commenced in June 1980.
(5) The Gamma Corporation has reported that three quarterly samplings have been made in the course of the monitoring program. The first quarter sampling in September 1980 showed the highest cobalt-60 activity of about 0.25 picocurie per gram. The seconl;! quarter sampling in December 1980 showed the same general pattern of cobalt-60 activity as in the first quarter. Crab and seawe,ed samples showed only normal activity. Sampling of air activity showed a continuing rise in fallout activity, presumably from a recent nuclear test in the atmosphere by China. Third quarter sampling in February 1981 showed about the same distribution pattern as obtained in'the previous quarter sampling, except for sample station no. 6 which recorded an activity of 0.488 picocurie per gram, about three times the average of previous activity recorded by that station.
Further sampling is required for a greater period to determine whether these samples are indicative of an increasing activity level in Pearl Harbour.
, (6) Japan has announced that it is proposing to conduct a trial dumping of about 10,000 drums of cement-solidified lowlevel waste, comprising less than 500 curies of radioactivity in the North Pacific. The dump site, 300 N 147E, is approximately 510 nautical miles (945 kilometres) south-east of Tokyo, and 290 nautical miles (540 kilometres) from the nearest island, which is a Japanese possession. The nearest island of the Mariana group is about 590 nautical miles (1,090 kilometres) from the proposed dump site.
(7) Australia and Japan participate in international studies on nuclear waste disposal in the Nuclear Energy Agency of the Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development and in the International Atomic Energy Agency.
Bilaterally, there has been an exchange of correspondence between scientists in the Australian Atomic Energy Commission and the Japan Atomic Energy Institute concerning

papers relating to the SYNROC technique for the immobi!isation of high-level nuclear waste developed at the Australian National University.
Apart from the exchanges between scientists on the SYNROC technique, Australia and Japan have not had bilateral discussions on alternative waste disposal techniques and Australia is not in a position to offer other alternative techniques on radioactive waste disposal to Japan.
(8) Details of the SYNROC research and development program have been published widel.y in. scienl!fic journals ~nd elsewhere. This is the only Australian Invenllon for handling nuclear waste disposal in the form of high-level waste.
The Government has said that there is no ,intention of Australia's storing other. countries' nuclear wastes. Information on atomic test sites and related details of nuclear contamination within Australia has been published in reports by the Australian Ionising and Radiation Advisory Council and the Department of National Development and Energy. These reports are available to the public.
Australia has made clear its opposition to the Pacific region becoming a dumping ground for the indiscriminate and uricontrolled disposal of nuclear wastes. Consistent with that position the Government will continue to monitor closely all developments related to the dumping of nuclear waste in the Pacific.
Japan's announcement that it proposes to dump low level radioactive waste in the North Pacific is the only case known to the Government of a country which has plans to dump radioactive waste in the Pacific Ocean.
(9) Australia supports the Antarctic Treaty, Article 5 of which prohibits the nuclearisation of the whole of the area south of the 600 S parallel. The question of extending the region to cover a wider area has not been raised in the regular consultations which take place underthe Treaty.
The Government does not conSider that calls for nuclearfree~zones, which try to encompass the totality of nuclear issues affecting regions, are likely to be effective in meeting the security and environmental concerns of countries in the areas where such calls have been made. These concerns and interests are more likely to be taken into account if attention is focused on particular problems which are raised by particular issues.

Zinc Prices
(Question No. 2359)
Mr Hurford asked the Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs, upon notice, on 28 May 1981:
(I) Is it a fact that, for a number of years, the Trade Practices Commission has been monitoring zinc prices in Australia.
(2) Is it also a fact that, after more detailed investigation by the Commission of pricing practices of zinc suppliers, the Commission was not convinced there was no collusion between suppliers in the setting of zinc prices.
(3) Was it a consideration in the Commission's decision not to proceed in the courts against the zinc suppliers, but instead maintain a surveillance of prices, that the Commission did \lot have sufficient staff or financial resources to proceed with the matter at the time.

(4) Will he give an undertaking that the acceptance by the Government of the recommendations of the Committee of Review of Commonwealth Functions will not lead to a further reduction of the Commission's resources and an abandoning of the Commission's investigative work 'in the area of zinc pricing.
Mr Moore-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The Trade Practices Commission did monitor zinc prices in Australia for several years.
(2) and (3) After making extensive enquiries the Trade Practices Commission concluded that a price agreement between zinc sijj2pliers could not be proved. As stated in the Commission's Annual Report for 1979-80, the Commission found that price uniformity in recent years appeared to be consistent with non-collusive price leadership.
(4) The Commission has stated that it will keep the matter under general review as circumstances and any future relevant evidence may require.
Changes to Tariff Levels
(Question No. 2360)
Mr Hurford asked the Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs, upon notice, on 28 May 1981:
(I) Has he or his Department received complaints From State Governments or others alleging that changes are made to tariff levels without adequate consideration being given to the effect of such changes on the economies of individual States and regions.
(2) Are these criticisms justified; if not, what is the reason.

Answers to Questions

19 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 515

(3) What constraints will the Government's adoption of the Committee of Review of Commonwealth Functions' recommendations concerning the Industries Assistance Commission (Hansard, 30 April 1981, page 1841) have on the capacity of that body to give greater consideration to the special problems of individual regions when it is formulating proposals on industry protection.
Mr Moore-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Most industries assistance decisions necessarily involve the granting or withdrawal of some benefit to industries located in particular States or regions. Therefore representation of various points of view are expected and welcomed by Government to assist it to make a balanced decision which takes into account all relevant viewpoints.
(2) It is Government policy that tariffs and other assistance to particular industries are not changed before advice is received from either of the government's advisory bodies" on industry assistance, the lAC or the TAA. The public inquiry process provides all bodies, including State Governments and regional authorities, with ample opportunity to put their point of view. The draft report procedure allows comment on the lAC's draft recommendations before a final lAC report is submitted to the Government.
Under Section 23A of the Industries Assistance Commission Act 1973, the Commission is required to report on the probable economic and social consequences of implementing its recommendations, as well as the likely employment consequences, both generally and in particular regions.
Before taking a decision on assistance levels for an industry, the Government fully considers the views of all interested parties as well as the likely implications of its decisions. In these circumstances it is not reasonable to criticise changes on the grounds cited.
(.l) Following the adoption of the recommendations of the Committee of Review of Commonwealth Functions, the workload of the lAC will be examined. Any changes to the staffing levels in the lAC will only occur as a result of reductions in the general workload. There are no plans to make reductions that would limit in any way the lAC's capacity to meet its obligations to provide the Government with full and detailed information on matters pertinent to industries assistance.
Australian Conservation Foundation
(Question No. 2361)

(2) The phrase 'other political organisations' could be construed in a number of ways and, in the circumstances. I do not feel able to provide a response to this part of the honourable

member's question. If he will let me know which particular bodies he is interested in, I shall endeavour to provide him with the information he is seeking.

Menindee Lakes Storage, New South Wales
(Question No. 2367)
Mr Jacobi asked the Minister representing the Minister for National Development and Energy, upon notice, on 2 June 1981:
(I) Is the Menindee Lakes Storage, New South Wales, controlled by the River Murray Commission or by the New South Wales Water Resources Commission.
(2) Do special arrangements exist for the allocation of water from the storage to New South Wales and to South Australia; if so, what are the details.
Mr Anthony-The Minister for National pevelopment and Energy has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(I) The Menindee Lakes Storage was constructed and is owned by New South Wales which operates and controls this storage through the New South Wales Water Resources Commission in accordance with the terms and conditions of the Menindee Lakes Storage Agreement between the Commonwealth, New South Wales, Victoria and South Australia.
(2) The Menindee Lakes Storage Agreement lays down the arrangements for the allocation of water from the storage to the States. The River Murray Commission may not call for the release of water from the lakes over the period from when the quantity of water stored in the lakes becomes less than 480,000 megalitres until the-next time the quantity of water so stored exceeds 640,000 megalitres; but during this time New South Wales may use the water stored in the lakes as it requires. At other times' water stored in the lakes in excess of 480,000 megalitres is available to the River Murray Commission to allocate for sharing between New South Wales, Victoria and South Australia, in accordance with the provisions of the River Murray Waters Agreement. However, in addition to any share it may receive under that Agreement, New South Wales is entitled to use 125,000 megalitres in any year commencing on the 1st May. There are no special arrangements for the allocation of Menindee water to South Australia. South Australia's entitlement under the River Murray Waters Agreement may be provided from any of the sources of supply available to the River Murray Commission, including the Menindee Lakes.

River Murray Waters Agreement
(Question No. 2368)
Mr Jacobi asked the Minister representing the Minister for National Development and Energy, upon notice, on 2 June 1981:
(I) What is the allocation of water to (a)-New South Wales, (b) Victoria and (c) South Australia under the River Murray Waters Agreement for (i) normal flows and (ii) very low flows.
: (2) Was at least 85 per cent of River Murray system water used by New South Wales and Victoria under the Agreement diverted via the (a) Mulwala Canal and the Wakool District and (b) Yarrawonga Canal and the Torrumbarry System in 1979-80; if not, what was the percentage.

(3) Was water used by New South Wales and Victoria approximately nine-fold less polluted in the Mulwala/ Yarrawonga Canals and four-fold less polluted in the Wakool/Torrumbarry System in respect of sodium than Murray River water flowingto South Australia in 1979-80; if not, what are the figures for the relevant proportions.
Mr Anthony-The Minister for National Development and Energy has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(I) (i) Under normal flows, South Australia is entitled to 1,850,000 megalitres per annum in specified monthly quantities. Having provided South Au-stralia's entitlement, New South Wales and Victoria are entitled to an equal share of the flow of the River Murray at Albury, plus any contribution from their own tributaries below Albury.
(ii) During periods of very low flow and after a formal period of restriction has been declared by the River Murray Commission, special arrangements apply. An assessment is made of the water resources available to the Commission taking account of estimated evaporation and transmission losses. Allowance is made for a base flow of 58,000 megalitres per month to South Australia or such other quantity as may be determined by the Commission. The balance of water available is then divided equally between New South Wales, Victoria and South Australia. In these circumstances, New South Wales and Victoria also retain their entitlement to the additional amounts of flow contribu'ted by their respective tributaries below Albury.
(2) No; from information avail~ble in the River Murray Commission's 1980 Annual Report, the percentage was about 81 percent.
(3) Information contained in the 1980 Annual Report of the River Murray Commission shows that the median values for sodium concentrations during the year at the following locations were:
Yarrawonga-6 milligrams per litre Torrumbarry-14 milligrams per litre Euston-32 milligrams per litre Wentworth-51 milligrams per litre Tailem Bend-93 milligrams per litre
If the figures for Wentworth are taken as indicative of the sodium concentration in the water entering South Australia, the concentration of sodium in water diverted by New South Wales and Victoria from Yarrawonga Weir was about oneeighth of that at Wentworth and for the water diverted by Victoria at Torrumbarry the proportion was about one-quarter.

Australian Taxation System
(Question No. 2376)
Mr Hurford asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 2 June 1981:
(I) Has the Confederation of Australian Industry written to him expressing substantial misgivings about the Australian taxation system and calling for a Green Paper on tax.
(2) If so, was he surprised to read the comments of this industry body, in view of his statement to the Parliament on 12 March 1981 on taxation policy (Hansard pages 760-70).
(3) Has Australian industry been critical of the Government for not having made, after 5t years in power, a clearer and more definitive statement on taxation matters as they' affect industry; if so, is there justification for this criticism.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The Director-General of the Confederation of Australian Industry wrote to me recently, setting out, inter alia, the Confederation's views on the Australian taxation structure and suggesting that a Green Paper should be prepared to enable informed public discussion of taxation questions.
(2) and (3) Judgments about the tax system differ not only on points of emphasis and detail but also, and more importantly, concerning such weightier issues as the constraints which the effects of possible changes upon the budgetary aggregates must impose, and the relative importance attached to effects on particular sectors or on the structure of the taxation system as a whole.
It should not be surprising that different views on these matters are possible, given the different basic stances from which people start out. I do not think that the existence of such differences of view is open to the kind of black-and-white interpretation implied in the question.
In his letter, the Director-General reiterated the Confederation's request for urgent consideration of several substantial amendments of the income tax law. A point I wish to make in regard to those suggestions is that their immediate implementation would have consequences for the revenue on a scale that would put at risk the Government's budgetary strategy and jeopardise the economic progress achieved since 1976.
Economic Relations with New Zealand
(Question No. 2377)
Mr Burr asked the Minister for Trade and Resources, upon notice, on 2 June 1981:
(I) Are negotiations under way for closer economic relations with New Zealand; if so, will he provide details.
(2) Has any agreement' been reached that will allow a greater import of raw and processed vegetables from New Zealand.
(3) If so, will any protection be given to Australian producers to prevent undermining the domestic Australian market by heavily subsidised New Zealand products.
(4) Will any measures be taken to prevent a flooding of the Australian potato market.

Mr Anthony-The answer to the honourable member'& question is as follows:
(I) I refer the honourable member to the statement to the Parliament by the Prime Minister on 25 March 1980 in which he outlined proposals agreed with the Prime Minister of New Zealand for a possible closer economic relationship between Australia and New Zealand.
(2) and (3) No agreement has been reached on any aspect of the proposed closer economic relationship. In his statement the Prime Minister emphasised that "no commitment to any specific proposal has been entered into at this stage, nor will any decisions be taken until the studies have been completed and there has been full consboth countries". However the central trade objective of the proposed closer economic relationship is the gradual and progressive elimination of all barriers to trans-Tasman trade on all goods produced in either country.
(4) There are no imports of fresh potatoes from New Zealand due to quarantine restrictions and any concerns the industry may have can only relate to processed potato products, imports of which have been minimal in recent years and well below Australian shipments to New Zealand.
Implicit in the Prime Minister's statement of 25 March 1980 is the principle that trade between Australia and New Zealand within a closer economic relationship could only take place under conditions of fair competition. This means that any movement towards trade liberalisation would only occur on the basis of fair and equitable trading opportunities. The honourable member can be assured that the concerns expressed by Australian potato growers relating to the prospect of unfair New Zealand competition in potato products are being carefully assessed in the light of the central trade objective of the proposed closer economic relationship.

Apple and Pear Promotion
(Question No. 2389)
Mr Kerin asked the Minister for Primary Industry, upon notice, on 3 June 1981:
(I) Are wholesale fruit agents required to deduct a levy of 7c per bushel from growers' returns to be diverted to the Apple and Pear Corporation for promotion in Australia and overseas.
(2) If so, what regulations under the Australian Apple and Pear Corporation Act require this to be done.
(3) Are proceeds from the Apple and Pear Export Charge also diverted to promotion.
(4) What proportion of the total sum spent on promotion was spent (a) overseas and (b) in Australia from grower and government funds during (i) 1979 and(ii) 1980.
(5) What amount of grower funds earmarked for promotion and publicity in (a) 1979 and (b) 1980 was spent on general Corporation overheads.
(6) Of the amount of grower funds spent in (a) 1979 and (b) 1980 what sum was spent on promotion and publicity in (i) each State and (ii) each overseas country.
(7) Are any attempts made locally and overseas to monitor the effects of expenditure on promotion and publicity on consumption.
(8) Is detailed information available to growers and agents on local and overseas promotion campaigns.
(9) As apple and pear growing is regional, have there been any demands from the industry for promotion to emphasise district attributes, for example, the Huon Valley, Adelaide Hills, Bilpin, Orange, Stanthorpe and so on.
Mr Nixon-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) and (2) Sections 5 and 6 of the Apple and Pear Levy 'Act 1976 provide for a levy to be imposed on apples and pears produced and sold or used in Australia. The levy is payable by the grower. In respect of apples and pears which are not used for juicing or processing, sub-regulation 3(c) of the Apple and Pear Levy Regulations provides that the rate of levy shall be 5c per box. Sub-section 6 (2) of the Apple and Pear Levy Collection Act 1976 enables wholesale fruit agents to deduct the amount of the levy from the proceeds of the sal!: of the fruit they handle on behalf of the grower. Section 29 of the Australian Apple and Pear Corporation Act 1973 provil;!es that all moneys received under the Apple and Pear LevylA5l1ection Act 1976 and the Apple and Pear Export Charge Collection Act 1976 (see (3) below) shall be paid to the Corporation. Section 6 of the Australian Apple and Pear C;orporation Act 1973 provides that the promotion of apples and pears in Australia and overseas is one of the functions of

the Corporation. Finally, section 32 of the same Act enables the moneys of the Corporation to be applied in payment or discharge of the expenses, charges, obligations and liabilities incurred or undertaken by the Corporation in the performance of its functions under the Act.

(3) Yes, in the same manner as the payment of the levy.
Pursuant to sub-section 7(1) of the Apple and Pear Export Charge Act 1976 and Regulation 3 of the Apple and Pear Export Charge Regulations the rate of charge applied is 5
cents per box of fruit exported from Australia.

Australian Wool Testing Authority: Sale
(Question No. 2390)
Mr Kerin asked the Minister for Primary Industry, upon notice, on 9 June 1981:
(I) Have Hill-Samuel Australia Limited undertaken a study of the various options available to the Government with regard to the sale of the Australian Wool Testing Authority; if so, do the results of this study indicate that whatever option is adopted will result in an increase in charges to growers by between 10 per cent and 20 per ce\tt.
(2) How will he reconcile this finding with an undertaking given by the Prime Minister to Mr Ian McLachlan, President of the Wool Council of Australia, that charges would not increase.
(3) Did he state in a Primary Industry Media Release on 15 May 1981, that the fundamental aim is to ensure that the AWTA continues to operate in precisely the same way as it does today; if so, does this mean that the only result of the sale of the AWTA will be that growers will have to pay more for the same service.
(4) What will be the anticipated reduction in Government spending resulting from the sale of the AWTA to private enterprise.
(5) Will the Government make the Hill-Samuel Australia Limited study public so that all sections of the wool industry can examine its findings and consider the full implications of any change; if not, why not.
(6) What are the requirements of the International Wool Testing Organisations with regard to the operation of wool testing houses.
Mr Nixon-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The Australian Wool Corporation has inforrned me that Hill-Samuel Ltd undertook two feasibility studies during May 1981 at the request of the Australian Wool Corporation

to canvass possible options for the sale of AWTA to the private sector. I have been informed that Hill-Samuel forecast various levels of costs from no significant increase up to a 25 per cent increase but could not be definite because the levels of some critical parameters were not known at the time of those studi~. A third and more detailed study commissioned by the Wool Corporation is at present in train.
(2) The Prime Minister gave an undertaking to the Chairmen of the Wool Council of Australia and the International Wool Textile Organisation that the AWTA would not be sold until the Minister for Primary Industry is satisfied that the terms and conditions surrounding such a sale are satisfactory to the Industry. The Government has charged the Corporation to recommend suggested terms and conditions for the sale of AWTA and the preferred form of the commercial entity to continue its wool testing activities. No final recommendations have been formulated, and consequently the future cost structure cannot be accurately assessed at this time.
(3) The Government's decision to sell the AWTA is based on the belief that activities of a commercial kind are generally better performed in the private sector. AWTA in recent years ha.s been operating as a commercial entity.
(4) There should be no increase or decrease in Government spending as a result of the sale of the AWTA.
(5) The'Government did not commission the Hill-Samuel reports; in the circumstances outlined above, they are commercially confidenti~ to the Wool Corporation. However, in due course a synopsis of the reports, or at least their main thrust, will no doubt be communicated by the Corporation to industry representatives to permit the development of a consensus recommeridation on the sale arrangements for AWTA.
(6) In accordance with IWTO requirements the operations of a test house must be independent of tbe sales transactions in connection with which certificates will be issued or used.
Sydney Airport
(Question No. 2396)
Dr Klugman asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 3 June 1981:
What would be the expected lead time for the establishment of a new large airport in the Sydney area?
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
In the MANS Study it was forecast that it would take a minimum of 12 years to develop a second international airport to operational standard. The forecast lead time included provision for detailed engineering design, environmental analyses, land acquisition and construction.
On the other hand, QANTAS, with some support from other airlines, has indicated in a public submission to the MANS Committee that in its experience the actual lead time could well be closer to 20 years.
In addition, I should perhaps draw attention to the difficulties likely to be incurred in attracting traffic to a new major airport. These difficulties have been well documented in respect of a number of overseas cities.

Brisbane Airport: Curfew

(Question No. 2402)

Mrs Darling asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 3 June 1981:

(I) Further to his answer to Question No. 1191 (Hansard, 5 May 1981, page 2014) will a curfew in any form apply to runways on the redeveloped Brisbane Airport.

(2) How many take-off's and landings outside the conditions of the curfew were approved in (a) 1976-77, (b) 1977-78, (c) 1978-79, (d) 1979-80 and (e) the period I July 1980 to 2 June 1981.

(3) Will he consider imposing penalties for unapproved operations if explanations provided by the operator are unsatisfactory.

(4) For what reasons are breaches of the curfew approved.

Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

(I) No.

(2) There are four situations in which turbo jet aircraft may operate during the curfew, they are-

(i) Operations approved by me or a delegate appointed byrne;

(ii) Operations within the operational provisions of the curfew;

(iii) Operations by quiet aircraft approved by me or a delegate appointed by me;- .

(iv) Operations outside the_provisions of the curfew.

The following answers refer to operations in the situations described in (i) and (iv) above. Part 7 of the answer to Question 1191 (Hansard, 5 May 1981, page 2014) referred to the situation described in (iv) above.

(a) There are no records of flights for the period I July 1976 to 30 June 1977

(b) 10 flights are recorded for the period I July 1977 to 30 June 1978

(c) There were 34 flights for the period I July 1978 to 30 June 1979

(d) There were 23 flights for the period I July 1979 to 30

June 1980

.

(e) There were 28 flights for the period I July 1980 to 2 June 1981.

(3) If an occasion arises when an aircraft operator deliberately operates without approval (that is the situation

described in (2) (iv) above), I will consider imposing a penalty.
(4) Flights may be approved to operate (see 2 (i) above) in the following circumstances:
(i) when exceptional passenger hardship is involved
(ii) for humanitarian reasons
(iii) for delayed flights by visiting Heads of State.
Flights approved to operate in these circumstances do not breach the curfew. Flights that operate outside the provisions of the curfew (that is, without approval) breach the curfew.
Manufacture of Australian Coins
(Question No, 2410)
Mrs Kelly asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 3 June 1981:
(I) Have there been any proposals made or decisions taken to allow a Canadian firm to manufacture Australian coins; if so, what are the details.
(2) Do any plans exist for a new mint to be built at Mitchell, ACT; if so, what are the details.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) As a consequence of the interruption to the Mint's production of circulating currency due to the Australian Society of Engineers dispute, the Government has cOlitracted for the supply of 220 million pieces of Australian circulating currency (approximately 15 weeks' production of the Mint) from overseas sources. Information regarding those sources has not been released.
(2) A proposal to build a coin blanking annex at Mitchell, ACT, was considered some years ago as one option for a possible expansion of the facilities of the Royal Australian Mint. However the option was not pursued.
Grain Storages: Sri Lanka
(Question No. 2413)
Mr Fisher asked the Minister for Foreign Affairs, upon notice, on 3 June 1981:
(I) Did Australia supply 32 horizontal grain storages to Sri Lanka through the Australian Development Assistance Bureau in 1979; if so, have the storages been erected and completed under the terms of the agreement.
(2) Have the completed storages been inspected by Bureau officers.
(3) Who received the tender to construct the storages and what was the cost of the program.
(4) Are further storages to be supplied to the Paddy Marketing Board of Sri Lanka under similar arrangements.
Mr Street-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) In 1979 the Sri Lankan Government called public tenders in Australia for the supply of 32 prefabricated grain storage sheds and subsequently entered into a contract with the successful tenderer for their supply and shipment to Sri Lanka. Funds for the purchase of these sheds were made available by the Australian Government as part of its development assistance program to Sri Lanka. The agreement between the two governments related to the provision of funds. Erection and inspection of the sheds was the responsibility of the Sri Lankan Government.
(2) The storage sheds have not been inspected by Bureau officers. Consultations between the Australian and recipient

Answers to Questions

19 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 521

governments are held from time to time to ensure effective implementation of particular projects but it is neither feasible, and nor would it be appropriate, for Bureau officers to inspect all aspects of all projects.
(3) The successful tenderer was Osborne Metal Industries Pty Ltd, Osborne Park, Western Australia. The cost of the sheds including freight was $753,784.
(4) I am unaware of any further requirement for grain storage sheds to be provided as part of Australia's aid program to Sri Lanka.
Aluminium Pricing
(Question No. 2414)
Mr Howe asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 3 June 1981:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to claims by AIusuisse that it has provided subsidies to the Icelandic Aluminium Company (ISAL) and its Australian affiliates producing alumina at Gove, NT, and that these subsidies may explain the difference between the invoice price and the costs incurred bylSAL.
(2)' Is he able to say whether subsidies are being paid to affiliates of Alusuisse in Australia; if so, do the subsidies have the effect of reducing taxable income paid in Australia, for example, on the production of alumina at Gove.
(3) Will he provide the House with evidence that transnational corporations trading internationally in bauxite, alumina.and aluminium are trading on an 'arm's length' basis as opposed to engaging in transfer pricing practices.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) I understand that Alusuisse did release a statement on 18 December 1980 about the difference between prices appearing from statistics of Australian alumina exports to Iceland and the import price to the Icelandic Aluminium Company.
(2) and (3) I am not able to provide this information. The secrecy provisions of the income tax law of course prevent the Commissioner of Taxation from providing me with information he has obtained about the affairs of particular companies.
National Representative Basins Program
(Question No. 2415)
Mr Jacobi asked the Minister representing the Minister for National Development and Energy, upon notice, on 3 June 1981:
(I) Was the National Representative Basins Program established as part of Australia's contribution to the International Hydrological Decade.
(2) If so, what State and Commonwealth Departments were involved in the program.
(3) Did this program involve the hydrological investigation of approximately 100 medium to small river basins throughout Australia.
(4) What was the aim of the program and when was it commenced.
(5) Was it anticipated that if the program was successful the data collected from the catchments referred to in part (3) could be used to solve water problems arising with the many other catchments where either no data exists or cannot be quickly collected.

(6) Can the Minister say whether the Bureau of Meteorology consistently failed to undertake its commitment to rainfall collection data; if so, was this because the Bureau was never funded or manned at the appropriate level by the Commonwealth Government, in spite of repeated calls by the Australian Water Resources Council.
(7) Can the -Minister also say whether the CSIRO spent much effort in setting up a mathematical hydrological model of the catchment processes, which could not be effectively directed or improved because of the lack of sufficient coordinated rainfall and stream flow data.
(8) What is to be the fate of the program.
(9) What sum has been spent on the program and has this money been well spent.
Mr Antbony-The Minister for National Development and Energy has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(1) Although the Australian Representative Basins Program (ARBP) was not part of Australia's formal contribution to the program of the International Hydrological Decade, it was established in the spirit of the objectives of that international endeavourin 1965.
(2) The Department of National Development and Energy (as well as earlier departments with responsibility for the interests of the Commonwealth in water resource matters), CSIRO, the Bureau of Meteorology and all State government water agencies have been involved in the ARBP.
(3) Yes.
(4) The primary objective of the ARBP was to improve understanding of the relationships between catchment characteristcs, climate and run-off by studying data obtained from matching meteorological and hydrological installations in catchments representative, in the main, of all Australian environments.
(5) Yes, but mainly in respect of run-off management requirements including regulatory structures, culverts, bridges, etc.
(6) Within the general resources available to the Bureau of Meteorology, considerable progress was made in meteorological instrumentation early in the program. Twenty-nine of the eighty-five basins established have been fully instrumented with the minimum meteorological instruments, and fortyeight basins are partially instrumented (of which about half have sufficient data for analytical purposes). Most of the data in the ARBP data bank have been provided by the Bureau, involving approximately 1050 station years of average basin rainfall, 950 years of pluviograph data and 400 years of evaporation data. The remainder of the basins/sites require automatic equipment which has development, manpower and funding problems not yet fully resolved.
(7) CSIRO has devoted considerable resources to the development of the Australian Representative Basins model, partly using data obtained from the ARBP. Sufficient co-ordinated data has been available for the CSIRO research program to proceed, but not within the broader scope of the program as originally envisaged.
(8) The future of the ARBP is at present under consideration in the Australian Water Resources Council.
(9) A reliable estimate of the cost of the program to date cannot be provided owing to problems in identifying costs incremental to the normal expenditures of the numerous agencies involved. However, the success of the program will depend on the extcnt to which analyses of data from the selected gauged catchments meet the objectives of the program, as indicated in (4) and (5) above. It will be some time before this becomes clear.

Answers to Questions

Interference by Soviet Radar
(Question No. 2416)
Dr Everingham asked the Minister for Communications, upon notice, on 3 June 1981:
What steps has the Government taken to reduce interference by Soviet Union over-the-horizon radar to short wave bands vital for emergency communications in Australia.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
Those authorities responsible for safety communications in Australia have not observed any circumstances of significant interference on any of the internationally recognised radio distress channels. However, there has been some observation recently of over-the-horizon radar signals occurring on the Australian safety frequency 27.880 MHz, though the extent has not been serious. On the basis of these observations, my Department has initiated communication with the USSR seeking their co-operation in avoiding interference to the 27.880 MHz safety channel.
Control of Seabed Resources
(Question No. 2417)
Dr Everingham asked the Minister for Foreign Affairs, upon notice, on 3 June 1981:
(I) Has he recently switched from support of the Canadian and consensus position on the law of the sea to support the United States of America and multinational mining position which opposes controlled development of seabed resources.
(2) Is he able to say whether the USA legislated unilaterally to allow seabed mining.
(3) Is he also able to say whether the rule of law between nations will be disregarded to the disadvantage of developing nations if the USA policy is implemented.
Mr Street-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
( I) The Government's position on this matter has been explained to Parliament on several recent occasions.I refer the honourable member to my reply to a question without notice on 30 April 1981, to my statement to the House on 26 May 1981, to the answer to Senate Question No. 579 (Hansard, 27 May 1981, pages 2224-2225) and to my reply to a question without notice on 5 June 1981.
(2) The United States 'Deep Seabed Hard Minerals Act' (Public Law 96-283) entered into force on 28 June 1980. The Act is expressly interim in nature. It places a moratorium on commercial recovery of seabed minerals until I January 1988. It is the intention of the legislation that, if and when a Law of the Sea Convention enters into force with respect to the United States, that Convention will automatically supersede the Act.
(3) The United States Government is currently reviewing its policy on the Law of the Sea Conference. Accordingly, it is not possible to give any useful answer to the honourable member's question at this stage.

Nuclear Power in Australia
(Question No. 2433)
Mr Hayden asked the Minister representing the Minister for National Development and Energy, upon notice, on 4 June 1981:
What is the basis for the projection made by the Australian Atomic Energy Commission, published in Atomic Energy in Australia, July 1980, that by the year 2000, there will be 900 megawatts of nuclear power installed in Australia.
Mr Anthony-The Minister for National Development and Energy has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
The figure quoted represented the AAEC's 'best estimate' of future nuclear power capacity based on information publicly available at the time. The possible location of a reactor was not specified, and the projected capacity of 900 MWe took account of the typical size of nuclear reactors being sold on the international market.
In reaching its estimate the Commission noted a Western Australian Government publication, An Energy Policy for Western Australia, which stated that the WA Government had 'taken positive action in establishing firm plans for the introduction of nuclear generation in the 1990s . . .' (page 3) and that 'Planning and siting studies will be carried out so that Western Australia can proceed to the installation of a nuclear power station by 1995' (page II).
The Commission's projections also included a 'low estimate' of zero, to cover the possibility that no reactor might be installed in Australia by the year 2000, and a 'high estimate' of 1500 MWe in case reactors are installed by more than one State.

Mr Newman-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
No. The Commonwealth does not intend closing the Riverside Canteen.
Agricultural Research Stations, Victoria
(Question No. 2449)
Mr Milton asked the Minister for Primary Industry, upon notice, on 6 June 1981:

Infant Mortality
(Question No. 2460)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 4 June 1981:
(I) Has the Minister's attention been drawn to (a) the claims of the Infant Formula Action Coalition (INFACT) that more than one million infants die each year in Third World countries due, amongst other reasons, to the use of polluted water in the preparation of infant milk formulas and

(b) the fact that Australia voted in favour of a world-wide advertising ban on the marketing of infant milk formulas.

(2) Can the high rate of infant mortality amongst Australian Aboriginals be attributed to (a) contaminated water
supplies on Aboriginal reserves and the use of contaminated water in the preparation of infant formulas or (b) excessive
dilution of infant formula.

Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

(I) (a) Yes.

(b) Yes. Australia voted in favour of the draft International Code of Marketing of Breastmilk Substitutes which was adopted by the World Health Assembly, Geneva on 21 May
1981.

(2) (a) and (b) No. The high infant mortality rate among
Aboriginals as compared to the non-Aboriginal infant mortality rate is due to a variety of factors not least of which are
the adverse environmental and living conditions prevailing in
many Aboriginal communities which place infants at risk from respiratory, gastrointestinal and other communicable disease. To attribute this entirely to the use of contaminated
water or excessive dilution of infant formula, in the preparation of infant feeds, as has been reputed to occur in Third World countries, would be incorrect.

Sporadic incidents and occasional epidemics of gastrointestinal infections do still occur, but the presence of Aboriginal
health workers, (chosen by their communities), who are trained in preventive measures and capable of administering prompt and effective treatment, has resulted in a decline in
both mortality and morbidity. Breast feeding is also encouraged and where formula feeding is necessary, health workers can assist and train mothers in hygiene and other measures necessary in preparation of feeds.

The Government recognises that while there has been some improvement in Aboriginal health status in recent years, it is still unacceptably low and, among measures necessary to
achieve further improvement, priority is being given to the
provision of appropriate housing, clean and adequate water supplies and sanitation.

Foreign-Controlled Mining Enterprises
(Question No.2469)
Mr Uren asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 5 June 1981:
What proportion of total mining industry value added was attributable to foreign-controlled enterprises for each State in (a) 1978-79 and (b) 1979-80.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

Foreign Investment in Australia
(Question No. 2472)
Mr Uren asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 5 June 1981:
What has been the net effect on the balance of payments of foreign investment in enterprises in Australia in each year since 1970-71.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The inflow of foreign investment in enterprises in Australia, depending upon the uses to which it is put, can have several effects on the capital and current accounts of the balance of payments. In addition to its direct and immediate effect of providing Australia with financial resources from the rest of the world, the investments undertaken with foreign capital may reduce Australia's reliance upon imports or develop exports and also affect the current account through servicing payments.
While it is not possible to trace through all the effects of foreign investment inflows on the capital and current accounts of the balance of payments, it is clear that Australia

has been able to sustain a deficit on current account, and thus have command over a greater pool of goods and services than are able to be produced domestically, because of the contribution of foreign capital.

The relative importance of foreign investment in enterprises in Australia can be gauged from a comparison of
such inflow with an indicator of aggregate economic activity in Australia. The table below shows inflow of foreign invest-
ment in enterprises in Australia as a proportion of gross nonfarm product, for each year since 1970-71:

Mobile Homes
(Question No. 2477)
Mr Uren asked the Minister for Housing and Construction, upon notice, on 5 June 1981:
(I) What is the nature and extent of work being carried out within his Department in relation to the design and development of mobile homes or transportable homes.
(2) Which company or companies are involved in the design or development of a prototype mobile home or transportable home.
(3) What other Commonwealth and State authorities are involved, or have been involved, in consideration of proposals for the design, development, marketing, sale or rental of mobile homes aQdmobile homes' parks.
(4) Which locations have been considered for the placement of mobile homes and transportable homes.
(5) What measures are available for the transportation of mobile homes.
(6) What planning codes in each State and Territory would require amendment to allow for the sale or rental of mobile or transportable homes for dwelling purposes.
Mr McVeigh-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The Department of Housing and Construction has been undertaking work on a type of manufactured housing, commonly called mobile homes, which has been very successful in the USA.
The Commonwealth-State Ministerial Council on Housing Costs has been considering the potential of manufactured/ mobile homes to offer lower cost housing in Australia.
The Department undertook a review of mobile homes in Australia and overseas for the Council. It is assisting the Council to develop model guidelines for the construction and siting of manufactured homes. The construction guidelines are being dev'llloped within the principles implicit in the Australian Mddell,Jniform Building Code to maintain acceptable

health and safety standards without the emphasis on conventional site building techniques.

The Department is acquiring a prototype manufactured house based on North American design principles featuring a lightweight stressed skin construction box beam design on an integrated steel chassis with its own running gear and suitable for replacement on low cost foundations. Tbe prototype will help governments, industry, and consumers to assess the quality and potential of this type of dwelling.

(2) Neilsen Design Associates Pty Limited were engaged

by the Department to assist with the preparatory design work

for the prototype as the basis for tenders.

.

The contract to construct the prototype manufactured house has been let to Yarrawonga Housing Industries Pty
Limited.

(3) I am not aware of any other Commonwealth authority being involved in the design or development of mobile homes and mobile home parks.

State and Territory Governments are participating with the
Commonwealth in the Ministerial Council on Housing Costs in reviewing the potential of maf!ufactured housing and
developing model guidelines. The Council has informed the Planning Ministers Conference and the Local Government
Ministers Conference.

While I understand from meetings of the Council that some
States are interested in giving more detailed consideration to manufactured housing, I have no information on specific pro-
posals they may be considering.

The Age newspaper of 5 June 1981 reported that the Victorian Housing Minister was reviewing proposals for a pilot scheme in Victoria.

The New South Wales Parliamentary Inquiry into Caravan and Mobile Home Parks recommended that mobile homes be studied as a form of alternative housing and consideration be given to a pilot scheme of mobile homes in a mobile home park. I am informed that following the Inquiry a committee of officials on mobile homes was set up to pursue this recommendation.

(4) The Department of Housing and Construction is not involved in the placement of mobile homes other than the prototype manufactured house, for which consideration is being given to various locations for display in the eastern
States.

I have no specific information on locations being considered by the States for the placement of mobile homes.

Transportable houses have been used by Commonwealth authorities and others in various parts of Australia, especially rural and remote areas, over a long period and usually where
they are a more practical option to site building.

(5) Transportation of a mobile home in North America usually consists of running gear which is attached to the integrated steel chassis. The running gear can be subsequently

Answers to Questions

19 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 527

removed and re-used or leFt on the house. It is also possible to place the home on a low loader.
The Australian Transport Advisory Council and the National Association of State Roads Authorities are considering appropriate policies and regulation For the movement of manuFactured housing. It will be up to the market place to develop the most appropriate cost-effective means of transportation.
(6) I understand that the sale or rental of mobile or transportable homes For dwelling purposes is not generally subject to planning codes.

Future Dwelling Stock Requirements
(Question No. 2482)
Mr Uren asked the Minister for Housing and Construction, upon notice, on 5 June 1981:
On the basis of the projections contained in the Review of Australia's Demographic Trends prepared by the Department of Immigration and Ethnic Affairs in February 1981 and on the basfs of the most recent and reliable assumptions of the growth in household headship ratios, what are the (a) projected total dwelling stock requirements to the year 2001 and (b) average annual dwelling cOnlpletion requirements to the year 2001 for (i) each State and Territory and (ii) Australia as a whole.
Mr McVeigh-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(a) and (b) Projections of the type requested, especially over such a long period, are subject to considerable uncertainty at the national level and even greater uncertainty at the State and Territory level, where interstate migration is an important factor to be considered. Whilst it is possible to produce a single set of figures as requested, this would imply a degree of precision in Forecasting methodologies which does not pertain.

That is why a range of projections of annual additional dwelling requirements for each State were presented in Chapter 5 of the 1981 Report of the Indicative Planning Council for the Housing Industry which I tabled in the Parliament on 10 June 1981. The demographic projections on which the demand estimates in that Report are based' are generally consistent with the sets of assumptions suggested in the Review of Australia's Demographic Trends although this Review did not produce population estimates below the national level.
Projections of total dwelling stock requirements can be derived from these estimates, after allowing for replacement for stock losses, and the 1976 Census count of dwellings.
I therefore refer the honourable member for Reid to the 1981 Report of the Indicative Planning Council for the Housing Industry.
Service Personnel: Discharge for Use of Marijuana
(Question No. 2489)
Dr Klugman asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 5 June 1981:
(I) Were a number of Service personnel discharged, for the use of marijuana whilst not on duty, in the Wagga area, NSW,
(2) If so, were they prosecuted for a breach of the law and were they convicted.
(3) Are Service personnel who have been charged and convicted in connection with the use of alcohol discharged from the Services.
Mr Killen-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Twelve airmen and airwomen of RAAF Base Wagga were discharged during May 1981 as a result of their involvement in the non-medical use of drugs.
(2) Disciplinary action was not taken. However, the members involved were discharged from the RAAF according to prescribed administrative procedures.
(3) Each case is considered on its merits. Servicemen are not normally discharged as the result of a civilian or military conviction in connection with the use of alcohol.
Medical Benefits: Transcendental Meditation
(Question No. 2492)
Dr Klugman asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 5 June 1981:
(1) Are medical benefits paid for professional attendances consisting of exercises in Transcendental Meditation (TM).
(2) If so, on what basis did his Department decide that TM is a medical service.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) and (2) No. However, if transcendental meditation was prescribed as a relaxation technique by a medical practitioner during the course of a professional attendance, benefits would be payable for that attendance under the appropriate consultation item. In this situation the benefits are payable on the grounds that there is a physical attendance, by a legally qualified medical practitioner upon the patient.
The fact that benefits are payable for an attendance at which transcendental meditation is prescribed ,should not be construed as an indication of support for the practice. As medical benefits are attracted for consultations by legally qualified medical practitioners, it would be neither Plactical

528 REPRESENTATIVES 19 August 1981

Answers to Questions

nor desirable to attempt to restrict the way in which doctors choose to conduct their consultations~ I might also mention that a request for a specific Medical Benefits Schedule item for transcendental meditation was rejected.
New Zealand/Australia Free Trade Agreement
(Question No. 2495)
Dr Everingham asked the Minister for Primary Industry, upon notice, on 9 June 1981:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to a submission of 20 May 1981 by the Queensland Commercial Fishermen's Organis. ation to the Queensland Premier and others protesting at imports of sea perch fish and fillets from New Zealand produced on giant Soviet trawlers in the Tasman Sea built, owned and manned by Soviet citizens, with crews trained by their Government, using fuel exempt from OPEC price agreements and engaged in joint ventures with New Zealand partners, with a 20 per cent New Zealand export subsidy and no right to sell in New Zealand under New Zealand law.
(2) If so, has this product rivalled reef fish in appeal.
(3) Does this product cost wholesale, as alleged, approxi. mately $3. \0 per kilogram at the Queensland Fish Board compared to $4. \0 per kilogram for Queensland mackerel.
(4) Has the Government received a request for anti-dumping action under Article 10 of the New Zealand/Aus. tralia Free Trade Agreement; if not, will he consult urgently with the State authorities on this matter.
(5) Has there been months-long delays in providing the promised extension of Rural Reconstruction Funds to the fishing industry; if so, have the deliberations of the Committee of Review of Commonwealth Functions been responsible for the delays.
(6) Is he able to say whether the Queensland Fish Board has blamed, over-ridden and reduced payments to fishermen consistently as a means of attacking marketing problems despite long dissatisfaction of fishermen with the management with the Board's promotion, marketing efficiency, communication with them and buy-in prices.
(7) Will he assist fishermen planning regional co.operatives to overcome these problems and liaise with the Queensland Government to achieveindustrial democracy in industries protecting Australia from unfair competition from .nationalised big business which is free of the bureaucratic inefficiencies and restraints suffered by Queensland fishermen on.their fuel prices and orderly marketing.
(8) Will he (a) inquire whether the Soviet fishermen get better conditions and more prompt replies to their organisation's correspondence from their marketing managers thah the Queensland Commercial Fishermen's Organisation gets from'the Queensland Fish Board, or (b) sponsor cultural exchanges between the fishermen and marketing managers of Australia and those of the Soviet Union and other efficient fishing nations to prevent international tensions such as those which led to the ruinous cod war between Britain and Iceland.
Mr Nixon-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) I have been advised that the Queensland Commercial Fishermen's organisation has expressed concern about imported New Zealand fish although my attention has not been drawn to that particular submission.
(2) and (3) While prices of both types of fish vary, I understand that the imported sea perch does rival reef fish in appeal and is cheaper.
(4) The Government has not received any specific request for anti-dumping action on New Zealand sea perch, although

complaints have been made that imports of fish from New Zealand and elsewhere are causing difficulties for Australian fishermen. I have already been in touch with the Queensland Minister for Primary Industries concerning the matter.
(5) The question of structural adjustment for the fishing industry was discussed at a meeting on 26 June 1981 between Federal, State and Northern Territory Ministers responsible for the administration of the Rural Adjustment Scheme.
Ministers reached agreement on the framework of a scheme for the fishing industry but a decision to prqceed with its implementation was deferred pending resolution of some issues relating to funding.
The deliberations of the Committee of Review of Commonwealth Functions have not delayed consideration of this matter.
(6) and (7) These are matters which should be addressed to the Queensland Government.
(8) (a) and (b) No.
Opinion Polls: Publication before Elections
(Question No. 2499)
Mrs Darling asked the Prime Minister, upon notice, on 9 June 1981:
(I) Is he able to say whether the French Government disallows the publication of results of opinion polls for a number of days before the beginning of their Presidential elections.
(2) Is it a fact that some controversy surrounded the effects of opinion polls on the National election held in Australia on 18 October 1980.
(3) Did he express the view, during the course of the election campaign, that opinion could have a distorting influence on the outcome of the election.
(4) Will he consider adopting a similar policy to that adopted in France toward the publication of results of opinion polls prior to Australian elections.
Mr Malcolm Fraser-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) to (4) Any effect that opinion polls mayor may not have on the outcome of elections is a matter for debate.
The most important elements in determining the outcome of elections are the policies and performance of the parties involved.

Radar Equipment used by Air Traffic Contr~lIers
(Question No. 2605)
Mr Morris asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 10 June 1981:
(I) What was the year of installation of the principal radar equipment used by Air Traffic Controllers at (a) Sydney, (b) Melbourne, (c) Brisbane, (d) Adelaide, (e) Perth, (f) Hobart and (g) Darwin airports.
(2) What proposals are planned for updating the equipment at each of the airports referred to in part (1 ) and what is the estimated (a) cost and (b) installation date in each case.
Mr Hunt-The answers to the honourable member's questions are as follows:
(I) A radar system comprises one or more sensors, which supply radar information to display systems used by Air Traffic Controllers. Three types of sensors are used by the Department of Transport; Route Surveillance Radar (RSR), Terminal Area Radar (TAR) and Secondary Surveillance Radar (SSR). In addition to radar sensors installed at the airports listed, a remote installation at The Round Mountain (NSW) supplies radar data to Air Traffic Controllers at Sydney and Brisbane. Likewise, the installation at Canberra supplies radar data to Air Traffic Controllers at Canberra, Melbourne and Sydney.
The year of installation in each case is as follows:

(2) The research is a continuation of laboratory work that has been in progress for two and a half years. Basically the treatment involves the preparation of a synthetic compound which, when applied to spilled oil at the rate of approximately one part of compound to five parts of oil, solidifies the oil into rubber like lumps. These lumps are apparently clean to handle and may be physically lifted from the water surface.

It is expected that sea tests will be conducted during July

1981. The results of these tests will be passed onto my Depart-

ment by the United Kingdom Government authority

involved.

Conference System of Shipping
(Question No. 2606)
Mr Morris asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 10 June 1981:
What specific action is he proposing in respect of the liner restrictive practices of the Conference System of shipping used to transport Australia's general cargo overseas trade given the Government's stated policy of supporting competition and free enterprise.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member'squestion is as follows:
The framework for commercial negotiations under the shil>ping provisions of the Trade Practices Act 1974 is consistent with the Government's free enterprise policies. The Government recognises the benefits to Australian exporters which a well managed conference system can provide in terms of frequency, reliability and regularity of services. However, it also -welcomes the competitive element provided by alternative commercial services as long as they do not adversely affect. the adequacy of shipping services for Australian trading interests.
The proposals for changes to Australia's overseas cargo shipping legislation which were foreshadowed in the last Parliament are currently under examination in the light of f\lrther advice from industry.
Household Earnings: Disposition
(Question No. 2618)
Mr Barry Jones asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 10 June 1981:
What was the percentage of average household earnings devoted to (a) food and drink purchased (i) for domestic preparation and consumption and (it) in a hotel, restaurant, fast food outlet (i.e. ready for consumption), (b) motor vehicles in

the (i) purchase or leasing of the vehicles (capital cost) and (ii) running costs (fuel and maintenance), (c) housing in (i) purchase or rental costs and (ii) repair and maintenance, rates and taxes, (d) gambling, (e) tourism divided into (i) domestic and (ii) overseas tourism, (f) recreation and (g) electrical appliances such as television, washing machines, radios, etc., for each year from 1966 to 1981.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
Annual estimates of the percentage of average household earnings devoted to categories of personal consumption expenditure as requested in the honourable member's question are not available. The following tables provide estimates of the percentages of both household income (Table I) and household disposable income (Table 2) devoted to those categories of private final consumption that most closely correspond to the items requested. These estimates are based on national accounts data released in 'Quarterly Estimates of National Income and Expenditure, March Quarter 1981' (5206.0). Estimates for the year 1980-81 will not be available until about March 1982.
Separate details are not available for (a) (i) food and drink purchased for domestic preparation and consumption and (a) (ii) food and drink purchased in a hotel, restaurant, or fast food outlet. The national accounts item 'operation of motor vehicles' has been used for (b) (ii) motor vehicle running costs. Operation of motor vehicles includes some household expenditure (e.g. parking fees and insurance service charges) which are not generally regarded as running costs. Housing costs have been represented by gross housing rent which includes imputed rent for owner occupied dwellings. Item (c) (ii) repairs and maintenance, rates and taxes can be considered as an expenditure paid out of gross rent. In the case of tenanted dwellings such expenditures are paid directly by the landlord and, as for owner occupied dwellings, they are one of the deductions made from gross rent to derive gross operating surplus in respect of the ownership of dwellings. Estimates for expenditure on tourism are not available from the national accounts since this expenditure is included in a number of categories of private final consumption expenditure e.g. 'fares', 'meals and accommodation' (which is part of 'other services') and is not separately identified within each relevant category.

Statistical Wind Data
(Question No. 2622)
Mr Innes asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 10 June 1981:
( I) Do published International Civil Aviation Organisation (ICAO) standards require the use of statistical wind data within the 95 per cent probability when designing locator patterns if this wind data is available.
(2) Has statistical wind data from the nearest official bureau of meteorology weather measuring station been utilised when calculating the locator pattern boundary at Ripley.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable ~ember's question is as fO.llows:
(I) No. ICAO has not published Standards and Recommended Practices relating to the design of holding patterns. The ICAO procedures which have been published are statements of operation practices with respect to which a measure of international uniformity is desirable. It is important to note that, where statistical wind data are available, the published ICAO material recommends the use of maximum wind speed

within the 95 per cent probability calculated on an omnidirectional basis.

(2) No. Because statistical wind data are not always avail- .

able, the Australian practice is to !Ise an alternative, ICAO for'

mula for calculating wind velocities. This then allows standar-

dised holding patterns to be used in all cases and produces a

result which is less restrictive, in most cases, then the use of

statistical data.

-
