National Health Scheme Prescriptions
(Question No. 2646)
Dr Everingham asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 10 June 1981:
(I) Is the definition of an urgent prescription left to the discretion of the prescriber when determining to request dispensing by telephone or other means of National Health Scheme (NHS) items before receipt of written prescription by the dispenser.
(2) What categories of persons are (a) recognised and permitted and (b) forbidden to communicate the contents of such a prescription from prescriber to dispenser.

Answers to Questions

19 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 533

(3) If no third party arrangements are allowed in such cases will this mean the NHS dispensing fee will be increased to allow for two trips by pharmacists (a) to collect prescriptions from hospitals and clinics and (b) to deliver the items prescribed, instead of as frequently at present one trip where the prescription and item are checked by each party before being exchanged.
(4) When an agreement exists for one pharmacist to relieve another fOl: after-hours dispensing, what restrictions are there on the rights of the dispenser to collect the patient moiety from the pharmacist with whom the patient has a continuing account.
(5) If 5 litres of intravenous fluid are prescribed on NHS and only 21itres used by the patient, what must the hospital do with the remaining 3 litres.
(6) Will intravenous NHS items be placed on the same basis for hospitals and doctors as doctors' bag supplies so that no patient moiety is charged, at least where a responsible hospital officer acknowledges receipt of those items.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) and (2) Under the National Health legislation it is the prescriber, that is a medical practitioner or participating dental practitioner who determines whether or not a prescription is required to be dispensed urgently and who is required to communicate the prescription to the dispenser in order that the pharmaceutical benefit can be supplied before the prescription is surrendered to the dispenser.
(3) (a) and (b) The medical practitioner or participating dental practitioner who has comdispenser shall reduce the communicated prescription to writing within 24 hours of the communication and despatch the prescription to the dispenser.
Only one professional fee is payable for each supply of a pharmaceutical benefit. However, the approved chemist supplying the benefit is entitled to charge the patient the cost of delivery when supply is made other than at the premises in respect of which he is approved.
(4) It is the responsibility of the approved person supplying the pharmaceutical benefit from the particular premises in respect of which he is approved, to charge the patient contribution for each supply of a pharmaceutical benefit except where supply is made to eligible pensioners or sickness beneficiaries in which case no patient contribution is payable.
(5) Under the terms of the National Health legislation when a pharmaceutical benefit is supplied to the patient it becomes the property of that patient for whom it is prescribed. Section 103 (4) of the NaTIonal Health Act states:
'A person for whom a prescription for a pharmaceutical benefit is written or to whom a pharmaceutical benefit is supplied shall not use, dispose of or otherwise deal with the pharmaceutical benefit in a way other than that for which the prescription was written or the pharmaceutical benefit supplied.
(6) It is the function of the Pharmaceutical Benefits Advisory Committee, an independant statutory body, to make recommendations to me, as Minister for Health, on matters concerning the availability of items listed as pharmaceutical benefits. Such matters would include any recommendation that intravenous fluids be made available as benefits in a similar arrangement to that which applies to those items available as Emergency Drug (Doctor's.Bag) Supplies.
Australian Broadcasting Commission: Australian Labor Party Campaign
Mr Sinclair-On 9 April 1981 (Hansard, page 1519) Mr Ian Robinson asked me, as Minister for

Communications, the following question without notice:
Is it a fact that a senior reporter with the Australian Broadcasting Commission maintained a virtual open line contact with a senior Australian Labor Party campaign official throughout the 1980 Federal election campaign and that the Australian Labor Party used this link to try to apply pressure on ABC producers? It is further a fact that the Australian Labor Party was being given advance information about the content of ABC current affairs programs and that the Australian Labor Party through this contact attempted to influence the coverage given to the Government side? Were program producers threatened by the Australian Labor Party? Did they find it necessary to complain to the management? Finally, can the Minister inform the House whether at the end of the election campaign the reporter concerned was a guest at a 'thank you' party put on by the Australian Labor Party?
The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The General Manager of the ABC has advised me that it is normal practice, during an election campaign, for reporters to have daily contact with officers of all political parties. There is no evidence to suggest any untoward contact existed between an ABC reporter and an ALP official during the last Federal election campaign.
The General Manager further points out that had advance information about the content of ABC public affairs programs been given to such an official by a reporter, that information could not have been used to influence the content of the programs, professional responsibility Jor which rests not with the reporter but with an executive producer.
It is a fact that on one occasion during the election campaign an executive producer answered a persistently-ringing telephone while a reporter was otherwise engaged and spoke to a person who identified herself as being a press officer with the ALP. It is also true that this perJion spoke forcefully to the executive producer who asked if she was attempting to bring political pressure to bear. At that point the caller terminated the conversation and apologised.
The incident caused concern and was brought to the attention of a member of the ABC's senior management.
It is not unusual fQr reporters to attend parties given by all -contenders in an election. As the honourable member does not name a particular reporter whether or not this reporter attended an ALP party, and if so, in what capacity, is not known.

Telephone Calls by Staff: Department of Trade and Resources
(Question No. 1868)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Trade and Resources, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to a report in the West Australian of 18 July 1979, page 37, that London Metropoli~ tan Police reduced its telephone bill by $985,000 or about 20 per cent by use of a monitoring device which recorded length, time and destination of telephone calls and the extension from which the call was made.
(2) Are personal telephone calls, particularly trunk and STD calls, made by staff of his Department controlled; if so, in what way.
(3) Has any consideration been given to the introduction of monitoring devices for telephone calls in his Department; if so, what was the outcome of the consideration.
Mr Anthony-The answer to the. honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) No.
(2) Yes. Limited STD barring is in operation at the Department's Melbourne Regional Office. Departmental instructionsildvise staff that telephones are provided for official purposes. Personal trunk calls may be booked through the switchboard with the approval of supervisors and the cost of the call recovered from the officer.
(3) Yes. Wider use of access-barring has been considered but rejected as impracticable for a variety of reasons. The need for regular contact between the Department's Central and Regional Offices and with the export sector of the business community at all levels makes the provision of STD facilities to many officers necessary as an integral part of their duties. Selective access-barring is constrained by a combination of the open-planned nature of the Department's offices and the cost of barring individual telephone extensions.

Telephone Calls by Staff: Department of Home Affairs and Environment
(Question No. 1892)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for. Home Affairs and Environment, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to a report in the West Australian of 18 July 1979, page 37, that London Metropolitan Police reduced its telephone bill by $985,000, or about 20 per cent by use of a monitoring device which recorded length, time and destination of telephone calls and the extension from which the call was made.
(2) Are personal telephone calls, particularly trunk and STD calls, made by staff of his Department controlled; if so, in what way.
(3) Has any consideration been given to the introduction of monitoring devices for telephone calls in his Department; if so, what was the outcome of the consideration.
Mr Wilson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) All telephones in the Department of Home Affairs and Environment are STD barred except for those used by approved senior officers. Staff may make personal trunk calls only with the approval of their Branch Head. These calls are recorded by the switchboard operator who advises the officer concerned of the cost of the call. Payment is to be made for the call within 24 hours. The switchboard operator also provides a monthly list of personal trunk calls to the Finance Section and any outstanding accounts are pursued until payment is made.
(3) Consideration has been given to the installation of monitoring devices. However, as trunk and STD calls are controlled, the Department has considered that the estimated cost saving on local unofficial calls would not warrant the costs involved in the installation of this equipment.

Abolition of Rent Control in Australian Capital Territory
(Question No. 2108)
Mrs Kelly asked the Minister for the Capital Territory, upon notice, on 6 May 1981:
(I) How will the abolition of rent control in the Australian Capital Territory (Hansard, 30 April 1981, pages 1831 and 1842) (a) streamline Commonwealth operations and (b) establish the conditions under which there can be better care for those in need.
- (2) What sum will the Government save as a result of this action.
Mr Hodgman-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Compulsory Rent Control was abolished in the Australian Capital Territory on 16 December 1976 and was replaced by a voluntary fair renting scheme whereby lessors, owners or lessees could make application to the Rent Controller for a Fair Rent to be determined. However, very few' lessors/lessees have taken advantage of the provisions of this scheme, with only 23 determinations being made in the 12 months to 30 April 1981. This is despite the fact that it is estimated that there are currently at least 10,000 privately let residential premises in the Australian Capital Territory.
The Government's recent decision will do away with all forms of Rent Control in the Territory and thus reduce the cost of bureaucracy and leave rent levels subject to competitive forces.
Emergency housing for those in need is available.
(2) The potential administrative savings, based on the level. of activity over the past 2 years, would be in the order of $8,000 in a full year.

Post-graduate Engineering Students
(Question No. 2382)
Mr Barry Jones asked the Minister for Education, upon notice, on 2 June 1981:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to the assertion of Professor Lance Endersbee, Dean of Engineering at Monash University, Victoria, that only 20 per cent of post-graduate engineering students at Monash University are Australian residents.
, (2) Is there any substance to this claim; if so, can he say what is the major reason for the small numbers of Australian engineers proceeding with post-graduate studies.
(3) Does the Government endeavour to encourage more Australians to continue these studies in this field.
(4) Is the high drop-out rate in Australian post-graduate studies largely attributable to low levels of tertiary assistance compared to salaries offered by businesses to young graduates in some disciplines.
(5) If so, will the imposition of fees for second degrees exacerbate this tendency.
(6) Will he undertake to reconsider the future national implications of the reimposition of fees for second degrees.
Mr Fife-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.

(2) Information provided to the Universities Council orthe C0l!'mo~wealth Tertiary Education CommissipE by Monash Umverslty shows that, out of a total of 239 students enrolled for higher degree courses in the Engineering Faculty in 1980, approximately 50 were identified as being overseas students. Even allowing for some discrepancies arising from various definitions of overseas students, it would appear that a substantial majority of students undertaking post-graduate engineering courses at Monash University are Australian residents.
(3) Under the Commonwealth Post Graduate award scheme it has been the policy since the scheme began not to earmark awards for particular fields of study. The scheme is seen to be one of general support across the whole range of higher degree studies.
(4) The stipend under Post Graduate Awards has always been significantly lower than the salaries available to graduates in employment. It is for the individual to assess whether the pursuit of higher degree studies under award conditions is preferable to the immediate financial advantages of employment. Acceptance of the award reflects the view that it is in the Award holder's long term interest to pursue a higher level of research and study at that stage rather than embark upon or continue in employment.
(5) and (6) A significant number of higher degree students, including full-time academic staff in tertiary institutions and holders of Commonwealth Government awards will be exempt from tuition fees. Higher degree students holding awards of $3500 per annum or more from State governments, universities and colleges of advanced education will also be exempt from tuition fees.
, The Government acknowledges the importance of tertiary education to the economy and to the community generally but it believes that there are substantial benefits which flow to individuals from tertiary education and the community as a whole should not be expected to meet all of the costs. The Government is proceeding with arrangements for the reintroduction of fees in 1982. It will monitor the experience in that year.
Tuition Fees: Universities and Colleges of Advanced Education
(Question No. 2441)
Mr Young asked the Minister for Education, upon notice, on 4 June 1981:
(I) Are tuition fees to be introduced in universities and colleges of advanced education for students undertaking degrees and diplomas subsequent to an initial tertiary qualification, except for recognised double degree or diploma combinations; if so, what is the definition of an initial tertiary qualification and will diplomas or associate diplomas gained in technical and further education institutions be classified as an initial tertiary qualification.
(2) What are recognised double degree or diploma combinations.
(3) Are fees to apply to new enrolments from 1982; if so, (a) what will be the definition of a new enrolment, (b) will students who have deferred their higher degree enrolment be liable for fees and (c) will only students cl~ssified by the institutions as cOntinuing students be eligible for exemption from the payment of fees.
(4) Will postgraduate students who receive Commonwealth postgraduate awards and university or college scholarships be exempt from the payment of fees; if so, will students who extend their enrolment beyond the length of their award become liable for the payment of fees.
(5) Will students on private scholarships be liable to pay fees.

540 REPRESENTATIVES 19 August 1981

Answers to Questions

(6) Will overseas students currently (a) exempted from the payment of the Overseas Student Charge under Statutory Rules No. 290, section 4, and (b) required to pay the Overseas Student Charge, be required to pay the new tuition fee.
(7) Will students who fail to complete their first qualification and enrol in another course be required to pay tuition fees for the second course; if so, for what proportion of the second course will they be required to pay fees.
(8) What will be the level of fees set and will it be different for universities and colleges of advanced education.
(9) Will a separate rate be levied for (a) part-time students and (b) external students; if so, what will be the levels in each case.
Mr Fife-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Tuition fees will be introduced for students undertaking degrees and diplomas subsequent to an initial qualification be it a degree or diploma or recognised combined course. Students will not be required to pay tuition fees if they are upgrading a diploma to a degree, or an associate diploma (or other equivalent course) to a diploma or degree in the same field as the initial qualification.
(2) [ will be announcing details of recognised combined courses as soon as possible..
(3) (a) The new arrangements for the payment of tuition fees will apply to students enrolling to commence second or higher qualifications in 1982. (b) Students who have deferred their higher degree enrolment will be liable for tuition fees if they have not commenced the higher degree course before 1982. (c) No. See answer to (a) above.
(4) Higher degree students in receipt of Commonwealth Government awards will be exempt from tuition fees as will higher degree students in receipt of awards of at least $3500 per annum from universities, colleges of advanced education and State Governments. Higher degree students who extend their enrolment beyond the length of these awards will be exempt from tuition fees for the remainder of the course.
(5) Yes.
(6) (a) and (b) No.
(7) No.
(8) An annual tuition fee of $\000 will apply fof full-time students in universities and colleges of advanced education.
(9) Tuition fees for internal and external part-time students will be calculated by individual tertiary instituti'ons on a pro rata basis.

Regulation and Control of Nuclear Activities
(Question No. 2505)
Mr Uren asked the Minister for Trade and Resources, upon notice, on 9 June 1981:
(I) Further to the Prime Minister's statement to the Parliament on 30 April 1981, as part of the Review of Commonwealth Functions, that responsibilities for the regulation and control of nuclear activities are to be implemented as far as possible by the States and Northern Territory, with the Commonwealth maintaining a co-ordinating role (Hansard, page 1837), which responsibilities for the regulation and control of nuclear activities within his portfolio will no longer be implemented by the Commonwealth.
(2) Have assurances been sought from the States that they will now implement these responsibilities; if so, what replies have been forthcoming.
(3) What arrangements exist or are proposed for the Cominonwealth to playa co-ordinating role.
Mr .Anthony-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) As Minister of State for Trade and Resources, I have certain responsiblities for the regulation and control of nuclear activities under the Atomic Energy Act as set out for my portfolio in the Administrative Arrangements Order. On 5 June 1981, in a joint statement with the Minister for National Development and Energy, I foreshadowed far reaching changes to Commonwealth legislation on nuclear matters which would affect, in particular, the Atomic Energy Act. In line with the Government's decision on the Review of Commonwealth Functions, responsibility for the regulation and control of nuclear activities in Australia will be implemented as far as possible by the States and the Northern Territory with the Commonwealth maintaining a co-ordinating role. Any changes which might occur in the responsibilities within my own portfolio could depend, to an extent, on the outcome of the discussions and negotiations with the States and the Northern Territory on control and regulation of nuclear matters.
(2) and (3) The Government in co-operation with the States and the Northern Territory will seek to establish a revised legal basis to cover nuclear activities through appropriate State legislation, complemented as necessary by Commonwealth legislation, taking account of the respective

Answers to Questions

19 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 541

competences of the Commonwealth, the States and the Northern Territory. In the development of the new legislative base, Government officials have been asked to maintain close consultation with the States and the Northern Territory.

Tertiary Education Fees
Mr Fife-On 9 June 1981 (Hansard, page 3331) Mr Steele Hall asked me a question, without notice, concerning the scheme to impose tertiary education fees.
The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
On the basis of 1980 enrolments in universities and colleges of advanced education.it is estimated that the revenue in 1982 from the limited re-introduction of tuition fees will be in the order of $8m. It is my intention that the arrangements to be adopted for tuition fee collection will be such that costs should be minimal.
Communication Facilities in Tasmania
MrSinclair-On 26 May 1981 (Hansard, page 2520), Mr Groom asked me a question, without notice, concerning radio services on the west coast of Tasmania.
The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
Officers of my Department have advised me that a draft planning proposal relating to the commercial broadcasting service is nearing completion, a field survey for the national service has been completed and a technical report has been prepared for submission to the National Broadcasting Service

Planning Committee for evaluation prior to being brought before me for my decision. It should be borne in mind however that implementation of these proposals is dependent upon the availability oflimited financial and man-power resources.

Mineral Extraction, Processing and Export

-

Developments

(Question No. 1082)

Mr Morris asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 7 April 1981 :

(I) How many studies has he commissioned the Bureau of Transport Economics or his Department to undertake into the implications and requirements that expanded mineral extraction, processing and export developments will pose for the Australian transport sector.

(2) What priorities in terms of resources and personnel have been given to these studies.

Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

(I) and (2) The implications of resource developments for the Australian Transport sector are taken into account in the ongoing work of the Bureau of Transport Economics, the Department, the AustralianRailway Research and Development Organisation and in the intergovernmental consultative

fora of the Australian Transport Advisory Council and the Marine and Ports Council of Australia. Moreover, close liaison is maintained with both industry and other Government portfolios.
Recent examples of the work of the Bureau of Transport Economics include a Study of Western Australian Ports, and the Economic Evaluation of Proposed Completion of the Sandy Hollow-Maryvale Railway.
The Study of Western Australian Ports was carried out jointly by the Office of the Director General of Transport in Western Australia and the Bureau of Transport Economics and included consideration of the needs of the expanding min- .eral industry. The Sandy Hollow-Maryvale Railway study was concerned with the development of the Ulan coal seam and the carriage of grain and metalliferous concentrates.
At the instigation of the Marine and Ports Council of Australia the Bureau of Transport Economics is to undertake a study of the extension of the standard gauge rail links to the ports of Brisbane,-Melbourne and Geelong. As a matter of course this will comprehend forecast impacts of resource developments projects.
At a more general level my Department will be providing a .submission to the Senate Standing Committee on Trade and Commerce Inquiry into the Australian export coal industry.

546 REPRESENTATIVES 19 August 1981

Answers to Questions

Furthermore, the Bureau of Transport Economics is preparing a paper on the transport implications of resource developments fo~ the Transport Outlook Conference later this year.
The honourable member may be interested to know that the jointly funded State/Commonwealth body Australian Railway Research and Development Organisation is expected to release its 1981 Report on Rail later this year. The report will articulate a basis for rail's future role and is expected to outline future rail infrastructure investment requirements.
Within the Australian Transport Advisory Council, the Railway Group is giving consideration to the question of rail infrastructure requirements. It is expected that this work will be further progressed on completion of the ARRDO 1981 Report. This will of course have direct relevance to the infrastructure requirements of the resources boom.

Nuclear Power Plants
(Question No. 1204)
Dr Everingham asked the Minister representing the Minister for National Development and Energy, upon notice, on 8 April 1981:
(I) Can the Minister say how many deaths resulted from breakdowns and accidents in (a) nuclear power plants in (i) Britain, (ii) the United States of America, (iii) the Union of Soviet Socialist Republics and (iv) Switzerland between 1947 and 1970, and (b) alternative energy industries producing the same proportion of the electrical supply for the same countries.
(2) Can he also say what compensation was paid by governments or companies to farmers and others for damage done by unplanned nuclear contamination from nuclear re_actors in the countries referred to in part (I).
(3) Can he also say whether the Price-Anderson Act limits the United States of America government and corporate liability to $560 million for each nuclear generator despite the 'likelihood that a melt-down accident would cause billions of dollars of property damage and casualties comparable to the Hiroshima bombing.
(4) Is it a fact that insurance firms offered cover for nuclear accidents with premiums comparable to those for other power plants in the United States.
(5) Is it also a fact that short-term employees in nuclear plants are used to limit radiation doses to 30 to 100 times recommended civilian dose tolerances of gamma rays without monitoring of alpha and beta radiation.
(6) Can he say whether the short-term employees referred to in part (5) are free to seek return employment at other reactors in the United States without checking previous radiation exposure.
(7) Can he also say whether recommended limits of exposure for civilians are roughly twice background radiation levels and believed to cause double the incidence of malignant diseases attributable to or contributed to by background radiation.

Mr Anthony-The Minister for National Development and Energy has provided the following answers to the honourable member's questions:

Answers to Questions

19 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES ?47

(I) (a) and (b) The Government does not maintain comprehensive statistics regarding deaths from breakdowns and accidents in overseas power generating plants. However, there have been no fatal radiation accidents at commercial nuclear power plants in Britain, the United States of America or Switzerland, and I am not aware of any fatal radiation accidents at USSR nuclear power plants. Comparative studies of electricity generation from various energy sources indicate that, in terms of occupational and public health risk, and taking into account all stages of the relevant fuel cycles, nuclear powered plants are considerably safer than coal or oil fired plants.
(2) The only nuclear reactor accident in Britain during the period 1947 to 1970 resulting in radioactive contamination of property was the Windscale accident in 1957. The United Kingdom Atomic Energy Authority, in accepting responsibility for the accident, paid claims amounting to Stg 70,000 mainly for financial losses incurred by the Milk Marketing Board and local farmers as a result of radioactive iodine contamination. I am not aware of any accident to a nuclear reac-. tor in the USA, USSR or Switzerland during the period in question resulting in contamination of farms or other property.
(3) The Price-Anderson Act currently limits the aggregate liability of licensees of nuclear plants in the United States for a single nuclear incident to $US560 million.
(4) In the United States nuclear plants must be insured in accordance with the Price-Anderson Act. Rates and policies have been arrived at under special arrangements with the insurance industry, and are not directly comparable with premiums applying to non-nuclear power plants.
(5) The International Commission on Radiological Protection (ICRP) recommends that radiation workers, whether short term or long term, should receive no more than 50 millisieverts in a year and that members of the public should receive no more than 5 millisieverts in a year for short term (a few years) exposure and no more than an average of I millisievert a year when averaged over a lifetime. These limits do not include doses received from background radiation or medical procedures. The 1977 report of the United Nations Scientific Committee on the Effects of Atomic Radiation (UNSCEAR) shows the average doses received by radiation workers in United States nuclear power plants to be 10 millisieverts per year, and in Britain 2-5 millisieverts per year. Almost all radiation experienced in nuclear power plants is gamma radiation, associated with a small proportion of beta radiation. The proportions of gamma and beta radiation are measured by film dosimetry. The only time alpha radiation would be present is in the handling of ruptured fuel, and if suspected is monitored separately.
(6) Short term employees seeking employment elsewhere in the industry in the United States are required to disclose their previous radiation exposure levels.
(7) Exposure to the maximum long term ICRP recommended dose of I millisievert in addition to the average natural background radiation dose, also of I millisievert, could be expected to double the mortality risk attributable to background radiation alone. The combined risk, however, would only amount to 1.4 per cent of that from cancer from all causes.
(8) (a) In most countries with large nuclear industries, licensees maintain cumulative records relating to employees' radiation exposures. Some countries, e.g. Canada, have established national registries for this purpose. Studies of the incidence of cancers arising in radiation workers have been undertaken in several countries, for example, USA and UK. In Australia, records of radiation exposures are kept by centres operating personal monitoring services. The Australian Radiation Laboratory of the Department of Health is remodelling its personal monitoring service to provide for determination of

working lifetime doses. This service covers over half of the radiation workers in Australia.
(b) Licensees are required to instruct all individuals, working in or frequenting any portion of a restricted area, on the health protection problems associated with exposure to radioactive materials or radiation.
(9) There have been conRicting reports of the cause and magnitude of the claimed USSR disaster. No information has been released by Soviet authorities. The honourable member's attention is also drawn to answers to Questions Nos. 1633 and 1658 answered in the Senate on 25 February 1977 (pages 508 and 509).
(10) The Attorney-General introduced the Freedom ofInformation Bill in the Senate on 2 April 1981.
(II) (a) Currently not all power generating authorities include decommissioning costs in their rates. In the United States typical quotes for allocations to cover decomissionings are around $2.5-3.5 million per year which translates to about 2-4 per cent of current generation costs. Similar percentages have been estimated recently for the Federal Republic of Germany.
(b) and (c) Generally the cost of guarding and disposing of the wastes arising from the nuclear generation of electricity using current disposal methods are included in the rates charged by the generating authorities. This is a small fraction of the total generating cost, e.g. the cost of ultimate disposal of all waste arising from the light water reactor fuel cycle was estimated by an International Nuclear Fuel Cycle Evaluation (INFCE) study to be in the range of 0.10 to 0.16 cents per kilowatt hour (1978 US dollars). This would be about 3-5 per cent of the total generating cost for a reactor coming on line in the 1977-78 period.
(e) Regulatory requirements for the nuclear industry ensure that radioactive contamination of humans and other species does not exceed limits established by public health and environment protection authorities. The costs associated with 'meeting these regulatory requirements are included in the costs of nuclear generated electricity.
(12) A recent analysis of nuclear generation costs for 1979 prepared by the US Department of Energy, including data from 61 operating nuclear power stations (all rated over 400 MWe), indicated a unit cost of the order of 2.1 cents US/kWH: at current rates of exchange, this is equivalent to \.9 cents Aust./kWH. The price of electricity to Australian smelters is publicly available in only two cases. Both of these involve prospective smelters, rather than existing smelters for which prices are likely to reflect long standng contracts. For the Alcoa (Portland, Vic) smelter, the net tariff for electricity (including an interruptibility discount and a rebate of the SECY turnover tax) is just over \,7 cents/kWH (as at early 1981). At the Tomago (NSW) smelter, the price (including an interruptibility allowance) is 1.825 cents/kWh (as at early 1981).

Defence Force Reserve Personnel

552 REPRESENTATIVES 19 August 1981

Answers to Questions

Mr Killen-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) On 30 June 1981, [ announced that the terms of the new scheme to provide additional compensation for Defence Force reservists had been settled. After six months compensable incapacity, reservists will receive 76.5 per cent of their average civilian weekly earnings, up to a maximum of $500 per week. [f the reservist is a student or is unemployed at the time of injury, the 76.5 per cent will be based on military salary.
(2) For the first six months of incapacity, reservists, like other Commonwealth employees, receive full pay under the existing compensation legislation.
(3) The additional post 26 weeks payments will be made in accordance with an amendment to the Defence Act. Legislation to achieve this will be introduced to Parliament shortly.
Imports of Artillery Ammunition
(Question No. 1918)
Mr Scholes asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 28 April 1981:
(I) [s it a fact that ammunition for Australian 5.5in artillery is being imported from Pakistan.
(2) Are Australian stocks of 5.5in ammunition sufficient to enable sustained action should the Army be called upon to perform a military task involving medium artillery.
(3) Does capacity exist for manufacture of 5.5in ammunition in Australia; if so, is that capacity being used.
(4) What quantity of ammunition has been imported into Australia.
Mr Killen-} have already replied to the honourable member's question as follows:
(I) No. (2) No. Due to age and condition of 5.5in medium gun no longer has an operational role. The remaining serviceable weapons are used for training and stocks of ammunition are adequate for that purpose. (3) Yes. Both the projectiles and propellant can be manufactured in Australia but because use has been limited it has not been economic to manufacture projectiles in Australia for many years. Propellant is currently being manufactured in Australia. (4) The last import was approximately 14,000 projectiles from the United Kingdom in 1970.
The following additional information is supplied in relation to paragraph 2 of the questions:
It should be noted however that should a contingency arise which requires the use of this weapon, a capability could be made available. Subject to lead times, additional stocks of am~ munition can be procured. The introduction of the MI98 howitzer due into service in 1984 will replace this weapon.

Sale of Post Offices
(Question No. 2288)
Mr Campbell asked the Minister for Communications, upon notice, on 14 May 1981:
Are there any plans to (a) close down or (b) hand over or sell to the private sector, any post offices in Western Australia; if so, which post offices will be affected and when is it anticipated that this will be done,
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(a) Australia Post continually reviews its network of post offices to ensure that the facilities and services provided best meet the reasonable needs of the community, having regard to economy in operation, Under this program, post offices in all States may be identified for closure, where the level of business transacted is not sufficent to justify retention. It is Australia Post's objective to ensure that reasonable alternative arrangements for postal services are made for communities affected by a closure.
[n Western Australia, the following offices are scheduled for closure, because the level of postal business transacted at each office cannot justify their continued operation. Australia Post considers that alternative arrangements for postal services in each area affected will meet the reasonable needs of residents concerned.

Name of office
Attadale Koongamia Meltham Nedlands West Viveash

Mahogany Creek Joondanna Highgate Wembley (Grantham Street)

28 February 1982
31 March 1982 30 April 1982 31 May 1982 30 June 1982

All relevant Federal and State Members of Parliament and local representative bodies have been advised of the proposed closures.
(b) Australia Post has no plans to hand over or to sell any post offices to the private sector. It is Australia Post policy, however, that small official post offices may be converted to the non-official method of operation, when the volumes of postal business transacted are no longer sufficient to justify continuation under official conditions. Under non-official conditions, Australia Post arranges for the conduct of post offices on an agency type basis, but it still retains control of these offices. No offices in Western Australia have been identified, at this stage, for conversion to non-official conditions.

Concessions to Pensioners for Funeral Expenses
(Question No. 1336)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister, representing the Minister for Social Security, upon notice, on 27 May 1981:
(I) Is a pensioner whose Income falls below the tax-free threshold and who meets the funeral expenses of a deceased next of kin and former taxpayer, entitled to any Ananclal concession in respect of the funeral expenses.
(2) What Anancial concession is made available to a (a) tux paying pensioner and (b) non-taxpaying pensioner in respect of funeral expenses.
Mr Hunt~The Minister for Social Security has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(I) and (2) A pensioner who is a taxpayer may be entitled to funeral beneAt and/or (2) taxation concessions in respect of the funeral expenses of a deceased person.

Under the provisions of the Social Services Act a funeral benefit of up to $40 is payable to any age, invalid or widow pensioner or supporting parent beneficiary whose annual income apart from pension or benefit is less than $2,040 (single) or $1,768 (married person) and who is liable for the funeral costs of either an age, invalid or widow pensioner or supporting parent beneficiary whose income also falls within those limits, or a spouse, or a child in his care. Where the pensioner or beneficiary has dependants, the income limits are higher than those mentioned.
A funeral benefit of up to $20 is payable to any person who is not entitled to the $40 benefit but who is liable for the funeral costs of an age or invalid pensioner whose income fell within the limits stated above.
I am informed that the current income tax lay provides that amounts paid by a taxpayer for the funeral expenses of deceased dependants, not exceeding $100 in respect of each deceased dependant, shall be treated as reba table amounts.
For this purpose a dependant is defined to include the spouse of a taxpayer, a child of the taxpayer less than 21 years of age, or a person towards whose maintenance the taxpayer contributes during the year of income and is thereby entitled to a concessional rebate in respect of that person. This latter group would include an invalid relative, as that term is defined in the Income Tax Assessment Act, and the parents or parents-in-law of the taxpayer.
Unless the total of the rebatable amounts exceeds $1,590 a rebate of tax is not allowable. Where the total of the rebatable amounts exceeds $1,590 the rebexcess of the rebate amount over $1,590. Other reba table amounts include medical, hospital and chemist expenses, education expenses, rates and land taxes and life assurance and superannuation contributions.
A pensioner who is not a taxpayer may qualify for funeral benefit as detailed above but would not be eligible for taxation concessions.

Government Housing, Canberra
(Question No. 2411)
Mrs Kelly asked the Minister for Defence the following question, upon notice, on 3 June 1981:
(1) Are 20 Government houses vacant in Miller Street, O'Connor, Australian Capital Territory.
(2) If so, how long have these houses been vacant.
(3) Will these houses be made available to the general Government housing pool to alleviate the long delays of up to 18 months currently experienced by the 1,889 persons on the waiting list; if not why not.
Mr Killen-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) There are 14 Defence houses vacant in Miller Street, O'Connor, Australian Capital Territory.
(2) Since 1978, they have been vacant for periods ranging from seven to 40 months.
(3) Most of the Defence houses in O'Connor have been vacant because their general standard has fallen below that approved for Service personnel. In view of civilian housing needs in the area, a study was undertaken to determine if these houses could be released or brought up to Defence standards.
As the houses are structurally sound and well located for use by Defence Force personnel, it was decided that they should be upgraded. A program of full internal upgrading and other improvements has been authorised. On completion of these works later this year, the houses will be occupied by Service families.
Reviews of Defence Science and Technology Organisation
(Question No. 2496)
Mr Scholes asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 9 June 1981:
(1) Did he commission external and internal reviews of the Defence Science and Technology (DST) Organisation for the Government approximately 3 months ago; if so, has he received the results of the reviews.
(2) Did he undertake to table the reviews and the Government's response to them for discussion by the ParliameI:lt and interested parties.
(3) Did his Department inform staff organisations on 8 May 1981 that the reviews would I)e tabled before the end of the autumn period of sittings.
. (4) Do the reviews comment very favourably on the efficiency and detailed research being undertaken by the DST Organisation and that this view is contrary to the Government's overriding intention to cut defence staff and research facilities without consideration for the long term defence needs of Australia; if so, is this the reason for the Government's refusal to table the reviews.
Mr Killen--'-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) I announced the Reviews referred to by the honourable member on 13 November 1979. They were conducted in 1980 and the Reports were received in November 1980.

Answers to Questions

19 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 555

(2) It was my intention to table the Reports during the Autumn period of sittings. However some of the recommendations of the Reports come within the terms of reference of the recently announced Defence Review, The Government decided therefore to refer the Reports to the Defence Review. That body has now identified a number of recommendations it wishes to consider. Rather than delay action on the remaining recommendations I have therefore released the reports and the Government's response to those recommendations not being considered by the Defence Review, I tabled the reports on 19 August 1981.
(3) My Department did not indicate the Reports would be tabled but stated that it was expected that this would be the case. Subsequently the Government decided to refer the Report to the Defence Review.
(4) The reviews make a number of comments on the efficiency and detailed research being undertaken by DSTO. Some are favourable, some critical. They identified deficiencies in the organisation, management administration and operations of the DSTO which the Government is anxious to see remedied. The honourable member can be assured that the Government has no intention of taking any action with regard to the DSTO without the long term defence needs of Australia well in mind. It is in this spirit that we have responded to the recommendations of both reviews.
Social Security: Disclosure ofInformation
(Question No. 2502)
. Mrs Darling asked the Minister, representing the Minister for Social Security, upon notice, on 9 June 1981:
(I) Does the Social Security Act prevent the disclosure of information such as the address of a social security beneficiary to the beneficiary's parents, next of kin, or State or Federal police even in those cases where the beneficiary is between 16 and 18 years old and listed as a missing person.
(2) If so, will the Minister consider raising the age limit whereby information concerning the whereabouts of social security beneficiaries is given to the beneficiaries' parents or legal guardians.
Mr Hunt-The Minister for Social Security has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(I) The Social Services Act prohibits the disclosure of any information with respect to the affairs of another person which has been acquired in the performance of duties or functions under the Act. These provisions apply regardless of the age of the person concerned.
Information may, however, be disclosed to third parties where the person to whom the information relates has expressly or impliedly authorised such disclosure or where the Minister for Social Security or the Director-General of Social Services certifies that it is in the public interest to disclose the information.
(2) The existing confidentiality provisions of the Act are necessary to protect the privacy of clients of the Department, while permitting access to information in situations mentioned above.
Where a person or authority is anxious to get in touch with a client of the Department of Social Security, the Department has a procedure under which it will forward on correspondence to the client or will advise him of the name and address of the person who wishes to contact him.

Estimates of Total Transportation Costs
(Question No. 2600)
Mr Morris asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 10 June 1981:
What are the most recent estimates that can be provided of the total transportation costs, from origin to overseas port, disaggregated by (a) mode, (b) service, (c) stage of production and (d) cost and as a percentage of the total cost of the product before unloading at an overseas port for (i) coal, (ii) bauxite, (iii) alumina, (iv) aluminium ingots, (v) iron ore, (vi) bulk grain, (vii) containerised wool and (viii) general containerised cargo in (A) full container loads and (B) less than container loads.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The question is a complex and wide-ranging one, requiring details of transport costs at each stage of production for a range of unspecified origins and destinations in respect of eight commodities. Against the background that much of the information sought is confidential to the organisations concerned, I do not believe that the employment of staff resources to undertake the considerable research needed to provide a detailed answer would be justified.
As the honourable member would be aware, most bulk commodities are transported on both land and sea at contract rates, details of which are not released by the organisations concerned. Consequently, the 'book' rates published by the various Australian rail systems do not reflect the actual rates charged for these traffics. Likewise, in respect of shipping the spot rates published in trade journals do not provide an adequate guide to rates applicable to long-term trade arrangements made between exporters and shipping companies.
In addition, it should be noted that for some commodities, especially iron ore, mining companies or customers provide their own transport for the rail or sea freight task.
Import ofhory Products
(Question No. 2614)
Mr Beazley asked the Minister for Trade and Resources the following question, upon notice, on 10 June 1981:
(I) Do any restrictions apply to the import of ivory products into Australia; if so, what are the details?
(2) Has the Government raised in any international organisation of which Australia is a member, the question of restrictions on the slaughter of elephants; if so, to what effect?
Mr Anthony-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Restrictions do apply to the import of ivory products into Australia. The restrictions are contained in the Customs (Endangered Species) Regulations which give effect to the Convention on International Trade in Endangered Species of Wild Fauna and Flora. The Convention, which took effect in Australia on 27 October, 1976 provides, inter alia, for regulation of the international trade in elephant ivory.
(2) At the third meeting of the Conference of the Parties to the Convention in New Delhi (February/March 1981)- the Australian delegation assisted in drafting a resolution, subsequently adopted by the Conference, which recommended in summary:
(i) that any imports, exports or re-exports of African elephant ivory by a Party be authorised only if the Party is satisfied that the ivory was legally acquired in the country of origin.

556 REPRESENTATIVES 19 August 1981
(ii) that permits or certificates for raw ivory be accepted only if they mention the actual country of origin.
(iii) that Parties do not accept raw ivory which is not clearly marked.

Answers to Questions
(iv) that where possible Parties adopt domestic measures whereby importers, exporters or re-exporters of raw ivory would be subject to a licence to conduct such trade, unless such trade is only conducted by the Party itself.
